# 1.2.1 The Ellipse


> [!review]
> 1. What distinguishes the major axis of an ellipse from the minor axis, and what are the points at the endpoints of each axis called?
> 2. How is the center of an ellipse related to its axes of symmetry, and what geometric relationship do the two axes have to each other at the center?
> 3. On which of the two axes of symmetry do the foci of an ellipse lie, and what relationship holds between the sum of the distances from the foci to any point on the ellipse and the distance between the foci themselves?



##### problem 1

The major axis of an ellipse is the longer of the two axes of symmetry, whereas the minor axis is the shorter of the two axes of symmetry. The two endpoints of the major axis are called the vertices of the ellipse. The two endpoints of the minor axis are called the co-vertices of the ellipse.

##### problem 2

The center of an ellipse is the midpoint of both axes of symmetry. In other words, if one looks at the major axis and the minor axis, the same point lies exactly halfway along each of them, and that common midpoint is the center. At the center, the two axes intersect each other at right angles. Thus the major axis and the minor axis are perpendicular to each other at the center.

##### problem 3

The foci of an ellipse lie on the major axis, not on the minor axis. If $P$ is any point on the ellipse and the foci are denoted by $F_1$ and $F_2$, then the defining geometric property of the ellipse is that the sum of the distances from $P$ to the two foci is constant:

$$
PF_1 + PF_2 = \text{constant}.
$$

This constant sum is greater than the distance between the two foci themselves. Thus, for every point $P$ on the ellipse, the quantity $PF_1 + PF_2$ stays the same, and that fixed value is larger than $F_1F_2$.


+++++


Every ellipse has two axes of symmetry. The longer axis is called the major axis, and the shorter axis is called the minor axis. Each endpoint of the major axis is the vertex of the ellipse (plural: vertices), and each endpoint of the minor axis is a co-vertex of the ellipse. The center of an ellipse is the midpoint of both the major and minor axes. The axes are perpendicular at the center. The foci always lie on the major axis, and the sum of the distances from the foci to any point on the ellipse (the constant sum) is greater than the distance between the foci (Figure 12.1.4).




> [!figure]
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260411234749.png)
> 
> 

In this section, we restrict ellipses to those that are positioned vertically or horizontally in the coordinate plane. That is, the axes will either lie on or be parallel to the $x$ - and $y$-axes. Later in the chapter, we will see ellipses that are rotated in the coordinate plane.

To work with horizontal and vertical ellipses in the coordinate plane, we consider two cases: those that are centered at the origin and those that are centered at a point other than the origin. First we will learn to derive the equations of ellipses, and then we will learn how to write the equations of ellipses in standard form. Later we will use what we learn to draw the graphs.



## 12.1.1 Deriving the Equation of an Ellipse Centered at the Origin


> [!review]
> 1. Starting from the definition of an ellipse as the set of points for which the sum of the distances to two foci is constant, derive the standard equation of an ellipse centered at the origin with foci on the $x$-axis.
> 2. In the standard equation $\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$ of an ellipse centered at the origin, what does the relative size of $a$ and $b$ indicate about the ellipse's orientation?


##### problem 1

Let the foci be $(-c,0)$ and $(c,0)$, and let $(x,y)$ be any point on the ellipse. By definition of the ellipse, the sum of the distances from $(x,y)$ to the two foci is constant. If $(a,0)$ is a vertex, then that constant is

$$
(a+c)+(a-c)=2a.
$$

Therefore $(x,y)$ lies on the ellipse exactly when

$$
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}=2a.
$$

We now derive the standard equation. Move one radical to the other side:

$$
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}=2a-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
$$

Square both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x+c)^2+y^2
&=\left(2a-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right)^2\\
&=(2a)^2-2(2a)\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+\left(\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right)^2\\
&=4a^2-4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+(x-c)^2+y^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Subtract $(x-c)^2+y^2$ from both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x+c)^2+y^2-\bigl((x-c)^2+y^2\bigr)
&=4a^2-4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Simplify the left-hand side:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x+c)^2+y^2-\bigl((x-c)^2+y^2\bigr)
&=(x+c)^2-(x-c)^2\\
&=(x^2+2cx+c^2)-(x^2-2cx+c^2)\\
&=4cx.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
4cx=4a^2-4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
$$

Divide by $4$:

$$
cx=a^2-a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
$$

Rearrange:

$$
a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}=a^2-cx.
$$

Divide by $a$:

$$
\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}=a-\frac{cx}{a}.
$$

Square again:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x-c)^2+y^2
&=\left(a-\frac{cx}{a}\right)^2\\
&=a^2-2a\cdot \frac{cx}{a}+\left(\frac{cx}{a}\right)^2\\
&=a^2-2cx+\frac{c^2x^2}{a^2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Expand the left-hand side:

$$
x^2-2cx+c^2+y^2=a^2-2cx+\frac{c^2x^2}{a^2}.
$$

Add $2cx$ to both sides:

$$
x^2+c^2+y^2=a^2+\frac{c^2x^2}{a^2}.
$$

Move the $x^2$-term on the right to the left:

$$
x^2-\frac{c^2x^2}{a^2}+y^2=a^2-c^2.
$$

Factor $x^2$:

$$
x^2\left(1-\frac{c^2}{a^2}\right)+y^2=a^2-c^2.
$$

Combine the expression in parentheses:

$$
x^2\left(\frac{a^2-c^2}{a^2}\right)+y^2=a^2-c^2.
$$

Now let

$$
b^2=a^2-c^2.
$$

Then

$$
\frac{b^2}{a^2}x^2+y^2=b^2.
$$

Divide both sides by $b^2$:

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Thus the standard equation of an ellipse centered at the origin with foci on the $x$-axis is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

where

$$
b^2=a^2-c^2.
$$

##### problem 2

In the standard equation

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

the larger denominator corresponds to the direction of the major axis, because the ellipse extends farther in the variable whose squared term is divided by the larger number. Therefore, if $a>b$, then the larger denominator is under $x^2$, so the major axis is horizontal and the ellipse is oriented along the $x$-axis. If $b>a$, then the larger denominator is under $y^2$, so the major axis is vertical and the ellipse is oriented along the $y$-axis. In the case considered in problem 1, the foci lie on the $x$-axis, so the major axis is horizontal and hence $a>b$.


+++++


To derive the equation of an ellipse centered at the origin, we begin with the foci $(-c, 0)$ and $(c, 0)$. The ellipse is the set of all points $(x, y)$ such that the sum of the distances from $(x, y)$ to the foci is constant, as shown in Figure 12.1.5.




> [!figure]
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260411235228.png)
> 
> 



If ( $a, 0$ ) is a vertex of the ellipse, the distance from ( $-c, 0$ ) to ( $a, 0$ ) is $a-(-c)=a+c$. The distance from ( $c, 0$ ) to ( $a, 0$ ) is $a-c$. The sum of the distances from the foci to the vertex is

$$
(a+c)+(a-c)=2 a
$$


If ( $x, y$ ) is a point on the ellipse, then we can define the following variables:
- $d_1=$ the distance from $(-c, 0)$ to $(x, y)$
- $d_2=$ the distance from $(c, 0)$ to $(x, y)$

By the definition of an ellipse, $d_1+d_2$ is constant for any point ( $x, y$ ) on the ellipse. We know that the sum of these distances is $2 a$ for the vertex ( $a, 0$ ). It follows that $d_1+d_2=2 a$ for any point on the ellipse. We will begin the derivation by applying the distance formula. The rest of the derivation is algebraic.

$$
\begin{aligned}
d_1+d_2 & =2 a \\
\sqrt{(x-(-c))^2+(y-0)^2}+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+(y-0)^2} & =2 a \quad \text { Distance formula } \\
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} & =2 a \quad \text { Simplify expressions. } \\
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2} & =2 a-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Move radical to opposite side. } \\
(x+c)^2+y^2 & =\left[2 a-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right]^2 \quad \text { Square both sides. } \\
x^2+2 c x+c^2+y^2 & =4 a^2-4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+(x-c)^2+y^2 \quad \text { Expand the squares. } \\
x^2+2 c x+c^2+y^2 & =4 a^2-4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+x^2-2 c x+c^2+y^2 \quad \text { Expand remaining squares. } \\
2 c x & =4 a^2-4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}-2 c x \quad \text { Combine like terms. } \\
4 c x-4 a^2 & =-4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Isolate the radical. } \\
c x-a^2 & =-a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Divide by } 4 . \\
{\left[c x-a^2\right]^2 } & =a^2\left[\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right]^2 \quad \text { Square both sides. }
\end{aligned}
$$


$$\begin{aligned} c^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^4 & =a^2\left(x^2-2 c x+c^2+y^2\right) \quad \text { Expand the squares. } \\ c^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^4 & =a^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^2 c^2+a^2 y^2 \quad \text { Distribute } a^2 \\ a^2 x^2-c^2 x^2+a^2 y^2 & =a^4-a^2 c^2 \quad \text { Rewrite. } \\ x^2\left(a^2-c^2\right)+a^2 y^2 & =a^2\left(a^2-c^2\right) \quad \text { Factor common terms. } \\ x^2 b^2+a^2 y^2 & =a^2 b^2 \quad \text { Set } b^2=a^2-c^2 \\ \frac{x^2 b^2}{a^2 b^2}+\frac{a^2 y^2}{a^2 b^2} & =\frac{a^2 b^2}{a^2 b^2} \quad \text { Divide both sides by } a^2 b^2 \\ \frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2} & =1 \quad \text { Simplify }\end{aligned}$$


Thus, the standard equation of an ellipse is $\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$. This equation defines an ellipse centered at the origin. If $a>b$, the ellipse is stretched further in the horizontal direction, and if $b>a$, the ellipse is stretched further in the vertical direction.




## 12.1.2 Writing Equations of Ellipses Centered at the Origin in Standard Form


> [!review]
> 1. What are the two standard forms of the equation of an ellipse centered at the origin, and what distinguishes them?
> 2. Given an ellipse centered at the origin in standard form, how can one read off from the equation the lengths of the major and minor axes, the coordinates of the vertices and covertices, and the coordinates of the foci?
> 3. How are the parameters $a, b$, and $c$ related for an ellipse centered at the origin?


##### problem 1

There are two standard forms for the equation of an ellipse centered at the origin. The first is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

and this form describes an ellipse whose major axis lies on the $x$-axis. The second is

$$
\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1,
$$

and this form describes an ellipse whose major axis lies on the $y$-axis. In both cases one assumes that $a>b>0$. What distinguishes the two forms is the location of the larger denominator $a^2$. If $a^2$ is under $x^2$, then the ellipse is stretched farther horizontally, so the major axis is horizontal. If $a^2$ is under $y^2$, then the ellipse is stretched farther vertically, so the major axis is vertical.

##### problem 2

Once an ellipse centered at the origin is written in standard form, its main geometric features can be read directly from the equation.

If the equation is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

with $a>b$, then the major axis is horizontal. Its length is $2a$, so the vertices are the endpoints of that axis:

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

The minor axis has length $2b$, so the co-vertices are

$$
(0,\pm b).
$$

The foci lie on the major axis, so they have coordinates

$$
(\pm c,0),
$$

where $c$ is determined by the relation $c^2=a^2-b^2$.

If the equation is

$$
\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with $a>b$, then the major axis is vertical. Its length is again $2a$, so the vertices are

$$
(0,\pm a).
$$

The minor axis has length $2b$, so the co-vertices are

$$
(\pm b,0).
$$

The foci lie on the major axis, so in this case they are

$$
(0,\pm c),
$$

again with $c^2=a^2-b^2$.

Thus the rule is the same in both forms: the larger denominator determines the direction of the major axis, the corresponding endpoints give the vertices, the smaller denominator determines the minor axis and the co-vertices, and the foci lie on the major axis at distance $c$ from the center.

##### problem 3

For an ellipse centered at the origin, the parameters $a$, $b$, and $c$ satisfy

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Equivalently,

$$
a^2=b^2+c^2.
$$

Here $a$ is the semi-major axis, $b$ is the semi-minor axis, and $c$ is the distance from the center to either focus. Since $a>b>0$, this relation shows that $c<a$, which agrees with the geometric fact that each focus lies between the center and a vertex on the major axis.

+++++


Standard forms of equations tell us about key features of graphs. Take a moment to recall some of the standard forms of equations we've worked with in the past: linear, quadratic, cubic, exponential, logarithmic, and so on. By learning to interpret standard forms of equations, we are bridging the relationship between algebraic and geometric representations of mathematical phenomena.

The key features of the ellipse are its center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and lengths and positions of the major and minor axes. Just as with other equations, we can identify all of these features just by looking at the standard form of the equation. There are four variations of the standard form of the ellipse. These variations are categorized first by the location of the center (the origin or not the origin), and then by the position (horizontal or vertical). Each is presented along with a description of how the parts of the equation relate to the graph. Interpreting these parts allows us to form a mental picture of the ellipse.



The standard form of the equation of an ellipse with center $(0,0)$ and major axis on the $x$-axis is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

where
- $a>b$
- the length of the major axis is $2 a$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $( \pm a, 0)$
- the length of the minor axis is $2 b$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(0, \pm b)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $( \pm c, 0)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$. See Figure 12.1.6a.

The standard form of the equation of an ellipse with center $(0,0)$ and major axis on the $y$-axis is

$$
\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1
$$

where
- $a>b$
- the length of the major axis is $2 a$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $(0, \pm a)$
- the length of the minor axis is $2 b$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $( \pm b, 0)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm c)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$. See Figure 12.1.6b.

Note that the vertices, co-vertices, and foci are related by the equation $c^2=a^2-b^2$. When we are given the coordinates of the foci and vertices of an ellipse, we can use this relationship to find the equation of the ellipse in standard form.


> [!figure]
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260411235626.png)
> 
> 




> [!NOTE] How to: Given the vertices and foci of an ellipse centered at the origin, write its equation in standard form
> 
> 1. Determine whether the major axis lies on the $x$ - or $y$-axis.
> - If the given coordinates of the vertices and foci have the form $( \pm a, 0)$ and $( \pm c, 0)$ respectively, then the major axis is the $\boldsymbol{x}$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}$
> 
> $$
> +\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
> $$
> 
> - If the given coordinates of the vertices and foci have the form $(0, \pm a)$ and $( \pm c, 0)$, respectively, then the major axis is the $y$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{x^2}{b^2} +\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1$
> 2. Use the equation $c^2=a^2-b^2$, along with the given coordinates of the vertices and foci, to solve for $b^2$.
> 3. Substitute the values for $a^2$ and $b^2$ into the standard form of the equation determined in Step 1.





> [!exercise]
> 
>
>Plot the ellipse given by the equation, $\frac{x^2}{9}+\frac{y^2}{25}=1$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.


##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
\frac{x^2}{9}+\frac{y^2}{25}=1.
$$

To identify its features, we first compare the denominators. Since $25>9$, the larger denominator is under $y^2$, so the major axis is vertical. Therefore the equation is in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with

$$
b^2=9
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
a^2=25.
$$

Taking square roots gives

$$
b=3
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
a=5.
$$

Because the ellipse is centered at the origin, its center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

Since the major axis is vertical, the vertices lie on the $y$-axis. They are

$$
(0,\pm a)=(0,\pm 5).
$$

Thus the vertices are

$$
(0,5)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-5).
$$

The co-vertices lie on the minor axis, which is the $x$-axis. They are

$$
(\pm b,0)=(\pm 3,0).
$$

Thus the co-vertices are

$$
(3,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-3,0).
$$

To find the foci, we use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=25$ and $b^2=9$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=25-9\\
&=16.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
c=4.
$$

Because the major axis is vertical, the foci lie on the $y$-axis, so their coordinates are

$$
(0,\pm c)=(0,\pm 4).
$$

Thus the foci are

$$
(0,4)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-4).
$$

So the labeled features of the ellipse are: center $(0,0)$, vertices $(0,\pm 5)$, co-vertices $(\pm 3,0)$, and foci $(0,\pm 4)$.

To plot the ellipse, place these points on the coordinate plane and draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about both axes, passing through the vertices and co-vertices.



++++


First, we determine the position of the major axis. Because $25>9$, the major axis is on the $y$-axis. Therefore, the equation is in the form $\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1$,where $b^2 =9$ and $a^2=25$. It follows that:
- the center of the ellipse is $(0,0)$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $(0, \pm a)=(0, \pm \sqrt{25})=(0, \pm 5)$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $( \pm b, 0)=( \pm 9,0)=( \pm 3,0)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm c)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$ Solving for $c$, we have:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c & = \pm \sqrt{a^2-b^2} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{25-9} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{16} \\
& = \pm 4
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore, the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm 4)$. Next, we plot and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and draw a smooth curve to form the ellipse. See Figure 12.1.8.



> [!figure]
> 
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412000047.png)
> 
> 



++++



> [!exercise]
> Graph the ellipse given by the equation $\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{4}=1$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.


##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{4}=1.
$$

We compare the denominators. Since $36>4$, the larger denominator is under $x^2$, so the major axis is horizontal. Therefore the equation is in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

with

$$
a^2=36
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=4.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{36}\\
&=6
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{4}\\
&=2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the ellipse is centered at the origin, the center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

Since the major axis is horizontal, the vertices lie on the $x$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

Substitute $a=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm a,0)
&=(\pm 6,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(6,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-6,0).
$$

The co-vertices lie on the minor axis, which is the $y$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(0,\pm b).
$$

Substitute $b=2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm b)
&=(0,\pm 2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(0,2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-2).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=36$ and $b^2=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=36-4\\
&=32.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{32}\\
&=\sqrt{16\cdot 2}\\
&=\sqrt{16}\sqrt{2}\\
&=4\sqrt{2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is horizontal, the foci lie on the $x$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(\pm c,0).
$$

Substitute $c=4\sqrt{2}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm c,0)
&=(\pm 4\sqrt{2},0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(4\sqrt{2},0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-4\sqrt{2},0).
$$

To graph the ellipse, plot the center, the two vertices, the two co-vertices, and the two foci, then draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about both axes passing through the vertices and co-vertices.



![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423215005.png)



```mathematica title:""
ContourPlot[
  x^2/36 + y^2/4 == 1,
  {x, -7, 7}, {y, -3, 3},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{6, 0}, {-6, 0}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{0, 2}, {0, -2}}],
    Purple, Point[{{4 Sqrt[2], 0}, {-4 Sqrt[2], 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {0, 0}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(6,0)", {6, 0}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(-6,0)", {-6, 0}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(0,2)", {0, 2}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(0,-2)", {0, -2}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(4 Sqrt[2],0)", {4 Sqrt[2], 0}, {0, 1.5}],
    Text["(-4 Sqrt[2],0)", {-4 Sqrt[2], 0}, {0, 1.5}]
  }
]
```




```python title:""
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

t = np.linspace(0, 2 * np.pi, 400)
x = 6 * np.cos(t)
y = 2 * np.sin(t)

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(6, 0), (-6, 0)]
co_vertices = [(0, 2), (0, -2)]
foci = [(4 * np.sqrt(2), 0), (-4 * np.sqrt(2), 0)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{4}=1$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0, 0.18, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(6, 0.18, "(6,0)", ha="center")
ax.text(-6, 0.18, "(-6,0)", ha="center")
ax.text(0, 2, "(0,2)", ha="right", va="center")
ax.text(0, -2, "(0,-2)", ha="right", va="center")
ax.text(4 * np.sqrt(2), -0.18, r"$(4\sqrt{2},0)$", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(-4 * np.sqrt(2), -0.18, r"$(-4\sqrt{2},0)$", ha="center", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-7, 7)
ax.set_ylim(-3, 3)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```





++++




> [!exercise]
> Graph the ellipse given by the equation $4 x^2+25 y^2=100$. Rewrite the equation in standard form. Then identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.



##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
4x^2+25y^2=100.
$$

First we rewrite the equation in standard form by dividing both sides by $100$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4x^2+25y^2&=100\\
\frac{4x^2}{100}+\frac{25y^2}{100}&=\frac{100}{100}\\
\frac{x^2}{25}+\frac{y^2}{4}&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compare the denominators. Since $25>4$, the larger denominator is under $x^2$, so the major axis is horizontal. Therefore the ellipse is in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

with

$$
a^2=25
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=4.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{25}\\
&=5
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{4}\\
&=2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the ellipse is centered at the origin, the center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

Since the major axis is horizontal, the vertices lie on the $x$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

Substitute $a=5$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm a,0)
&=(\pm 5,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(5,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-5,0).
$$

The co-vertices lie on the minor axis, which is the $y$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(0,\pm b).
$$

Substitute $b=2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm b)
&=(0,\pm 2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(0,2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-2).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=25$ and $b^2=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=25-4\\
&=21.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{21}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is horizontal, the foci lie on the $x$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(\pm c,0).
$$

Substitute $c=\sqrt{21}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm c,0)
&=(\pm \sqrt{21},0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(\sqrt{21},0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-\sqrt{21},0).
$$

To graph the ellipse, plot the center, the two vertices, the two co-vertices, and the two foci, then draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about both axes passing through the vertices and co-vertices.




++++


First, use algebra to rewrite the equation in standard form.

$$
\begin{aligned}
4 x^2+25 y^2 & =100 \\
\frac{4 x^2}{100}+\frac{25 y^2}{100} & =\frac{100}{100} \\
\frac{x^2}{25}+\frac{y^2}{4} & =1
\end{aligned}
$$


Next, we determine the position of the major axis. Because $25>4$, the major axis is on the $x$-axis. Therefore, the equation is in the form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$, where $a^2 =25$ and $b^2=4$. It follows that:
- the center of the ellipse is $(0,0)$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $( \pm a, 0)=( \pm \sqrt{25}, 0)=( \pm 5,0)$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(0, \pm b)=(0, \pm \sqrt{4})=(0, \pm 2)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $( \pm c, 0)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$. Solving for $c$, we have:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c & = \pm \sqrt{a^2-b^2} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{25-4} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{21}
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore the coordinates of the foci are $( \pm \sqrt{21}, 0)$.
Next, we plot and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and draw a smooth curve to form the ellipse.


> [!figure]
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412000347.png)
> 





++++


> [!exercise]
> Plot the ellipse given by the equation $49 x^2+16 y^2=784$. Rewrite the equation in standard form. Then identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.


##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
49x^2+16y^2=784.
$$

First rewrite the equation in standard form by dividing both sides by $784$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
49x^2+16y^2&=784\\
\frac{49x^2}{784}+\frac{16y^2}{784}&=\frac{784}{784}\\
\frac{x^2}{16}+\frac{y^2}{49}&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compare the denominators. Since $49>16$, the larger denominator is under $y^2$, so the major axis is vertical. Therefore the ellipse is in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with

$$
b^2=16
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
a^2=49.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{49}\\
&=7
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{16}\\
&=4.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the ellipse is centered at the origin, the center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

Since the major axis is vertical, the vertices lie on the $y$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(0,\pm a).
$$

Substitute $a=7$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm a)
&=(0,\pm 7).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(0,7)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-7).
$$

The co-vertices lie on the minor axis, which is the $x$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(\pm b,0).
$$

Substitute $b=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm b,0)
&=(\pm 4,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(4,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-4,0).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=49$ and $b^2=16$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=49-16\\
&=33.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{33}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is vertical, the foci lie on the $y$-axis. Their coordinates are

$$
(0,\pm c).
$$

Substitute $c=\sqrt{33}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm c)
&=(0,\pm \sqrt{33}).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(0,\sqrt{33})
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-\sqrt{33}).
$$

To graph the ellipse, plot the center, the two vertices, the two co-vertices, and the two foci, then draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about both axes passing through the vertices and co-vertices.


++++



## 12.1.3 Graphing Ellipses Not Centered at the Origin



> [!review]
> 1. What are the two standard forms of the equation of an ellipse centered at a point $(h, k)$ ?
> 2. Given an ellipse centered at ( $h, k$ ) in standard form, how are the coordinates of the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci obtained from the equation?
> 3. What is meant by the general form of the equation of an ellipse, and what procedure converts it to standard form?



##### problem 1

The two standard forms of the equation of an ellipse centered at a point $(h,k)$ are

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

and

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1,
$$

where $a>b>0$. The first form represents an ellipse whose major axis is parallel to the $x$-axis, because the larger denominator $a^2$ is under the $x$-term. The second form represents an ellipse whose major axis is parallel to the $y$-axis, because the larger denominator $a^2$ is under the $y$-term.

##### problem 2

If an ellipse is written in standard form, then its center is read directly from the shifts in the squared terms: the center is $(h,k)$.

If the equation is

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

with $a>b$, then the major axis is horizontal. In that case:

$$
\text{center}=(h,k),
$$

the vertices are obtained by moving $a$ units left and right from the center:

$$
(h\pm a,k),
$$

the co-vertices are obtained by moving $b$ units up and down from the center:

$$
(h,k\pm b),
$$

and the foci are obtained by moving $c$ units left and right from the center:

$$
(h\pm c,k).
$$

If the equation is

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with $a>b$, then the major axis is vertical. In that case:

$$
\text{center}=(h,k),
$$

the vertices are obtained by moving $a$ units up and down from the center:

$$
(h,k\pm a),
$$

the co-vertices are obtained by moving $b$ units left and right from the center:

$$
(h\pm b,k),
$$

and the foci are obtained by moving $c$ units up and down from the center:

$$
(h,k\pm c).
$$

In either form, the quantity $c$ is found from the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

##### problem 3

The general form of the equation of an ellipse is an equation in which the terms have not yet been arranged into one of the standard completed-square forms. In other words, it is an equation written in expanded algebraic form rather than in the form that displays the center and axis lengths immediately.

To convert the general form to standard form, one first groups together the $x$-terms and the $y$-terms. Then one factors the coefficients of the squared terms if necessary. After that, one completes the square in the $x$-expression and in the $y$-expression. Finally, one rewrites the equation so that the right-hand side is $1$. The result is one of the two standard forms above, from which the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and orientation of the ellipse can be read directly.


+++++


When an ellipse is not centered at the origin, we can still use the standard forms to find the key features of the graph. When the ellipse is centered at some point, $(h, k)$,we use the standard forms $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1, a>b$ for horizontal ellipses and $\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1, a>b$ for vertical ellipses. From these standard equations, we can easily determine the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and positions of the major and minor axes.




> [!NOTE] How to: Given the standard form of an equation for an ellipse centered at $(h, k)$, sketch the graph.
> 
> 1. Use the standard forms of the equations of an ellipse to determine the center, position of the major axis, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.
> - If the equation is in the form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$, where $a>b$, then
> 	- the center is $(h, k)$
> 	- the major axis is parallel to the $x$-axis
> 	- the coordinates of the vertices are ( $h \pm a, k$ )
> 	- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h, k \pm b)$
> 	- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h \pm c, k$ )
> -  If the equation is in the form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1$, where $a>b$, then
> 	- the center is $(h, k)$
> 	- the major axis is parallel to the $y$-axis
> 	- the coordinates of the vertices are $(h, k \pm a)$
> 	- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h \pm b, k)$
> 	- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h, k \pm c$ )
> 1. Solve for $c$ using the equation $c^2=a^2-b^2$.
> 2. Plot the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci in the coordinate plane, and draw a smooth curve to form the ellipse.




> [!exercise]
> Graph the ellipse given by the equation, $\frac{(x+2)^2}{4}+\frac{(y-5)^2}{9}=1$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.



##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
\frac{(x+2)^2}{4}+\frac{(y-5)^2}{9}=1.
$$

We first compare the denominators. Since $9>4$, the larger denominator is under the $y$-term, so the major axis is vertical. Therefore the equation is in the standard form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with

$$
h=-2,
\qquad
k=5,
\qquad
b^2=4,
\qquad
a^2=9.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{9}\\
&=3
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{4}\\
&=2.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the center is

$$
(h,k)=(-2,5).
$$

Since the major axis is vertical, the vertices have the form

$$
(h,k\pm a).
$$

Substitute $h=-2$, $k=5$, and $a=3$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm a)
&=(-2,5\pm 3).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(-2,5+3)&=(-2,8),\\
(-2,5-3)&=(-2,2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(-2,8)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-2,2).
$$

Since the minor axis is horizontal, the co-vertices have the form

$$
(h\pm b,k).
$$

Substitute $h=-2$, $b=2$, and $k=5$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm b,k)
&=(-2\pm 2,5).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(-2+2,5)&=(0,5),\\
(-2-2,5)&=(-4,5).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(0,5)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-4,5).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=9$ and $b^2=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=9-4\\
&=5.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{5}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is vertical, the foci have the form

$$
(h,k\pm c).
$$

Substitute $h=-2$, $k=5$, and $c=\sqrt{5}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm c)
&=(-2,5\pm \sqrt{5}).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute the two foci:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(-2,5+\sqrt{5}),\\
(-2,5-\sqrt{5}).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(-2,5+\sqrt{5})
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-2,5-\sqrt{5}).
$$

To graph the ellipse, plot the center, the two vertices, the two co-vertices, and the two foci, then draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about the lines $x=-2$ and $y=5$, passing through the vertices and co-vertices.

Mathematica code:

```mathematica title:""
ContourPlot[
  (x + 2)^2/4 + (y - 5)^2/9 == 1,
  {x, -6, 2}, {y, 1, 9},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{-2, 5}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-2, 8}, {-2, 2}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{0, 5}, {-4, 5}}],
    Purple, Point[{{-2, 5 + Sqrt[5]}, {-2, 5 - Sqrt[5]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {-2, 5}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(-2,8)", {-2, 8}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["(-2,2)", {-2, 2}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(0,5)", {0, 5}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(-4,5)", {-4, 5}, {-1.2, 0}],
    Text["(-2, 5 + Sqrt[5])", {-2, 5 + Sqrt[5]}, {1.8, 0}],
    Text["(-2, 5 - Sqrt[5])", {-2, 5 - Sqrt[5]}, {1.8, 0}]
  }
]
```

Python code:

```python title:""
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

t = np.linspace(0, 2 * np.pi, 400)
x = -2 + 2 * np.cos(t)
y = 5 + 3 * np.sin(t)

center = (-2, 5)
vertices = [(-2, 8), (-2, 2)]
co_vertices = [(0, 5), (-4, 5)]
foci = [(-2, 5 + np.sqrt(5)), (-2, 5 - np.sqrt(5))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$\frac{(x+2)^2}{4}+\frac{(y-5)^2}{9}=1$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-2, 5.2, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(-2, 8, "(-2,8)", ha="center", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, 2, "(-2,2)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(0, 5, "(0,5)", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-4, 5, "(-4,5)", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, 5 + np.sqrt(5), r"$(-2,\,5+\sqrt{5})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, 5 - np.sqrt(5), r"$(-2,\,5-\sqrt{5})$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(5, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(-2, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-6, 2)
ax.set_ylim(1, 9)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++


First, we determine the position of the major axis. Because $9>4$, the major axis is parallel to the $y$-axis. Therefore, the equation is in the form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2} +\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1$, where $b^2=4$ and $a^2=9$. It follows that:
- the center of the ellipse is $(h, k)=(-2,5)$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $(h, k \pm a)=(-2,5 \pm \sqrt{9})=(-2,5 \pm 3)$, or $(-2,2)$ and $(-2,8)$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h \pm b, k)=(-2 \pm \sqrt{4}, 5)=(-2 \pm 2,5)$, or $(-4,5)$ and $(0,5)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $(h, k \pm c)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$. Solving for $c$, we have:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c & = \pm \sqrt{a^2-b^2} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{9-4} \\
& = \pm \sqrt{5}
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore, the coordinates of the foci are $(-2,5-\sqrt{5})$ and $(-2,5+\sqrt{5})$.
Next, we plot and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and draw a smooth curve to form the ellipse.


++++



> [!figure]
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412000923.png)
> 
> 





> [!NOTE] How to: Given the general form of an equation for an ellipse centered at $(h, k)$, express the equation in standard form.
> 
> 1. Recognize that an ellipse described by an equation in the form $a x^2+b y^2+c x+d y+e=0$ is in general form.
> 2. Rearrange the equation by grouping terms that contain the same variable. Move the constant term to the opposite side of the equation.
> 3. Factor out the coefficients of the $x^2$ and $y^2$ terms in preparation for completing the square.
> 4. Complete the square for each variable to rewrite the equation in the form of the sum of multiples of two binomials squared set equal to a constant, $m_1(x-h)^2+m_2(y-k)^2=m_3$, where $m_1, m_2$, and $m_3$ are constants.
> 5. Divide both sides of the equation by the constant term to express the equation in standard form.



> [!exercise]
> Plot the ellipse given by the equation $4 x^2+9 y^2-40 x+36 y+100=0$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci.



##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
4x^2+9y^2-40x+36y+100=0.
$$

We first rewrite the equation in standard form. Group the $x$-terms and the $y$-terms, and move the constant term to the other side:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4x^2+9y^2-40x+36y+100&=0\\
(4x^2-40x)+(9y^2+36y)&=-100.
\end{aligned}
$$

Factor out the coefficients of the squared terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x^2-10x)+9(y^2+4y)&=-100.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now complete the square in each parenthesis. For $x^2-10x$, half of $-10$ is $-5$, and $(-5)^2=25$. For $y^2+4y$, half of $4$ is $2$, and $2^2=4$. Add these inside the parentheses and balance the equation on the right:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x^2-10x+25)+9(y^2+4y+4)
&=-100+4(25)+9(4)\\
&=-100+100+36\\
&=36.
\end{aligned}
$$

Rewrite the completed squares:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x-5)^2+9(y+2)^2&=36.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide both sides by $36$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{4(x-5)^2}{36}+\frac{9(y+2)^2}{36}&=\frac{36}{36}\\
\frac{(x-5)^2}{9}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{4}&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the standard form is

$$
\frac{(x-5)^2}{9}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{4}=1.
$$

Now compare the denominators. Since $9>4$, the larger denominator is under the $x$-term, so the major axis is horizontal. Therefore the ellipse is in the standard form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

with

$$
h=5,
\qquad
k=-2,
\qquad
a^2=9,
\qquad
b^2=4.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{9}\\
&=3
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{4}\\
&=2.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the center is

$$
(h,k)=(5,-2).
$$

Since the major axis is horizontal, the vertices have the form

$$
(h\pm a,k).
$$

Substitute $h=5$, $a=3$, and $k=-2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm a,k)
&=(5\pm 3,-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(5+3,-2)&=(8,-2),\\
(5-3,-2)&=(2,-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(8,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(2,-2).
$$

Since the minor axis is vertical, the co-vertices have the form

$$
(h,k\pm b).
$$

Substitute $h=5$, $k=-2$, and $b=2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm b)
&=(5,-2\pm 2).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(5,-2+2)&=(5,0),\\
(5,-2-2)&=(5,-4).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(5,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(5,-4).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=9$ and $b^2=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=9-4\\
&=5.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{5}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is horizontal, the foci have the form

$$
(h\pm c,k).
$$

Substitute $h=5$, $c=\sqrt{5}$, and $k=-2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm c,k)
&=(5\pm \sqrt{5},-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute the two foci:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(5+\sqrt{5},-2),\\
(5-\sqrt{5},-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(5+\sqrt{5},-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(5-\sqrt{5},-2).
$$

To graph the ellipse, plot the center, the two vertices, the two co-vertices, and the two foci, then draw a smooth closed curve symmetric about the lines $x=5$ and $y=-2$, passing through the vertices and co-vertices.

Mathematica code:


![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423223001.png)




```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (x - 5)^2/9 + (y + 2)^2/4 == 1,
  {x, 1, 9}, {y, -5, 1},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{5, -2}}],
    Blue, Point[{{8, -2}, {2, -2}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{5, 0}, {5, -4}}],
    Purple, Point[{{5 + Sqrt[5], -2}, {5 - Sqrt[5], -2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {5, -2}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(8,-2)", {8, -2}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(2,-2)", {2, -2}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(5,0)", {5, 0}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["(5,-4)", {5, -4}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(5 + Sqrt[5],-2)", {5 + Sqrt[5], -2}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["(5 - Sqrt[5],-2)", {5 - Sqrt[5], -2}, {0, 1.2}]
  }
]
```

Python code:

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

t = np.linspace(0, 2 * np.pi, 400)
x = 5 + 3 * np.cos(t)
y = -2 + 2 * np.sin(t)

center = (5, -2)
vertices = [(8, -2), (2, -2)]
co_vertices = [(5, 0), (5, -4)]
foci = [(5 + np.sqrt(5), -2), (5 - np.sqrt(5), -2)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$\frac{(x-5)^2}{9}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{4}=1$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(5, -1.8, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(8, -2, "(8,-2)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(2, -2, "(2,-2)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(5, 0, "(5,0)", ha="center", va="bottom")
ax.text(5, -4, "(5,-4)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(5 + np.sqrt(5), -2, r"$(5+\sqrt{5},-2)$", ha="center", va="bottom")
ax.text(5 - np.sqrt(5), -2, r"$(5-\sqrt{5},-2)$", ha="center", va="bottom")

ax.axhline(-2, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(5, color="gray", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(1, 9)
ax.set_ylim(-5, 1)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++



We must begin by rewriting the equation in standard form.

$$
4 x^2+9 y^2-40 x+36 y+100=0
$$


Group terms that contain the same variable, and move the constant to the opposite side of the equation.

$$
\left(4 x^2-40 x\right)+\left(9 y^2+36 y\right)=-100
$$


Factor out the coefficients of the squared terms.

$$
4\left(x^2-10 x\right)+9\left(y^2+4 y\right)=-100
$$


Complete the square twice. Remember to balance the equation by adding the same constants to each side.

$$
4\left(x^2-10 x+25\right)+9\left(y^2+4 y+4\right)=-100+100+36
$$


Rewrite as perfect squares.

$$
4(x-5)^2+9(y+2)^2=36
$$


Divide both sides by the constant term to place the equation in standard form.

$$
\frac{(x-5)^2}{9}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{4}=1
$$


Now that the equation is in standard form, we can determine the position of the major axis. Because $9>4$, the major axis is parallel to the $x$-axis. Therefore, the equation is in the form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$, where $a^2=9$ and $b^2=4$. It follows that:
- the center of the ellipse is $(h, k)=(5,-2)$
- the coordinates of the vertices are $(h \pm a, k)=(5 \pm \sqrt{9},-2)=(5 \pm 3,-2)$, or $(2,-2)$ and $(8,-2)$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h, k \pm b)=(5,-2 \pm \sqrt{4})=(5,-2 \pm 2)$, or $(5,-4)$ and $(5,0)$
- the coordinates of the foci are $(h \pm c, k)$, where $c^2=a^2-b^2$. Solving for $c$, we have:

Therefore, the coordinates of the foci are $(5-\sqrt{5},-2)$ and $(5+\sqrt{5},-2)$.
Next we plot and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and draw a smooth curve to form the ellipse as shown in Figure 12.1.14.



++++


> [!figure]
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412001404.png)
> 
> 




++++



> [!exercise]
> Express the equation of the ellipse given in standard form. Identify the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci of the ellipse.
> 
> $$
> 4 x^2+y^2-24 x+2 y+21=0
> $$



##### solution

We are given the ellipse

$$
4x^2+y^2-24x+2y+21=0.
$$

We first rewrite the equation in standard form by completing the square.

Group the $x$-terms and the $y$-terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4x^2+y^2-24x+2y+21&=0\\
(4x^2-24x)+(y^2+2y)+21&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Factor out the coefficient of the $x^2$-term:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x^2-6x)+(y^2+2y)+21&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now complete the square in each variable. For $x^2-6x$, half of $-6$ is $-3$, and $(-3)^2=9$. For $y^2+2y$, half of $2$ is $1$, and $1^2=1$. Add the corresponding terms inside the parentheses and balance the equation:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x^2-6x+9)+(y^2+2y+1)+21
&=4(9)+1\\
&=36+1\\
&=37.
\end{aligned}
$$

Rewrite the left-hand side as perfect squares:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x-3)^2+(y+1)^2+21&=37.
\end{aligned}
$$

Subtract $21$ from both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4(x-3)^2+(y+1)^2&=16.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide both sides by $16$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{4(x-3)^2}{16}+\frac{(y+1)^2}{16}&=\frac{16}{16}\\
\frac{(x-3)^2}{4}+\frac{(y+1)^2}{16}&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the standard form is

$$
\frac{(x-3)^2}{4}+\frac{(y+1)^2}{16}=1.
$$

Now identify the parameters. Since $16>4$, the larger denominator is under the $y$-term, so the major axis is vertical. Therefore the ellipse is in the standard form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1
$$

with

$$
h=3,
\qquad
k=-1,
\qquad
b^2=4,
\qquad
a^2=16.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{16}\\
&=4
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{4}\\
&=2.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the center is

$$
(h,k)=(3,-1).
$$

Since the major axis is vertical, the vertices have the form

$$
(h,k\pm a).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $k=-1$, and $a=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm a)
&=(3,-1\pm 4).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(3,-1+4)&=(3,3),\\
(3,-1-4)&=(3,-5).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(3,3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(3,-5).
$$

Since the minor axis is horizontal, the co-vertices have the form

$$
(h\pm b,k).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $b=2$, and $k=-1$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm b,k)
&=(3\pm 2,-1).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(3+2,-1)&=(5,-1),\\
(3-2,-1)&=(1,-1).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(5,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(1,-1).
$$

To find the foci, use the relation

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=16$ and $b^2=4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=16-4\\
&=12.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{12}\\
&=\sqrt{4\cdot 3}\\
&=\sqrt{4}\sqrt{3}\\
&=2\sqrt{3}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the major axis is vertical, the foci have the form

$$
(h,k\pm c).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $k=-1$, and $c=2\sqrt{3}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm c)
&=(3,-1\pm 2\sqrt{3}).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(3,-1+2\sqrt{3})
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(3,-1-2\sqrt{3}).
$$



++++


> [!table]
> 
>
>
> | Horizontal ellipse, center at origin | $\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1, a>b$ |
> | :--- | :--- |
> | Vertical ellipse, center at origin | $\frac{x^2}{b^2}+\frac{y^2}{a^2}=1, a>b$ |
> | Horizontal ellipse, center $(h, k)$ | $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1, a>b$ |
> | Vertical ellipse, center $(h, k)$ | $\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}=1, a>b$ |
> 
> 










# 1.3 Locating the Vertices and Foci of a Hyperbola



> [!review]
> 1. What is the definition of a hyperbola as a set of points in the plane, and how does this definition compare with that of an ellipse?
> 2. What are the transverse and conjugate axes of a hyperbola, and which points lie at the endpoints of each?
> 3. On which axis of a hyperbola do the foci lie, and how is the center of the hyperbola characterized in relation to its axes?
> 4. What are the asymptotes of a hyperbola, and how is the central rectangle used to sketch them?



##### problem 1

A hyperbola is the set of all points $(x,y)$ in the plane such that the difference of the distances from $(x,y)$ to two fixed points, called the foci, is a positive constant. This is closely analogous to the definition of an ellipse, but with one important change. For an ellipse, one takes the sum of the distances from a point on the curve to the two foci and requires that sum to be constant. For a hyperbola, one takes the difference of those two distances and requires that difference to be a positive constant.

##### problem 2

The transverse axis of a hyperbola is the line segment that passes through the center of the hyperbola and has the vertices as its endpoints. The conjugate axis is perpendicular to the transverse axis and has the co-vertices as its endpoints. Thus the vertices lie at the endpoints of the transverse axis, while the co-vertices lie at the endpoints of the conjugate axis.

##### problem 3

The foci of a hyperbola lie on the line that contains the transverse axis. In other words, the foci lie on the same axis as the vertices. The center of the hyperbola is the midpoint of both the transverse axis and the conjugate axis, so it is the point where those two axes intersect.

##### problem 4

The asymptotes of a hyperbola are the two lines through the center that the branches of the hyperbola approach as one moves farther and farther away from the center. They are not part of the hyperbola itself, but they describe the directions in which the branches extend.

The central rectangle is a useful graphing aid for sketching these asymptotes. It is centered at the center of the hyperbola, and its sides pass through the vertices and co-vertices. Once this rectangle is drawn, the asymptotes are obtained by drawing and extending its diagonals. Those diagonals give the asymptote lines that guide the shape of the hyperbola.




+++++



In analytic geometry, a hyperbola is a conic section formed by intersecting a right circular cone with a plane at an angle such that both halves of the cone are intersected. This intersection produces two separate unbounded curves that are mirror images of each other (Figure 12.2.2).



Like the ellipse, the hyperbola can also be defined as a set of points in the coordinate plane. A hyperbola is the set of all points ( $x, y$ ) in a plane such that the difference of the distances between ( $x, y$ ) and the foci is a positive constant.

Notice that the definition of a hyperbola is very similar to that of an ellipse. The distinction is that the hyperbola is defined in terms of the difference of two distances, whereas the ellipse is defined in terms of the sum of two distances.

As with the ellipse, every hyperbola has two axes of symmetry. The transverse axis is a line segment that passes through the center of the hyperbola and has vertices as its endpoints. The foci lie on the line that contains the transverse axis. The conjugate axis is perpendicular to the transverse axis and has the covertices as its endpoints. The center of a hyperbola is the midpoint of both the transverse and conjugate axes, where they intersect. Every hyperbola also has two asymptotes that pass through its center. As a hyperbola recedes from the center, its branches approach these asymptotes. The central rectangle of the hyperbola is centered at the origin with sides that pass through each vertex and co-vertex; it is a useful tool for graphing the hyperbola and its asymptotes. To sketch the asymptotes of the hyperbola, simply sketch and extend the diagonals of the central rectangle (Figure 12.2.3).


> [!figure]
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412002128.png)
> Key features of the hyperbola


In this section, we will limit our discussion to hyperbolas that are positioned vertically or horizontally in the coordinate plane; the axes will either lie on or be parallel to the $x$-and $y$-axes. We will consider two cases: those that are centered at the origin, and those that are centered at a point other than the origin.



## 1.2.2 Deriving the Equation of an Hyperbola Centered at the Origin



> [!review]
> 1. Starting from the definition of a hyperbola as the set of points for which the difference of the distances to two foci is a positive constant, derive the standard equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin with transverse axis on the $x$-axis.
> 2. What are the two standard forms of the equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin, and what distinguishes them?
> 3. Given a hyperbola centered at the origin in standard form, how can one identify the lengths of the transverse and conjugate axes, the coordinates of the vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and the equations of the asymptotes?
> 4. How are the parameters $a, b$, and $c$ related for a hyperbola centered at the origin, and how does this relationship compare with the analogous relationship for an ellipse?


##### problem 1

Let the foci be $(-c,0)$ and $(c,0)$, and let $(x,y)$ be any point on the hyperbola. If $(a,0)$ is a vertex, then the difference of the distances from $(a,0)$ to the two foci is

$$
(a+c)-(c-a)=2a.
$$

Therefore, by the definition of the hyperbola, every point $(x,y)$ on the hyperbola satisfies

$$
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}=2a.
$$

We now derive the standard equation. Move the second radical to the other side:

$$
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}=2a+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
$$

Square both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x+c)^2+y^2
&=\left(2a+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right)^2\\
&=(2a)^2+2(2a)\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+\left(\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right)^2\\
&=4a^2+4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+(x-c)^2+y^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Expand both squares:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+2cx+c^2+y^2
&=4a^2+4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+x^2-2cx+c^2+y^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Subtract $x^2+c^2+y^2$ from both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2cx
&=4a^2+4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}-2cx.
\end{aligned}
$$

Add $2cx$ to both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4cx
&=4a^2+4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Subtract $4a^2$ from both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4cx-4a^2
&=4a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $4$:

$$
cx-a^2=a\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}.
$$

Square again:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(cx-a^2)^2
&=a^2\left((x-c)^2+y^2\right).
\end{aligned}
$$

Expand both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2x^2-2a^2cx+a^4
&=a^2(x^2-2cx+c^2+y^2)\\
&=a^2x^2-2a^2cx+a^2c^2+a^2y^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Add $2a^2cx$ to both sides:

$$
c^2x^2+a^4=a^2x^2+a^2c^2+a^2y^2.
$$

Move the $a^2x^2$-term and the $a^2y^2$-term to the left, and move the $a^4$-term to the right:

$$
c^2x^2-a^2x^2-a^2y^2=a^2c^2-a^4.
$$

Factor:

$$
x^2(c^2-a^2)-a^2y^2=a^2(c^2-a^2).
$$

Now let

$$
b^2=c^2-a^2.
$$

Then

$$
b^2x^2-a^2y^2=a^2b^2.
$$

Now divide both sides by $a^2b^2$:

$$
\frac{b^2x^2}{a^2b^2}-\frac{a^2y^2}{a^2b^2}=\frac{a^2b^2}{a^2b^2}.
$$

So

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Thus the standard equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin with transverse axis on the $x$-axis is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

##### problem 2

The two standard forms of the equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin are

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

and

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

The first form represents a hyperbola whose transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis. The second form represents a hyperbola whose transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. What distinguishes them is which squared variable has the positive term. The variable with the positive term indicates the direction of the transverse axis.

##### problem 3

If the hyperbola is in the form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

then the transverse axis is horizontal. Its length is $2a$, so the vertices are

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

The conjugate axis has length $2b$, so the co-vertices are

$$
(0,\pm b).
$$

The foci lie on the transverse axis, so they are

$$
(\pm c,0),
$$

where $c^2=a^2+b^2$. The asymptotes are

$$
y=\pm \frac{b}{a}x.
$$

If the hyperbola is in the form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

then the transverse axis is vertical. Its length is again $2a$, so the vertices are

$$
(0,\pm a).
$$

The conjugate axis has length $2b$, so the co-vertices are

$$
(\pm b,0).
$$

The foci lie on the transverse axis, so they are

$$
(0,\pm c),
$$

where again $c^2=a^2+b^2$. The asymptotes are

$$
y=\pm \frac{a}{b}x.
$$

##### problem 4

For a hyperbola centered at the origin, the parameters $a$, $b$, and $c$ satisfy

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

This differs from the corresponding relation for an ellipse. For an ellipse, the relation is

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

So the hyperbola uses a plus sign, whereas the ellipse uses a minus sign. This reflects the geometric fact that for a hyperbola the foci lie farther from the center than the vertices, while for an ellipse the foci lie between the center and the vertices.


+++++


Let ( $-c, 0$ ) and ( $c, 0$ ) be the foci of a hyperbola centered at the origin. The hyperbola is the set of all points ( $x, y$ ) such that the difference of the distances from $(x, y)$ to the foci is constant. See Figure 12.2.4.



If ( $a, 0$ ) is a vertex of the hyperbola, the distance from ( $-c, 0$ ) to ( $a, 0$ ) is $a-(-c)=a+c$. The distance from ( $c, 0$ ) to ( $a, 0$ ) is $c-a$. The sum of the distances from the foci to the vertex is

$$
(a+c)-(c-a)=2 a
$$


If ( $x, y$ ) is a point on the hyperbola, we can define the following variables:

$$
\begin{aligned}
d_2 & =\text { the distance from }(-c, 0) \text { to }(x, y) \\
d_1 & =\text { the distance from }(c, 0) \text { to }(x, y)
\end{aligned}
$$


By definition of a hyperbola, $d_2-d_1$ is constant for any point ( $x, y$ ) on the hyperbola. We know that the difference of these distances is $2 a$ for the vertex ( $a, 0$ ). It follows that $d_2-d_1=2 a$ for any point on the hyperbola. As with the derivation of the equation of an ellipse, we will begin by applying the distance formula. The rest of the derivation is algebraic. Compare this derivation with the one from the previous section for ellipses.




$$
\begin{aligned}
d_2-d_1 & =2 a \\
\sqrt{(x-(-c))^2+(y-0)^2}-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+(y-0)^2} & =2 a \quad \text { Distance Formula } \\
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2}-\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} & =2 a \quad \text { Simplify expressions. } \\
\sqrt{(x+c)^2+y^2} & =2 a+\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Move radical to opposite side. } \\
(x+c)^2+y^2 & =\left(2 a+\sqrt{\left.(x-c)^2+y^2\right)} \quad\right. \text { Square both sides. } \\
x^2+2 c x+c^2+y^2 & =4 a^2+4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+(x-c)^2+y^2 \quad \text { Expand the squares. } \\
x^2+2 c x+c^2+y^2 & =4 a^2+4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}+x^2-2 c x+c^2+y^2 \quad \text { Expand remaining square. } \\
2 c x & =4 a^2+4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}-2 c x \quad \text { Combine like terms. } \\
4 c x-4 a^2 & =4 a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Isolate the radical. } \\
c x-a^2 & =a \sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2} \quad \text { Divide by } 4 . \\
\left(c x-a^2\right)^2 & =a^2\left[\sqrt{(x-c)^2+y^2}\right]^2 \quad \text { Square both sides. } \\
c^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^4 & =a^2\left(x^2-2 c x+c^2+y^2\right) \quad \text { Expand the squares. } \\
c^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^4 & =a^2 x^2-2 a^2 c x+a^2 c^2+a^2 y^2 \quad \text { Distribute } a^2 \\
a^4+c^2 x^2 & =a^2 x^2+a^2 c^2+a^2 y^2 \quad \text { Combine like terms. } \\
x^2\left(c^2-a^2 x^2-a^2 y^2-a^2 y^2\right. & =a^2 c^2-a^4 \quad \text { Rearrange terms. } \\
x^2 b^2-a^2 y^2 & =a^2 b^2 \quad \text { Set } b^2=c^2-a^2 \\
\frac{x^2 b^2}{a^2 b^2}-\frac{a^2 y^2}{a^2 b^2} & =\frac{a^2 b^2}{a^2 b^2} \quad \text { Divide both sides by } a^2 b^2 \\
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2} & =1 \quad \text { acommon terms. }
\end{aligned}
$$


This equation defines a hyperbola centered at the origin with vertices ( $\pm a, 0$ ) and co-vertices ( $0, \pm b$ ).


> [!NOTE]
> STANDARD FORMS OF THE EQUATION OF A HYPERBOLA WITH CENTER $(0,0)$
> The standard form of the equation of a hyperbola with center $(0,0)$ and transverse axis on the $x$-axis is
> 
> $$
> \frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
> $$
> 
> where
> - the length of the transverse axis is $2 a$
> - the coordinates of the vertices are ( $\pm a, 0$ )
> - the length of the conjugate axis is $2 b$
> - the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(0, \pm b)$
> - the distance between the foci is $2 c$, where $c^2=a^2+b^2$
> - the coordinates of the foci are ( $\pm c, 0$ )
> - the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{b}{a} x$
> 
> See Figure 12.2.5a.
> The standard form of the equation of a hyperbola with center $(0,0)$ and transverse axis on the $y$-axis is
> 
> $$
> \frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1
> $$
> 
> where
> - the length of the transverse axis is $2 a$
> - the coordinates of the vertices are $(0, \pm a)$
> - the length of the conjugate axis is $2 b$
> - the coordinates of the co-vertices are $( \pm b, 0)$
> - the distance between the foci is $2 c$, where $c^2=a^2+b^2$
> - the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm c)$
> - the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{a}{b} x$
> 
> 
> See Figure 12.2.5b.
> Note that the vertices, co-vertices, and foci are related by the equation $c^2=a^2+b^2$. When we are given the equation of a hyperbola, we can use this relationship to identify its vertices and foci.
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> > [!figure]
> > 
> > ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412002745.png)
> > 
> > 
> 
> 





> [!NOTE]
> How to: Given the equation of a hyperbola in standard form, locate its vertices and foci
> 1. Determine whether the transverse axis lies on the $x$-or $y$-axis. Notice that $a^2$ is always under the variable with the positive coefficient. So, if you set the other variable equal to zero, you can easily find the intercepts. In the case where the hyperbola is centered at the origin, the intercepts coincide with the vertices.
> - If the equation has the form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$, then the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis. The vertices are located at $( \pm a, 0)$, and the foci are located at $( \pm c, 0)$.
> - If the equation has the form $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$, then the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. The vertices are located at $(0, \pm a)$, and the foci are located at $(0, \pm c)$.
> 1. Solve for $a$ using the equation $a=\sqrt{a^2}$.
> 2. Solve for $c$ using the equation $c=\sqrt{a^2+b^2}$.






> [!exercise] Example 12.2.1: Locating a Hyperbola's Vertices and Foci
> 
> Identify the vertices and foci of the hyperbola with equation $\frac{y^2}{49}-\frac{x^2}{32}=1$.
> 



##### solution

We are given the hyperbola

$$
\frac{y^2}{49}-\frac{x^2}{32}=1.
$$

This equation is in the standard form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore,

$$
a^2=49
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=32.
$$

Now solve for $a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a
&=\sqrt{49}\\
&=7.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the hyperbola is centered at the origin and the positive term is the $y$-term, the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. Therefore the vertices have the form

$$
(0,\pm a).
$$

Substitute $a=7$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm a)
&=(0,\pm 7).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(0,7)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-7).
$$

To find the foci, use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=49$ and $b^2=32$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=49+32\\
&=81.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{81}\\
&=9.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis, the foci have the form

$$
(0,\pm c).
$$

Substitute $c=9$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm c)
&=(0,\pm 9).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(0,9)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-9).
$$


++++



The equation has the form $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$, so the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. The hyperbola is centered at the origin, so the vertices serve as the $y$ intercepts of the graph. To find the vertices, set $x=0$, and solve for $y$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
1 & =\frac{y^2}{49}-\frac{x^2}{32} \\
1 & =\frac{y^2}{49}-\frac{0^2}{32} \\
1 & =\frac{y^2}{49} \\
y^2 & =49 \\
y & = \pm \sqrt{49} \\
& = \pm 7
\end{aligned}
$$


The foci are located at $(0, \pm c)$. Solving for $c$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
c & =\sqrt{a^2+b^2} \\
& =\sqrt{49+32} \\
& =\sqrt{81} \\
& =9
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore, the vertices are located at $(0, \pm 7)$, and the foci are located at $(0,9)$.




> [!exercise]
> Identify the vertices and foci of the hyperbola with equation $\frac{x^2}{9}-\frac{y^2}{25}=1$.


##### solution

We are given the hyperbola

$$
\frac{x^2}{9}-\frac{y^2}{25}=1.
$$

This equation is already in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore,

$$
a^2=9
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=25.
$$

Now solve for $a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a
&=\sqrt{9}\\
&=3.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the hyperbola is centered at the origin and the positive term is the $x$-term, the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis. Therefore the vertices have the form

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

Substitute $a=3$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm a,0)
&=(\pm 3,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(3,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-3,0).
$$

To find the foci, use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=9$ and $b^2=25$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=9+25\\
&=34.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{34}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis, the foci have the form

$$
(\pm c,0).
$$

Substitute $c=\sqrt{34}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm c,0)
&=(\pm \sqrt{34},0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(\sqrt{34},0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-\sqrt{34},0).
$$


++++



## 1.2.1 Writing Equations of Hyperbolas in Standard Form


Just as with ellipses, writing the equation for a hyperbola in standard form allows us to calculate the key features: its center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, asymptotes, and the lengths and positions of the transverse and conjugate axes. Conversely, an equation for a hyperbola can be found given its key features. We begin by finding standard equations for hyperbolas centered at the origin. Then we will turn our attention to finding standard equations for hyperbolas centered at some point other than the origin.



## 1.2.2 Hyperbolas Centered at the Origin


> [!review]
> Given the coordinates of the vertices and foci of a hyperbola centered at the origin, how can the equation of the hyperbola in standard form be obtained?


##### problem 1

To obtain the standard form equation of a hyperbola centered at the origin from its vertices and foci, one first determines whether the vertices and foci lie on the $x$-axis or on the $y$-axis. This tells us whether the transverse axis is horizontal or vertical.

If the vertices and foci have the form

$$
(\pm a,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(\pm c,0),
$$

then the transverse axis is the $x$-axis, and the standard form of the equation is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

If the vertices and foci have the form

$$
(0,\pm a)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,\pm c),
$$

then the transverse axis is the $y$-axis, and the standard form of the equation is

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

After the correct form is chosen, one reads off $a$ from the vertices and $c$ from the foci. Then one uses the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2
$$

to solve for $b^2$. Equivalently, one may write

$$
b^2=c^2-a^2.
$$

Finally, one substitutes the values of $a^2$ and $b^2$ into the appropriate standard form. That gives the equation of the hyperbola.


+++++


Reviewing the standard forms given for hyperbolas centered at $(0,0)$, we see that the vertices, co-vertices, and foci are related by the equation $c^2=a^2+b^2$. Note that this equation can also be rewritten as $b^2=c^2-a^2$. This relationship is used to write the equation for a hyperbola when given the coordinates of its foci and vertices.


> [!NOTE]
> How to: Given the vertices and foci of a hyperbola centered at ( 0,0 ), write its equation in standard form
> 1. Determine whether the transverse axis lies on the $x$ - or $y$-axis.
> - If the given coordinates of the vertices and foci have the form ( $\pm a, 0$ ) and ( $\pm c, 0$ ), respectively, then the transverse axis is the $x$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$.
> - If the given coordinates of the vertices and foci have the form $(0, \pm a)$ and $(0, \pm c)$, respectively, then the transverse axis is the $y$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$.
> 1. Find $b^2$ using the equation $b^2=c^2-a^2$.
> 2. Substitute the values for $a^2$ and $b^2$ into the standard form of the equation determined in Step 1.




> [!exercise] Example 12.2.2: Finding the Equation of a Hyperbola Centered at $(0,0)$ Given its Foci and Vertices
> 
> What is the standard form equation of the hyperbola that has vertices $( \pm 6,0)$ and foci $( \pm 2 \sqrt{10}, 0)$ ?



##### solution

We are asked to find the standard form equation of the hyperbola with vertices

$$
(\pm 6,0)
$$

and foci

$$
(\pm 2\sqrt{10},0).
$$

Because both the vertices and the foci lie on the $x$-axis, the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis. Therefore the hyperbola has the form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

From the vertices $(\pm 6,0)$, we read off

$$
a=6.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
a^2
&=6^2\\
&=36.
\end{aligned}
$$

From the foci $(\pm 2\sqrt{10},0)$, we read off

$$
c=2\sqrt{10}.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=(2\sqrt{10})^2\\
&=2^2(\sqrt{10})^2\\
&=4\cdot 10\\
&=40.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Solve for $b^2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=40-36\\
&=4.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now substitute $a^2=36$ and $b^2=4$ into the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

This gives

$$
\frac{x^2}{36}-\frac{y^2}{4}=1.
$$

Therefore, the standard form equation of the hyperbola is

$$
\frac{x^2}{36}-\frac{y^2}{4}=1.
$$



++++


The vertices and foci are on the $x$-axis. Thus, the equation for the hyperbola will have the form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$.
The vertices are $( \pm 6,0)$, so $a=6$ and $a^2=36$.
The foci are $( \pm 2 \sqrt{10}, 0)$, so $c=2 \sqrt{10}$ and $c^2=40$.
Solving for $b^2$, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
& b^2=c^2-a^2 \\
& b^2=40-36 \quad \text { Substitute for } c^2 \text { and } a^2 \\
& b^2=4 \quad \text { Subtract. }
\end{aligned}
$$


Finally, we substitute $a^2=36$ and $b^2=4$ into the standard form of the equation, $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$. The equation of the hyperbola is $\frac{x^2}{36}-\frac{y^2}{4}=1$, as shown in Figure 12.2.6.



> [!figure]
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412003325.png)




> [!exercise]
> What is the standard form equation of the hyperbola that has vertices $(0, \pm 2)$ and foci $(0, \pm 2 \sqrt{5})$ ?



##### solution

We are asked to find the standard form equation of the hyperbola with vertices

$$
(0,\pm 2)
$$

and foci

$$
(0,\pm 2\sqrt{5}).
$$

Because both the vertices and the foci lie on the $y$-axis, the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. Therefore the hyperbola has the form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

From the vertices $(0,\pm 2)$, we read off

$$
a=2.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
a^2
&=2^2\\
&=4.
\end{aligned}
$$

From the foci $(0,\pm 2\sqrt{5})$, we read off

$$
c=2\sqrt{5}.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=(2\sqrt{5})^2\\
&=2^2(\sqrt{5})^2\\
&=4\cdot 5\\
&=20.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Solve for $b^2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=20-4\\
&=16.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now substitute $a^2=4$ and $b^2=16$ into the standard form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

This gives

$$
\frac{y^2}{4}-\frac{x^2}{16}=1.
$$

Therefore, the standard form equation of the hyperbola is

$$
\frac{y^2}{4}-\frac{x^2}{16}=1.
$$



++++



## 1.2.3 Hyperbolas Not Centered at the Origin



> [!review]
> 1. What are the two standard forms of the equation of a hyperbola centered at a point ( $h, k$ ), and what does each form's structure indicate about the orientation of the transverse axis?
> 2. Given a hyperbola centered at ( $h, k$ ) in standard form, how are the coordinates of the center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci, and the equations of the asymptotes, obtained from the equation?


##### problem 1

The two standard forms of the equation of a hyperbola centered at a point $(h,k)$ are

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

and

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

The first form indicates that the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$-axis, because the positive term is the squared expression involving $x-h$. The second form indicates that the transverse axis is parallel to the $y$-axis, because the positive term is the squared expression involving $y-k$. Thus, as with hyperbolas centered at the origin, the variable with the positive term determines the direction of the transverse axis.

##### problem 2

If the hyperbola is written in the form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

then the center is

$$
(h,k).
$$

Because the transverse axis is horizontal, the vertices are obtained by moving $a$ units left and right from the center:

$$
(h\pm a,k).
$$

The co-vertices are obtained by moving $b$ units up and down from the center:

$$
(h,k\pm b).
$$

The foci are obtained by moving $c$ units left and right from the center:

$$
(h\pm c,k),
$$

where

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

The asymptotes pass through the center and have slopes $\pm \frac{b}{a}$, so their equations are

$$
y=\pm \frac{b}{a}(x-h)+k.
$$

If the hyperbola is written in the form

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1,
$$

then the center is again

$$
(h,k).
$$

Because the transverse axis is vertical, the vertices are

$$
(h,k\pm a).
$$

The co-vertices are

$$
(h\pm b,k).
$$

The foci are

$$
(h,k\pm c),
$$

where again

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

The asymptotes pass through the center and have slopes $\pm \frac{a}{b}$, so their equations are

$$
y=\pm \frac{a}{b}(x-h)+k.
$$


+++++


Like the graphs for other equations, the graph of a hyperbola can be translated. If a hyperbola is translated $h$ units horizontally and $k$ units vertically, the center of the hyperbola will be $(h, k)$. This translation results in the standard form of the equation we saw previously, with $x$ replaced by $(x-h)$ and $y$ replaced by $(y-k)$.



STANDARD FORMS OF THE EQUATION OF A HYPERBOLA WITH CENTER $(H, K)$
The standard form of the equation of a hyperbola with center $(h, k)$ and transverse axis parallel to the $x$-axis is

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

where
- the length of the transverse axis is $2 a$
- the coordinates of the vertices are ( $h \pm a, k$ )
- the length of the conjugate axis is $2 b$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are ( $h, k \pm b$ )
- the distance between the foci is $2 c$, where $c^2=a^2+b^2$
- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h \pm c, k$ )

The asymptotes of the hyperbola coincide with the diagonals of the central rectangle. The length of the rectangle is $2 a$ and its width is $2 b$. The slopes of the diagonals are $\pm \frac{b}{a}$, and each diagonal passes through the center $(h, k)$. Using the point-slope formula, it is simple to show that the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{b}{a}(x-h)+k$. See Figure 12.2.7a.

The standard form of the equation of a hyperbola with center $(h, k)$ and transverse axis parallel to the $y$-axis is

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

where
- the length of the transverse axis is $2 a$
- the coordinates of the vertices are ( $h, k \pm a$ )
- the length of the conjugate axis is $2 b$
- the coordinates of the co-vertices are ( $h \pm b, k$ )
- the distance between the foci is $2 c$, where $c^2=a^2+b^2$
- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h, k \pm c$ )

Using the reasoning above, the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{a}{b}(x-h)+k$. See Figure 12.2.7b.



> [!figure]
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412003735.png)
> 



Like hyperbolas centered at the origin, hyperbolas centered at a point $(h, k)$ have vertices, co-vertices, and foci that are related by the equation $c^2=a^2+b^2$. We can use this relationship along with the midpoint and distance formulas to find the standard equation of a hyperbola when the vertices and foci are given.






> [!NOTE] How to: Given the vertices and foci of a hyperbola centered at $(h, k)$, write its equation in standard form
> 
> 1. Determine whether the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$ - or $y$-axis.
> - If the $y$-coordinates of the given vertices and foci are the same, then the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2} -\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$.
> - If the $x$-coordinates of the given vertices and foci are the same, then the transverse axis is parallel to the $y$-axis. Use the standard form $\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}$
> 
> $$
> -\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1
> $$
> 
> 2. Identify the center of the hyperbola, $(h, k)$, using the midpoint formula and the given coordinates for the vertices.
> 3. Find $a^2$ by solving for the length of the transverse axis, $2 a$, which is the distance between the given vertices.
> 4. Find $c^2$ using $h$ and $k$ found in Step 2 along with the given coordinates for the foci.
> 5. Solve for $b^2$ using the equation $b^2=c^2-a^2$.
> 6. Substitute the values for $h, k, a^2$, and $b^2$ into the standard form of the equation determined in Step 1.
> 
> 



> [!exercise]
> Example: Finding the Equation of a Hyperbola Centered at $(h, k)$ Given its Foci and Vertices
> 
> What is the standard form equation of the hyperbola that has vertices at $(0,-2)$ and $(6,-2)$ and foci at $(-2,-2)$ and $(8,-2)$ ?



##### solution

We are asked to find the standard form equation of the hyperbola with vertices

$$
(0,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(6,-2)
$$

and foci

$$
(-2,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(8,-2).
$$

The $y$-coordinates of both vertices are the same, and the $y$-coordinates of both foci are also the same. Therefore the transverse axis is horizontal, so the hyperbola has the form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Now find the center $(h,k)$. The center is the midpoint of the two vertices:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k)
&=\left(\frac{0+6}{2},\frac{-2+(-2)}{2}\right)\\
&=\left(\frac{6}{2},\frac{-4}{2}\right)\\
&=(3,-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
h=3
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
k=-2.
$$

Next find $a$. Since the vertices are $(h\pm a,k)$, the length of the transverse axis is the distance between the two vertices, which is $2a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2a
&=|6-0|\\
&=6.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a
&=\frac{6}{2}\\
&=3.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
a^2
&=3^2\\
&=9.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now find $c$. Since the foci are $(h\pm c,k)$ and we already know that $h=3$, use the focus $(8,-2)$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
h+c
&=8\\
3+c
&=8\\
c
&=8-3\\
&=5.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=5^2\\
&=25.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Solve for $b^2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=25-9\\
&=16.
\end{aligned}
$$

Finally, substitute $h=3$, $k=-2$, $a^2=9$, and $b^2=16$ into the standard form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

This gives

$$
\frac{(x-3)^2}{9}-\frac{(y+2)^2}{16}=1.
$$

Therefore, the standard form equation of the hyperbola is

$$
\frac{(x-3)^2}{9}-\frac{(y+2)^2}{16}=1.
$$



++++


The $y$-coordinates of the vertices and foci are the same, so the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$-axis. Thus, the equation of the hyperbola will have the form

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1
$$


First, we identify the center, $(h, k)$. The center is halfway between the vertices $(0,-2)$ and $(6,-2)$. Applying the midpoint formula, we have

$$
