(h, k)=\left(\frac{0+6}{2}, \frac{-2+(-2)}{2}\right)=(3,-2)
$$


Next, we find $a^2$. The length of the transverse axis, $2 a$, is bounded by the vertices. So, we can find $a^2$ by finding the distance between the $x$-coordinates of the vertices.

$$
\begin{aligned}
2 a & =|0-6| \\
2 a & =6 \\
a & =3 \\
a^2 & =9
\end{aligned}
$$


Now we need to find $c^2$. The coordinates of the foci are $(h \pm c, k)$. So $(h-c, k)=(-2,-2)$ and $(h+c, k)=(8,-2)$. We can use the $x$-coordinate from either of these points to solve for $c$. Using the point $(8,-2)$, and substituting $h=3$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
h+c & =8 \\
3+c & =8 \\
c & =5 \\
c^2 & =25
\end{aligned}
$$


Next, solve for $b^2$ using the equation $b^2=c^2-a^2$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2 & =c^2-a^2 \\
& =25-9 \\
& =16
\end{aligned}
$$


Finally, substitute the values found for $h, k, a^2$, and $b^2$ into the standard form of the equation.

$$
\frac{(x-3)^2}{9}-\frac{(y+2)^2}{16}=1
$$




> [!exercise]
> What is the standard form equation of the hyperbola that has vertices $(1,-2)$ and $(1,8)$ and foci $(1,-10)$ and $(1,16)$ ?



##### solution

We are asked to find the standard form equation of the hyperbola with vertices

$$
(1,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(1,8)
$$

and foci

$$
(1,-10)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(1,16).
$$

Because the $x$-coordinates of both vertices are the same, and the $x$-coordinates of both foci are also the same, the transverse axis is vertical. Therefore the hyperbola has the form

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Now find the center $(h,k)$. The center is the midpoint of the two vertices:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k)
&=\left(\frac{1+1}{2},\frac{-2+8}{2}\right)\\
&=\left(\frac{2}{2},\frac{6}{2}\right)\\
&=(1,3).
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
h=1
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
k=3.
$$

Next find $a$. Since the vertices are $(h,k\pm a)$, the length of the transverse axis is the distance between the two vertices, which is $2a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2a
&=|8-(-2)|\\
&=|10|\\
&=10.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $a$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a
&=\frac{10}{2}\\
&=5.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
a^2
&=5^2\\
&=25.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now find $c$. Since the foci are $(h,k\pm c)$ and we already know that $k=3$, use the focus $(1,16)$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
k+c
&=16\\
3+c
&=16\\
c
&=16-3\\
&=13.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=13^2\\
&=169.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Solve for $b^2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=169-25\\
&=144.
\end{aligned}
$$

Finally, substitute $h=1$, $k=3$, $a^2=25$, and $b^2=144$ into the standard form

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

This gives

$$
\frac{(y-3)^2}{25}-\frac{(x-1)^2}{144}=1.
$$

Therefore, the standard form equation of the hyperbola is

$$
\frac{(y-3)^2}{25}-\frac{(x-1)^2}{144}=1.
$$


++++



## 1.2.4 Graphing Hyperbolas Centered at the Origin




When we have an equation in standard form for a hyperbola centered at the origin, we can interpret its parts to identify the key features of its graph: the center, vertices, co-vertices, asymptotes, foci, and lengths and positions of the transverse and conjugate axes. To graph hyperbolas centered at the origin, we use the standard form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$ for horizontal hyperbolas and the standard form $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$ for vertical hyperbolas.



> [!NOTE] How to: Given a standard form equation for a hyperbola centered at $(0,0)$, sketch the graph
> 
> 1. Determine which of the standard forms applies to the given equation.
> 2. Use the standard form identified in Step 1 to determine the position of the transverse axis; coordinates for the vertices, co-vertices, and foci; and the equations for the asymptotes.
> - If the equation is in the form $\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1$, then
> - the transverse axis is on the $x$-axis
> - the coordinates of the vertices are \\((\pm a,0)\0
> - the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(0, \pm b)$
> - the coordinates of the foci are ( ± c,0)
> - the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{b}{a} x$
> 
> ○ If the equation is in the form $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$, then
> - the transverse axis is on the $y$-axis
> - the coordinates of the vertices are $(0, \pm a)$
> - the coordinates of the co-vertices are ( $\pm b, 0$ )
> - the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm c)$
> - the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{a}{b} x$
> 3. Solve for the coordinates of the foci using the equation $c= \pm \sqrt{a^2+b^2}$.
> 4. Plot the vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes in the coordinate plane, and draw a smooth curve to form the hyperbola.


> [!exercise] Example: Graphing a Hyperbola Centered at $(0,0)$ Given an Equation in Standard Form
> 
> Graph the hyperbola given by the equation $\frac{y^2}{64}-\frac{x^2}{36}=1$. Identify and label the vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes.



##### solution

We are given the hyperbola

$$
\frac{y^2}{64}-\frac{x^2}{36}=1.
$$

This equation is already in the standard form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore,

$$
a^2=64
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=36.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{64}\\
&=8
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{36}\\
&=6.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the positive term is the $y$-term, the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis. Since the hyperbola is centered at the origin, the center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

The vertices have the form

$$
(0,\pm a).
$$

Substitute $a=8$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm a)
&=(0,\pm 8).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(0,8)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-8).
$$

The co-vertices have the form

$$
(\pm b,0).
$$

Substitute $b=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm b,0)
&=(\pm 6,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(6,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-6,0).
$$

To find the foci, use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=64$ and $b^2=36$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=64+36\\
&=100.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{100}\\
&=10.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the transverse axis lies on the $y$-axis, the foci have the form

$$
(0,\pm c).
$$

Substitute $c=10$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm c)
&=(0,\pm 10).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(0,10)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-10).
$$

The asymptotes for a hyperbola of the form

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1
$$

are

$$
y=\pm \frac{a}{b}x.
$$

Substitute $a=8$ and $b=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=\pm \frac{8}{6}x\\
&=\pm \frac{4}{3}x.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the asymptotes are

$$
y=\frac{4}{3}x
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
y=-\frac{4}{3}x.
$$

To graph the hyperbola, plot the center, the vertices, the co-vertices, and the foci. Then draw the asymptotes. Finally, sketch the two branches opening upward and downward, approaching the asymptotes as they move away from the center.




![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423233742.png)





**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  y^2/64 - x^2/36 == 1,
  {x, -15, 15}, {y, -15, 15},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-11.25, -15}, {11.25, 15}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-11.25, 15}, {11.25, -15}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{0, 8}, {0, -8}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{6, 0}, {-6, 0}}],
    Purple, Point[{{0, 10}, {0, -10}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {0, 0}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(0,8)", {0, 8}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(0,-8)", {0, -8}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(6,0)", {6, 0}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(-6,0)", {-6, 0}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(0,10)", {0, 10}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(0,-10)", {0, -10}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["y = (4/3)x", {8.5, 11.5}, {-1, 0}],
    Text["y = -(4/3)x", {8.5, -11.5}, {-1, 0}]
  }
]
```

**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y_top = np.linspace(8.05, 15, 400)
y_bottom = np.linspace(-15, -8.05, 400)
x_top = 6 * np.sqrt(y_top**2 / 64 - 1)
x_bottom = 6 * np.sqrt(y_bottom**2 / 64 - 1)

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(0, 8), (0, -8)]
co_vertices = [(6, 0), (-6, 0)]
foci = [(0, 10), (0, -10)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x_top, y_top, color="C0")
ax.plot(-x_top, y_top, color="C0", label=r"$\frac{y^2}{64}-\frac{x^2}{36}=1$")
ax.plot(x_bottom, y_bottom, color="C0")
ax.plot(-x_bottom, y_bottom, color="C0")

x_line = np.linspace(-11.25, 11.25, 200)
ax.plot(x_line, (4/3) * x_line, "--", color="gray")
ax.plot(x_line, -(4/3) * x_line, "--", color="gray")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0, -0.8, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(0, 8, "(0,8)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(0, -8, "(0,-8)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(6, 0, "(6,0)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(-6, 0, "(-6,0)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(0, 10, "(0,10)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(0, -10, "(0,-10)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(8.5, 11.5, r"$y=\frac{4}{3}x$", ha="left")
ax.text(8.5, -11.5, r"$y=-\frac{4}{3}x$", ha="left")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-15, 15)
ax.set_ylim(-15, 15)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++



The standard form that applies to the given equation is $\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1$. Thus, the transverse axis is on the $y$-axis
The coordinates of the vertices are $(0, \pm a)=(0, \pm \sqrt{64})=(0, \pm 8)$
The coordinates of the co-vertices are $( \pm b, 0)=( \pm \sqrt{36}, 0)=( \pm 6,0)$
The coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm c)$, where $c= \pm \sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Solving for $c$, we have

$$
c= \pm \sqrt{a^2+b^2}= \pm \sqrt{64+36}= \pm \sqrt{100}= \pm 10
$$


Therefore, the coordinates of the foci are $(0, \pm 10)$
The equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{a}{b} x= \pm \frac{8}{6} x= \pm \frac{4}{3} x$
Plot and label the vertices and co-vertices, and then sketch the central rectangle. Sides of the rectangle are parallel to the axes and pass through the vertices and co-vertices. Sketch and extend the diagonals of the central rectangle to show the asymptotes. The central rectangle and asymptotes provide the framework needed to sketch an accurate graph of the hyperbola. Label the foci and asymptotes, and draw a smooth curve to form the hyperbola, as shown in Figure 12.2.8.


> [!figure]
> 
> ![500](Pasted%20image%2020260412004431.png)
> 
> 





> [!exercise]
> Graph the hyperbola given by the equation $\frac{x^2}{144}-\frac{y^2}{81}=1$. Identify and label the vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes.



##### solution

We are given the hyperbola

$$
\frac{x^2}{144}-\frac{y^2}{81}=1.
$$

This equation is already in the standard form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore,

$$
a^2=144
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
b^2=81.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{144}\\
&=12
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{81}\\
&=9.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the positive term is the $x$-term, the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis. Since the hyperbola is centered at the origin, the center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

The vertices have the form

$$
(\pm a,0).
$$

Substitute $a=12$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm a,0)
&=(\pm 12,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(12,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-12,0).
$$

The co-vertices have the form

$$
(0,\pm b).
$$

Substitute $b=9$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,\pm b)
&=(0,\pm 9).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(0,9)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-9).
$$

To find the foci, use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=144$ and $b^2=81$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=144+81\\
&=225.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{225}\\
&=15.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the transverse axis lies on the $x$-axis, the foci have the form

$$
(\pm c,0).
$$

Substitute $c=15$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm c,0)
&=(\pm 15,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(15,0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-15,0).
$$

The asymptotes for a hyperbola of the form

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}-\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1
$$

are

$$
y=\pm \frac{b}{a}x.
$$

Substitute $a=12$ and $b=9$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=\pm \frac{9}{12}x\\
&=\pm \frac{3}{4}x.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the asymptotes are

$$
y=\frac{3}{4}x
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
y=-\frac{3}{4}x.
$$

To graph the hyperbola, plot the center, the vertices, the co-vertices, and the foci. Then draw the asymptotes. Finally, sketch the two branches opening left and right, approaching the asymptotes as they move away from the center.



![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423233607.png)


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2/144 - y^2/81 == 1,
  {x, -20, 20}, {y, -15, 15},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-20, -15}, {20, 15}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-20, 15}, {20, -15}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{12, 0}, {-12, 0}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{0, 9}, {0, -9}}],
    Purple, Point[{{15, 0}, {-15, 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {0, 0}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(12,0)", {12, 0}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(-12,0)", {-12, 0}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(0,9)", {0, 9}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(0,-9)", {0, -9}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(15,0)", {15, 0}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["(-15,0)", {-15, 0}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["y = (3/4)x", {14, 10.5}, {-1, 0}],
    Text["y = -(3/4)x", {14, -10.5}, {-1, 0}]
  }
]
```

**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x_right = np.linspace(12.05, 20, 400)
x_left = np.linspace(-20, -12.05, 400)
y_right = 9 * np.sqrt(x_right**2 / 144 - 1)
y_left = 9 * np.sqrt(x_left**2 / 144 - 1)

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(12, 0), (-12, 0)]
co_vertices = [(0, 9), (0, -9)]
foci = [(15, 0), (-15, 0)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x_right, y_right, color="C0")
ax.plot(x_right, -y_right, color="C0", label=r"$\frac{x^2}{144}-\frac{y^2}{81}=1$")
ax.plot(x_left, y_left, color="C0")
ax.plot(x_left, -y_left, color="C0")

x_line = np.linspace(-20, 20, 200)
ax.plot(x_line, (3/4) * x_line, "--", color="gray")
ax.plot(x_line, -(3/4) * x_line, "--", color="gray")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0, -0.8, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(12, 0, "(12,0)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(-12, 0, "(-12,0)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(0, 9, "(0,9)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(0, -9, "(0,-9)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(15, 0, "(15,0)", ha="center", va="bottom")
ax.text(-15, 0, "(-15,0)", ha="center", va="bottom")
ax.text(14, 10.5, r"$y=\frac{3}{4}x$", ha="left")
ax.text(14, -10.5, r"$y=-\frac{3}{4}x$", ha="left")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-20, 20)
ax.set_ylim(-15, 15)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++

## 1.3.3 Graphing Hyperbolas Not Centered at the Origin



Graphing hyperbolas centered at a point $(h, k)$ other than the origin is similar to graphing ellipses centered at a point other than the origin. We use the standard forms $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$ for horizontal hyperbolas, and $\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1$ for vertical hyperbolas. From these standard form equations we can easily calculate and plot key features of the graph: the coordinates of its center, vertices, co-vertices, and foci; the equations of its asymptotes; and the positions of the transverse and conjugate axes.




> [!note] How to: Given a general form for a hyperbola centered at ( $h, k$ ), sketch the graph
> 
> 1. Convert the general form to that standard form. Determine which of the standard forms applies to the given equation.
> 2. Use the standard form identified in Step 1 to determine the position of the transverse axis; coordinates for the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci; and equations for the asymptotes.
> 	1. If the equation is in the form $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$, then
> 	- the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$-axis
> 	- the center is $(h, k)$
> 	- the coordinates of the vertices are ( $h \pm a, k$ )
> 	- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h, k \pm b)$
> 	- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h \pm c, k$ )
> 	- the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{b}{a}(x-h)+k$
> 	1. If the equation is in the form $\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1$, then
> 	- the transverse axis is parallel to the $y$-axis
> 	- the center is $(h, k)$
> 	- the coordinates of the vertices are $(h, k \pm a)$
> 	- the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h \pm b, k)$
> 	- the coordinates of the foci are ( $h, k \pm c$ )
> 	- the equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{a}{b}(x-h)+k$
> 3. Solve for the coordinates of the foci using the equation $c= \pm \sqrt{a^2+b^2}$.
> 4. Plot the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes in the coordinate plane and draw a smooth curve to form the hyperbola.
> 
> 



> [!exercise]
> Example: Graphing a Hyperbola Centered at $(h, k)$ Given an Equation in General Form
>
>Graph the hyperbola given by the equation $9 x^2-4 y^2-36 x-40 y-388=0$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes.



Start by expressing the equation in standard form. Group terms that contain the same variable, and move the constant to the opposite side of the equation.

$$
\left(9 x^2-36 x\right)-\left(4 y^2+40 y\right)=388
$$


Factor the leading coefficient of each expression.

$$
9\left(x^2-4 x\right)-4\left(y^2+10 y\right)=388
$$


Complete the square twice. Remember to balance the equation by adding the same constants to each side.

$$
9\left(x^2-4 x+4\right)-4\left(y^2+10 y+25\right)=388+36-100
$$


Rewrite as perfect squares.

$$
9(x-2)^2-4(y+5)^2=324
$$


Divide both sides by the constant term to place the equation in standard form.

$$
\frac{(x-2)^2}{36}-\frac{(y+5)^2}{81}=1
$$


The standard form that applies to the given equation is $\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1$, where $a^2=36$ and $b^2=81$, or $a=6$ and $b=9$. Thus, the transverse axis is parallel to the $x$-axis. It follows that:
the center of the ellipse is $(h, k)=(2,-5)$
the coordinates of the vertices are $(h \pm a, k)=(2 \pm 6,-5)$, or $(-4,-5)$ and $(8,-5)$
the coordinates of the co-vertices are $(h, k \pm b)=(2,-5 \pm 9)$, or $(2,-14)$ and $(2,4)$
the coordinates of the foci are ( $h \pm c, k$ ), where $c= \pm \sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Solving for $c$, we have

$$
c= \pm \sqrt{36+81}= \pm \sqrt{117}= \pm 3 \sqrt{13}
$$


Therefore, the coordinates of the foci are $(2-3 \sqrt{13},-5)$ and $(2+3 \sqrt{13},-5)$.
The equations of the asymptotes are $y= \pm \frac{b}{a}(x-h)+k= \pm \frac{3}{2}(x-2)-5$.
Next, we plot and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes and draw smooth curves to form the hyperbola, as shown in Figure 12.2.10.



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412004740.png)
> 
> 




> [!exercise]
> Graph the hyperbola given by the standard form of an equation $\frac{(y+4)^2}{100}-\frac{(x-3)^2}{64}=1$. Identify and label the center, vertices, co-vertices, foci, and asymptotes.


##### solution

We are given the hyperbola

$$
\frac{(y+4)^2}{100}-\frac{(x-3)^2}{64}=1.
$$

This equation is already in the standard form

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore,

$$
h=3,
\qquad
k=-4,
\qquad
a^2=100,
\qquad
b^2=64.
$$

Now solve for $a$ and $b$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
a&=\sqrt{100}\\
&=10
\end{aligned}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\begin{aligned}
b&=\sqrt{64}\\
&=8.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the positive term is the $y$-term, the transverse axis is vertical. So the center is

$$
(h,k)=(3,-4).
$$

The vertices have the form

$$
(h,k\pm a).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $k=-4$, and $a=10$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm a)
&=(3,-4\pm 10).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(3,-4+10)&=(3,6),\\
(3,-4-10)&=(3,-14).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the vertices are

$$
(3,6)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(3,-14).
$$

The co-vertices have the form

$$
(h\pm b,k).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $b=8$, and $k=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm b,k)
&=(3\pm 8,-4).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compute each point:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(3+8,-4)&=(11,-4),\\
(3-8,-4)&=(-5,-4).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the co-vertices are

$$
(11,-4)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-5,-4).
$$

To find the foci, use the hyperbola relation

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Substitute $a^2=100$ and $b^2=64$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c^2
&=100+64\\
&=164.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now solve for $c$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
c
&=\sqrt{164}\\
&=\sqrt{4\cdot 41}\\
&=2\sqrt{41}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the transverse axis is vertical, the foci have the form

$$
(h,k\pm c).
$$

Substitute $h=3$, $k=-4$, and $c=2\sqrt{41}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k\pm c)
&=(3,-4\pm 2\sqrt{41}).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the foci are

$$
(3,-4+2\sqrt{41})
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(3,-4-2\sqrt{41}).
$$

The asymptotes for a hyperbola of the form

$$
\frac{(y-k)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(x-h)^2}{b^2}=1
$$

are

$$
y=\pm \frac{a}{b}(x-h)+k.
$$

Substitute $a=10$, $b=8$, $h=3$, and $k=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=\pm \frac{10}{8}(x-3)-4\\
&=\pm \frac{5}{4}(x-3)-4.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the asymptotes are

$$
y=\frac{5}{4}(x-3)-4
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
y=-\frac{5}{4}(x-3)-4.
$$

To graph the hyperbola, plot the center, the vertices, the co-vertices, and the foci. Then draw the asymptotes. Finally, sketch the two branches opening upward and downward, approaching the asymptotes as they move away from the center.

Mathematica code:

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (y + 4)^2/100 - (x - 3)^2/64 == 1,
  {x, -12, 18}, {y, -20, 12},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-9.8, -20}, {15.8, 12}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{15.8, -20}, {-9.8, 12}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{3, -4}}],
    Blue, Point[{{3, 6}, {3, -14}}],
    Darker[Green], Point[{{11, -4}, {-5, -4}}],
    Purple, Point[{{3, -4 + 2 Sqrt[41]}, {3, -4 - 2 Sqrt[41]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center", {3, -4}, {0, -1.5}],
    Text["(3,6)", {3, 6}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(3,-14)", {3, -14}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(11,-4)", {11, -4}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(-5,-4)", {-5, -4}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(3,-4 + 2 Sqrt[41])", {3, -4 + 2 Sqrt[41]}, {1.8, 0}],
    Text["(3,-4 - 2 Sqrt[41])", {3, -4 - 2 Sqrt[41]}, {1.8, 0}],
    Text["y = (5/4)(x-3)-4", {11, 6}, {-1, 0}],
    Text["y = -(5/4)(x-3)-4", {11, -14}, {-1, 0}]
  }
]
```

Python code:

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y_top = np.linspace(6.05, 12, 400)
y_bottom = np.linspace(-20, -14.05, 400)
x_top = 3 + 8 * np.sqrt((y_top + 4)**2 / 100 - 1)
x_top_left = 3 - 8 * np.sqrt((y_top + 4)**2 / 100 - 1)
x_bottom = 3 + 8 * np.sqrt((y_bottom + 4)**2 / 100 - 1)
x_bottom_left = 3 - 8 * np.sqrt((y_bottom + 4)**2 / 100 - 1)

center = (3, -4)
vertices = [(3, 6), (3, -14)]
co_vertices = [(11, -4), (-5, -4)]
foci = [(3, -4 + 2 * np.sqrt(41)), (3, -4 - 2 * np.sqrt(41))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x_top, y_top, color="C0")
ax.plot(x_top_left, y_top, color="C0", label=r"$\frac{(y+4)^2}{100}-\frac{(x-3)^2}{64}=1$")
ax.plot(x_bottom, y_bottom, color="C0")
ax.plot(x_bottom_left, y_bottom, color="C0")

x_line = np.linspace(-9.8, 15.8, 200)
ax.plot(x_line, (5/4) * (x_line - 3) - 4, "--", color="gray")
ax.plot(x_line, -(5/4) * (x_line - 3) - 4, "--", color="gray")

ax.scatter(*center, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*co_vertices), color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="purple", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(3, -4.8, "Center", ha="center")
ax.text(3, 6, "(3,6)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(3, -14, "(3,-14)", ha="left", va="center")
ax.text(11, -4, "(11,-4)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(-5, -4, "(-5,-4)", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(3, -4 + 2 * np.sqrt(41), r"$(3,-4+2\sqrt{41})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(3, -4 - 2 * np.sqrt(41), r"$(3,-4-2\sqrt{41})$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(11, 6, r"$y=\frac{5}{4}(x-3)-4$", ha="left")
ax.text(11, -14, r"$y=-\frac{5}{4}(x-3)-4$", ha="left")

ax.axhline(-4, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(3, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-12, 18)
ax.set_ylim(-20, 12)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++


# 1.4 The Parabola


> [!review]
> 1. What is the definition of a parabola as a set of points in the plane?
> 2. What are the directrix, focus, and latus rectum of a parabola, and what geometric relationships hold among the directrix, focus, vertex, and axis of symmetry?
> 3. Starting from the defining property of a parabola (equidistance from the focus and the directrix), derive the standard equation of a parabola with vertex at the origin and axis of symmetry along the $y$-axis.
> 4. What are the two standard forms of the equation of a parabola with vertex at the origin, and how does the sign of $p$ determine the direction in which the parabola opens?
> 5. Given the standard form of a parabola with vertex at the origin, how can one identify the axis of symmetry, focus, equation of the directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum?



##### problem 1

A parabola is the set of all points in the plane that are equidistant from a fixed point and a fixed line, where the fixed point does not lie on the fixed line. The fixed point is called the focus, and the fixed line is called the directrix.

##### problem 2

The directrix of a parabola is the fixed line used in its definition. The focus is the fixed point used in its definition. The latus rectum is the line segment through the focus that is parallel to the directrix and whose endpoints lie on the parabola.

The axis of symmetry passes through the focus and the vertex, and it is perpendicular to the directrix. The vertex is halfway between the focus and the directrix along the axis of symmetry. Thus the focus and the directrix lie on opposite sides of the vertex, and the vertex is the midpoint of that perpendicular separation.

##### problem 3

We derive the standard equation for a parabola with vertex at the origin and axis of symmetry along the $y$-axis. In this case the focus is

$$
(0,p)
$$

and the directrix is

$$
y=-p.
$$

Let $(x,y)$ be a point on the parabola. By definition of a parabola, the distance from $(x,y)$ to the focus equals the distance from $(x,y)$ to the directrix.

The distance from $(x,y)$ to the focus is

$$
\sqrt{(x-0)^2+(y-p)^2}.
$$

The distance from $(x,y)$ to the directrix is the vertical distance from $(x,y)$ to the line $y=-p$, which is

$$
y-(-p)=y+p.
$$

Set these equal:

$$
\sqrt{x^2+(y-p)^2}=y+p.
$$

Now square both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+(y-p)^2
&=(y+p)^2\\
x^2+\left(y^2-2py+p^2\right)
&=y^2+2py+p^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Subtract $y^2+p^2$ from both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-2py
&=2py.
\end{aligned}
$$

Add $2py$ to both sides:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2
&=4py.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, the standard equation of a parabola with vertex at the origin and axis of symmetry along the $y$-axis is

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

##### problem 4

The two standard forms of the equation of a parabola with vertex at the origin are

$$
y^2=4px
$$

and

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

If the equation is

$$
y^2=4px,
$$

then the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis. In that case:

- if $p>0$, the parabola opens to the right;
- if $p<0$, the parabola opens to the left.

If the equation is

$$
x^2=4py,
$$

then the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis. In that case:

- if $p>0$, the parabola opens upward;
- if $p<0$, the parabola opens downward.

So the sign of $p$ determines the direction in which the parabola opens along its axis of symmetry.

##### problem 5

Given a parabola in standard form with vertex at the origin, its features are read directly from the appropriate standard equation.

If the equation is

$$
y^2=4px,
$$

then the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis, the focus is

$$
(p,0),
$$

the directrix is

$$
x=-p,
$$

and the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(p,\pm 2p).
$$

If the equation is

$$
x^2=4py,
$$

then the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis, the focus is

$$
(0,p),
$$

the directrix is

$$
y=-p,
$$

and the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(\pm 2p,p).
$$

Thus the standard form tells you immediately the axis of symmetry, the focus, the directrix, and the latus-rectum endpoints once the value of $p$ is identified.


+++++


Like the ellipse and hyperbola, the parabola can also be defined by a set of points in the coordinate plane. A parabola is the set of all points ( $x, y$ ) in a plane that are the same distance from a fixed line, called the directrix, and a fixed point (the focus) not on the directrix.

Previously, we learned about a parabola's vertex and axis of symmetry. Now we extend the discussion to include other key features of the parabola (Figure 12.3.3). Notice that the axis of symmetry passes through the focus and vertex and is perpendicular to the directrix. The vertex is the midpoint between the directrix and the focus. The line segment that passes through the focus and is parallel to the directrix is called the latus rectum. The endpoints of the latus rectum lie on the curve. By definition, the distanced d from the focus to any point $P$ on the parabola is equal to the distance from $P$ to the directrix.



> [!figure]
> 
> ![500](Pasted%20image%2020260412005244.png)
> Key features of the parabola


To work with parabolas in the coordinate plane, we consider two cases: those with a vertex at the origin and those with a vertex at a point other than the origin. We begin with the former.




![500](Pasted%20image%2020260412005340.png)





Let $(x, y)$ be a point on the parabola with vertex $(0,0)$, focus $(0, p)$, and directrix $y=-p$ as shown in Figure 12.3.4. The distanced d from point $(x, y)$ to point $(x,-p)$ on the directrix is the difference of the $y$-values: $d=y+p$. The distance from the focus $(0, p)$ to the point $(x, y)$ is also equal to $d$ and can be expressed using the distance formula.

$$
\begin{aligned}
d & =\sqrt{(x-0)^2+(y-p)^2} \\
& =\sqrt{x^2+(y-p)^2}
\end{aligned}
$$


Set the two expressions for $d$ equal to each other and solve for $y$ to derive the equation of the parabola. We do this because the distance from $(x, y)$ to $(0, p)$ equals the distance from $(x, y)$ to $(x,-p)$.

$$
\sqrt{x^2+(y-p)^2}=y+p
$$


We then square both sides of the equation, expand the squared terms, and simplify by combining like terms.

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+(y-p)^2 & =(y+p)^2 \\
x^2+y^2-2 p y+p^2 & =y^2+2 p y+p^2 \\
x^2-2 p y & =2 p y \\
x^2 & =4 p y
\end{aligned}
$$


The equations of parabolas with vertex $(0,0)$ are $y^2=4 p x$ when the $x$-axis is the axis of symmetry and $x^2=4 p y$ when the $y$-axis is the axis of symmetry. These standard forms are given below, along with their general graphs and key features.





> [!NOTE] STANDARD FORMS OF PARABOLAS WITH VERTEX $(0,0)$
> 
> Table 12.3.1 and Figure 12.3.5 summarize the standard features of parabolas with a vertex at the origin.
> 
>
>
> | Table 12.3.1 |  |  |  |  |
> | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
> | Axis of Symmetry | Equation | Focus | Directrix | Endpoints of Latus Rectum |
> | $x$-axis | $y^2=4 p x$ | $(p, 0)$ | $x=-p$ | $(p, \pm 2 p)$ |
> | $y$-axis | $x^2=4 p y$ | $(0, p)$ | $y=-p$ | $( \pm 2 p, p)$ |
> 
> > [!figure]
> > 
> > ![500](Pasted%20image%2020260412005550.png)
> > (a) When $p>0$ and the axis of symmetry is the x-axis, the parabola opens right. (b) When $p<0$ and the axis of symmetry is the x-axis, the parabola opens left. (c) When $p<0$ and the axis of symmetry is the $\mathbf{y}$-axis, the parabola opens up. (d) When $p<0$ and the axis of symmetry is the $\mathbf{y}$-axis, the parabola opens down.
> 
> 




> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412010254.png)
> 
> 




> [!NOTE] How to: Given a standard form equation for a parabola centered at $(0,0)$, sketch the graph
> 
> 1. Determine which of the standard forms applies to the given equation: $y^2=4 p x$ or $x^2=4 p y$.
> 2. Use the standard form identified in Step 1 to determine the axis of symmetry, focus, equation of the directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.
> - If the equation is in the form $y^2=4 p x$, then
> 	- the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis, $y=0$
> 	- set $4 p$ equal to the coefficient of $x$ in the given equation to solve for $p$. If $p>0$, the parabola opens right. If $p<0$, the parabola opens left.
> 	- use $p$ to find the coordinates of the focus, ( $p, 0$ )
> 	- use $p$ to find the equation of the directrix, $x=-p$
> 	- use $p$ to find the endpoints of the latus rectum, ( $p, \pm 2 p$ ). Alternately, substitute $x=p$ into the original equation.
> - If the equation is in the form $x^2=4 p y$,then
> 	- the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis, $x=0$
> 	- set $4 p$ equal to the coefficient of $y$ in the given equation to solve for $p$. If $p>0$, the parabola opens up. If $p<0$, the parabola opens down.
> 	- use $p$ to find the coordinates of the focus, $(0, p)$
> 	- use $p$ to find equation of the directrix, $y=-p$
> 	- use $p$ to find the endpoints of the latus rectum, $( \pm 2 p, p)$
> 1. Plot the focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola.
> 
> 



> [!exercise]
> Graph $y^2=24 x$. Identify and label the focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.



##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
y^2=24x.
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

Therefore,

$$
4p=24.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=24\\
p&=\frac{24}{4}\\
&=6.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $p>0$, the parabola opens to the right.

For a parabola in the form

$$
y^2=4px,
$$

the focus is

$$
(p,0).
$$

Substitute $p=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(p,0)
&=(6,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(6,0).
$$

The directrix is

$$
x=-p.
$$

Substitute $p=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x&=-6.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
x=-6.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(p,\pm 2p).
$$

Substitute $p=6$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(p,\pm 2p)
&=(6,\pm 2\cdot 6)\\
&=(6,\pm 12).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(6,12)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(6,-12).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the focus, the directrix, and the endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening to the right, symmetric about the $x$-axis.





![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423233354.png)



Mathematica code:


```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  y^2 == 24 x,
  {x, -8, 18}, {y, -15, 15},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-6, -15}, {-6, 15}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{6, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{6, 12}, {6, -12}}],
    Black,
    Text["Focus (6,0)", {6, 0}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(6,12)", {6, 12}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(6,-12)", {6, -12}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["x = -6", {-6, 13}, {0, 0}]
  }
]
```

Python code:

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-15, 15, 400)
x = y**2 / 24

focus = (6, 0)
latus_rectum = [(6, 12), (6, -12)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$y^2=24x$")
ax.axvline(-6, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$x=-6$")

ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(6, 0, "Focus (6,0)", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(6, 12, "(6,12)", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(6, -12, "(6,-12)", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-8, 18)
ax.set_ylim(-15, 15)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++



The standard form that applies to the given equation is $y^2=4 p x$. Thus, the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis. It follows that:
- $24=4 p$, so $p=6$. Since $p>0$, the parabola opens right
- the coordinates of the focus are $(p, 0)=(6,0)$
- the equation of the directrix is $x=-p=-6$
- the endpoints of the latus rectum have the same $x$-coordinate at the focus. To find the endpoints, substitute $x=6$ into the original equation: $(6, \pm 12)$

Next we plot the focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola (Figure 12.3.7).



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412010556.png)
> 
> 




> [!exercise]
> Graph $y^2=-16 x$. Identify and label the focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.




##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
y^2=-16x.
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

Therefore,

$$
4p=-16.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=-16\\
p&=\frac{-16}{4}\\
&=-4.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $p<0$, the parabola opens to the left.

For a parabola in the form

$$
y^2=4px,
$$

the focus is

$$
(p,0).
$$

Substitute $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(p,0)
&=(-4,0).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(-4,0).
$$

The directrix is

$$
x=-p.
$$

Substitute $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x&=-(-4)\\
&=4.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
x=4.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(p,\pm 2p).
$$

Substitute $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(p,\pm 2p)
&=(-4,\pm 2(-4))\\
&=(-4,\pm (-8)).
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
\pm (-8)=\pm 8,
$$

the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(-4,8)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-4,-8).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the focus, the directrix, and the endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening to the left, symmetric about the $x$-axis.



![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423234054.png)



**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  y^2 == -16 x,
  {x, -12, 6}, {y, -10, 10},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{4, -10}, {4, 10}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{-4, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-4, 8}, {-4, -8}}],
    Black,
    Text["Focus (-4,0)", {-4, 0}, {-1.2, 0}],
    Text["(-4,8)", {-4, 8}, {-1.2, 0}],
    Text["(-4,-8)", {-4, -8}, {-1.2, 0}],
    Text["x = 4", {4, 9}, {0, 0}]
  }
]
```

**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-10, 10, 400)
x = -(y**2) / 16

focus = (-4, 0)
latus_rectum = [(-4, 8), (-4, -8)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$y^2=-16x$")
ax.axvline(4, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$x=4$")

ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-4, 0, "Focus (-4,0)", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-4, 8, "(-4,8)", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-4, -8, "(-4,-8)", ha="right", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-12, 6)
ax.set_ylim(-10, 10)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++



> [!exercise]
> Graph $x^2=-6 y$. Identify and label the focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.




##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
x^2=-6y.
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

Therefore,

$$
4p=-6.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=-6\\
p&=\frac{-6}{4}\\
&=-\frac{3}{2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $p<0$, the parabola opens downward.

For a parabola in the form

$$
x^2=4py,
$$

the focus is

$$
(0,p).
$$

Substitute $p=-\frac{3}{2}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(0,p)
&=\left(0,-\frac{3}{2}\right).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
\left(0,-\frac{3}{2}\right).
$$

The directrix is

$$
y=-p.
$$

Substitute $p=-\frac{3}{2}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=-\left(-\frac{3}{2}\right)\\
&=\frac{3}{2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
y=\frac{3}{2}.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(\pm 2p,p).
$$

Substitute $p=-\frac{3}{2}$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(\pm 2p,p)
&=\left(\pm 2\left(-\frac{3}{2}\right),-\frac{3}{2}\right)\\
&=\left(\pm (-3),-\frac{3}{2}\right).
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
\pm (-3)=\pm 3,
$$

the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
\left(3,-\frac{3}{2}\right)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\left(-3,-\frac{3}{2}\right).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the focus, the directrix, and the endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening downward, symmetric about the $y$-axis.



![|center|500](Pasted%20image%2020260423234330.png)



**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 == -6 y,
  {x, -5, 5}, {y, -8, 4},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-5, 3/2}, {5, 3/2}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{0, -3/2}}],
    Blue, Point[{{3, -3/2}, {-3, -3/2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Focus (0,-3/2)", {0, -3/2}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(3,-3/2)", {3, -3/2}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["(-3,-3/2)", {-3, -3/2}, {0, -1.2}],
    Text["y = 3/2", {3.5, 3/2}, {0, 1.2}]
  }
]
```




**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-5, 5, 400)
y = -(x**2) / 6

focus = (0, -3/2)
latus_rectum = [(3, -3/2), (-3, -3/2)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$x^2=-6y$")
ax.axhline(3/2, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$y=\frac{3}{2}$")

ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0, -3/2, r"Focus $(0,-\frac{3}{2})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(3, -3/2, r"$(3,-\frac{3}{2})$", ha="center", va="top")
ax.text(-3, -3/2, r"$(-3,-\frac{3}{2})$", ha="center", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-5, 5)
ax.set_ylim(-8, 4)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```




++++


The standard form that applies to the given equation is $x^2=4 p y$. Thus, the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis. It follows that:
- $-6=4 p$,so $p=-\frac{3}{2}$. Since $p<0$, the parabola opens down.
- the coordinates of the focus are $(0, p)=\left(0,-\frac{3}{2}\right)$
- the equation of the directrix is $y=-p=\frac{3}{2}$
- the endpoints of the latus rectum can be found by substituting $y=\frac{3}{2}$ into the original equation, $\left( \pm 3,-\frac{3}{2}\right)$

Next we plot the focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola.



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412010734.png)
> 
> 
> 




> [!exercise]
> Graph $x^2=8 y$. Identify and label the focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.



##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
x^2=8y.
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
x^2=4py,
$$

so

$$
4p=8,
$$

and therefore

$$
p=2.
$$

Since $p>0$, the parabola opens upward.

For a parabola in the form

$$
x^2=4py,
$$

the focus is

$$
(0,p).
$$

Substituting $p=2$, we obtain

$$
(0,p)=(0,2).
$$

So the focus is

$$
(0,2).
$$

The directrix is

$$
y=-p.
$$

Substituting $p=2$, we get

$$
y=-2.
$$

So the directrix is

$$
y=-2.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(\pm 2p,p).
$$

Substituting $p=2$, we obtain

$$
(\pm 2p,p)=(\pm 4,2).
$$

To verify this directly, substitute $y=2$ into the equation:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2&=8y\\
&=8(2)\\
&=16,
\end{aligned}
$$

so

$$
x=\pm 4.
$$

Hence the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(-4,2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4,2).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the focus, the directrix, and the endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening upward, symmetric about the $y$-axis.



![|center|500](Pasted%20image%20202604240001-x2-equals-8y.png)



**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 == 8 y,
  {x, -6, 6}, {y, -3, 8},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-6, -2}, {6, -2}}]},
    Red, PointSize[0.02],
    Point[{{0, 2}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-4, 2}, {4, 2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Focus (0,2)", {0, 2}, {1.2, 0}],
    Text["(-4,2)", {-4, 2}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["(4,2)", {4, 2}, {0, 1.2}],
    Text["y = -2", {4.2, -2}, {0, -1.2}]
  }
]
```




**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-6, 6, 400)
y = (x**2) / 8

focus = (0, 2)
latus_rectum = [(-4, 2), (4, 2)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$x^2=8y$")
ax.axhline(-2, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$y=-2$")

ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0, 2, r"Focus $(0,2)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-4, 2, r"$(-4,2)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(4, 2, r"$(4,2)$", ha="left", va="bottom")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-6, 6)
ax.set_ylim(-3, 8)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++



## 1.4.1 Writing Equations of Parabolas in Standard Form



> [!review]
> Given the focus and directrix of a parabola with vertex at the origin, how can the equation of the parabola in standard form be obtained?



##### problem 1

To obtain the equation, first determine whether the axis of symmetry is horizontal or vertical. If the focus has the form $(p,0)$ and the directrix is the vertical line $x=-p$, then the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis and the standard form is

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

If the focus has the form $(0,p)$ and the directrix is the horizontal line $y=-p$, then the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis and the standard form is

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

After identifying the correct form, read off the value of $p$ from the focus or directrix, compute $4p$, and substitute it into the appropriate standard equation. The sign of $p$ determines the direction in which the parabola opens: positive $p$ means it opens right or up, while negative $p$ means it opens left or down.


+++++


In the previous examples, we used the standard form equation of a parabola to calculate the locations of its key features. We can also use the calculations in reverse to write an equation for a parabola when given its key features.

> [!NOTE] How to: Given its focus and directrix, write the equation for a parabola in standard form
> 
> 1. Determine whether the axis of symmetry is the $x$ - or $y$-axis.
> i. If the given coordinates of the focus have the form ( $p, 0$ ), then the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis. Use the standard form $y^2=4 p x$.
> ii. If the given coordinates of the focus have the form $(0, p)$, then the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis. Use the standard form $x^2=4 p y$.
> 2. Multiply $4 p$.
> 3. Substitute the value from Step 2 into the equation determined in Step 1.




> [!exercise] Example 12.3.3: Writing the Equation of a Parabola in Standard Form Given its Focus and Directrix
> 
> What is the equation for the parabola with focus $\left(-\frac{1}{2}, 0\right)$ and directrix $x=\frac{1}{2}$ ?



##### solution

The focus has the form $(p,0)$, so the axis of symmetry is the $x$-axis. Therefore the standard form of the parabola is

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

Since the focus is

$$
\left(-\frac{1}{2},0\right),
$$

we have

$$
p=-\frac{1}{2}.
$$

This is consistent with the directrix, because for a parabola with vertex at the origin and axis along the $x$-axis, the directrix is

$$
x=-p.
$$

Substituting $p=-\frac{1}{2}$ gives

$$
x=-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)=\frac{1}{2},
$$

which matches the given directrix.

Now compute $4p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p
&=4\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)\\
&=-2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Substitute this into the standard form:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y^2
&=4px\\
&=-2x.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, the equation of the parabola is

$$
y^2=-2x.
$$


++++

The focus has the form $(p, 0)$, so the equation will have the form $y^2=4 p x$.
- Multiplying $4 p$, we have $4 p=4\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)=-2$.
- Substituting for $4 p$, we have $y^2=4 p x=-2 x$. $=$

Therefore, the equation for the parabola is $y^2=-2 x$.




> [!exercise]
> What is the equation for the parabola with focus $\left(0, \frac{7}{2}\right)$ and directrix $y=-\frac{7}{2}$ ?



##### solution

The focus has the form $(0,p)$, so the axis of symmetry is the $y$-axis. Therefore the standard form of the parabola is

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

Since the focus is

$$
\left(0,\frac{7}{2}\right),
$$

we have

$$
p=\frac{7}{2}.
$$

This is consistent with the directrix, because for a parabola with vertex at the origin and axis along the $y$-axis, the directrix is

$$
y=-p.
$$

Substituting $p=\frac{7}{2}$ gives

$$
y=-\frac{7}{2},
$$

which matches the given directrix.

Now compute $4p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p
&=4\left(\frac{7}{2}\right)\\
&=2 \cdot 7\\
&=14.
\end{aligned}
$$

Substitute this into the standard form:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2
&=4py\\
&=14y.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, the equation of the parabola is

$$
x^2=14y.
$$



++++


## 1.4.2 Graphing Parabolas with Vertices Not at the Origin



> [!review]
> 1. What are the two standard forms of the equation of a parabola with vertex at a point $(h, k)$ , and how does the sign of $p$ in each form determine the direction in which the parabola opens?
> 2. Given a parabola with vertex at $(h, k)$ in standard form, how are the axis of symmetry, focus, equation of the directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum obtained from the equation?


##### problem 1

The two standard forms of the equation of a parabola with vertex at $(h,k)$ are

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h)
$$

and

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

In the first form, the axis of symmetry is horizontal, so the parabola opens to the right if $p>0$ and to the left if $p<0$. In the second form, the axis of symmetry is vertical, so the parabola opens upward if $p>0$ and downward if $p<0$.

##### problem 2

If the equation is written in the form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h),
$$

then the vertex is $(h,k)$, the axis of symmetry is $y=k$, the focus is $(h+p,k)$, the directrix is

$$
x=h-p,
$$

and the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h+p,k\pm 2p).
$$

If the equation is written in the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k),
$$

then the vertex is $(h,k)$, the axis of symmetry is $x=h$, the focus is $(h,k+p)$, the directrix is

$$
y=k-p,
$$

and the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h\pm 2p,k+p).
$$


+++++

Like other graphs we've worked with, the graph of a parabola can be translated. If a parabola is translated $h$ units horizontally and $k$ units vertically, the vertex will be $(h, k)$. This translation results in the standard form of the equation we saw previously with $x$ replaced by $(x-h)$ and $y$ replaced by $(y-k)$.

To graph parabolas with a vertex $(h, k)$ other than the origin, we use the standard form $(y-k)^2=4 p(x-h)$ for parabolas that have an axis of symmetry parallel to the $x$-axis, and $(x-h)^2=4 p(y-k)$ for parabolas that have an axis of symmetry parallel to the $y$-axis. These standard forms are given below, along with their general graphs and key features.





> [!NOTE] STANDARD FORMS OF PARABOLAS WITH VERTEX $(H, K)$
> 
> Table 12.3.2 and Figure 12.3.11 summarize the standard features of parabolas with a vertex at a point $(h, k)$.
> 
>
>
> | Axis of Symmetry |      Equation      |   Focus    | Directrix | Endpoints of Latus Rectum |
> | :--------------: | :----------------: | :--------: | :-------: | :-----------------------: |
> |      $y=k$       | $(y-k)^2=4 p(x-h)$ | $(h+p, k)$ |  $x=h-p$  |    $(h+p, k \pm 2 p)$     |
> |      $x=h$       | $(x-h)^2=4 p(y-k)$ | $(h, k+p)$ |  $y=k-p$  |    $(h \pm 2 p, k+p)$     |
> 
> 
> 
> ![600](Pasted%20image%2020260412011304.png)
> Figure 12.3.11: (a) When $p>0$, the parabola opens right. (b) When $p<0$, the parabola opens left. (c) When $p>0$, the parabola opens up. (d) When $p<0$, the parabola opens down.





> [!NOTE] How to: Given a standard form equation for a parabola centered at $(h, k)$, sketch the graph
> 
> 1. Determine which of the standard forms applies to the given equation: $(y-k)^2=4 p(x-h)$ or $(x-h)^2=4 p(y-k)$.
> 2. Use the standard form identified in Step 1 to determine the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, equation of the directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.
> - If the equation is in the form $(y-k)^2=4 p(x-h)$,then:
> 	- use the given equation to identify $h$ and $k$ for the vertex, $(h, k)$
> 	- use the value of $k$ to determine the axis of symmetry, $y=k$
> 	- set $4 p$ equal to the coefficient of $(x-h)$ in the given equation to solve for $p$. If $p>0$,the parabola opens right. If $p<0$, the parabola opens left.
> 	- use $h, k$, and $p$ to find the coordinates of the focus, $(h+p, k)$
> 	- use $h$ andp p to find the equation of the directrix, $x=h-p$
> 	- use $h, k$, and $p$ to find the endpoints of the latus rectum, $(h+p, k \pm 2 p)$
> - If the equation is in the form $(x-h)^2=4 p(y-k)$,then:
> 	- use the given equation to identify $h$ and $k$ for the vertex, $(h, k)$
> 	- use the value of $h$ to determine the axis of symmetry, $x=h$
> 	- set $4 p$ equal to the coefficient of $(y-k)$ in the given equation to solve for $p$. If $p>0$, the parabola opens up. If $p<0$, the parabola opens down.
> 	- use $h, k$, and $p$ to find the coordinates of the focus, $(h, k+p)$
> 	- use $k$ and $p$ to find the equation of the directrix, $y=k-p$
> 	- use $h, k$, and $p$ to find the endpoints of the latus rectum, $(h \pm 2 p, k+p)$
> 1. Plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola.





> [!exercise] Example: Graphing a Parabola with Vertex ( $h, k$ ) and Axis of Symmetry Parallel to the $x$-axis
> 
> Graph $(y-1)^2=-16(x+3)$. Identify and label the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.


##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
(y-1)^2=-16(x+3).
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h).
$$

Comparing the two equations, we obtain

$$
h=-3,
\qquad
k=1,
\qquad
4p=-16.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=-16\\
p&=\frac{-16}{4}\\
&=-4.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(h,k)=(-3,1).
$$

Since this parabola is in the form $(y-k)^2=4p(x-h)$, its axis of symmetry is the horizontal line

$$
y=k.
$$

Substituting $k=1$, we get

$$
y=1.
$$

Because $p<0$, the parabola opens to the left.

For a parabola in the form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h+p,k).
$$

Substitute $h=-3$, $k=1$, and $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h+p,k)
&=(-3+(-4),1)\\
&=(-7,1).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(-7,1).
$$

The directrix is

$$
x=h-p.
$$

Substitute $h=-3$ and $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=-3-(-4)\\
&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
x=1.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h+p,k\pm 2p).
$$

Substitute $h=-3$, $k=1$, and $p=-4$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h+p,k\pm 2p)
&=(-3+(-4),1\pm 2(-4))\\
&=(-7,1\pm (-8))\\
&=(-7,1-8)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-7,1+8).
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(-7,-7)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-7,9).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening to the left, symmetric about the line $y=1$.



> [!figure]
> 
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240028-ym1sq-eq-neg16xp3.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (y - 1)^2 == -16 (x + 3),
  {x, -14, 4}, {y, -10, 12},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{1, -10}, {1, 12}}]},
    {LightGray, Dotted, Line[{{-14, 1}, {4, 1}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-3, 1}}],
    Red, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-7, 1}}],
    Blue, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-7, -7}, {-7, 9}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (-3,1)", {-3, 1}, {1.3, 0}],
    Text["Focus (-7,1)", {-7, 1}, {-1.3, 0}],
    Text["(-7,9)", {-7, 9}, {-0.6, 1.2}],
    Text["(-7,-7)", {-7, -7}, {-0.6, -1.2}],
    Text["x = 1", {1, 10.5}, {1.2, 0}]
  }
]
```




**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-10, 12, 600)
x = -((y - 1) ** 2) / 16 - 3

vertex = (-3, 1)
focus = (-7, 1)
latus_rectum = [(-7, -7), (-7, 9)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$(y-1)^2=-16(x+3)$")
ax.axvline(1, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$x=1$")
ax.axhline(1, color="lightgray", linestyle=":")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-3, 1, r"Vertex $(-3,1)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-7, 1, r"Focus $(-7,1)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-7, 9, r"$(-7,9)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-7, -7, r"$(-7,-7)$", ha="right", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-14, 4)
ax.set_ylim(-10, 12)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++


The standard form that applies to the given equation is $(y-k)^2=4 p(x-h)$. Thus, the axis of symmetry is parallel to the $x$-axis. It follows that:
- the vertex is $(h, k)=(-3,1)$
- the axis of symmetry is $y=k=1$
- $-16=4 p$, so $p=-4$. Since $p<0$, the parabola opens left.
- the coordinates of the focus are $(h+p, k)=(-3+(-4), 1)=(-7,1)$
- the equation of the directrix is $x=h-p=-3-(-4)=1$
- the endpoints of the latus rectum are $(h+p, k \pm 2 p)=(-3+(-4), 1 \pm 2(-4))$, or $(-7,-7)$ and $(-7,9)$

Next we plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola (Figure 12.3.10).


> [!figure]
> 
> ![300](Pasted%20image%2020260412011606.png)
> 
> 





> [!exercise]
> Graph $(y+1)^2=4(x-8)$. Identify and label the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.


##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
(y+1)^2=4(x-8).
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h).
$$

Comparing the two equations, we obtain

$$
h=8,
\qquad
k=-1,
\qquad
4p=4.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=4\\
p&=\frac{4}{4}\\
&=1.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(h,k)=(8,-1).
$$

Since this parabola is in the form $(y-k)^2=4p(x-h)$, its axis of symmetry is the horizontal line

$$
y=k.
$$

Substituting $k=-1$, we get

$$
y=-1.
$$

Because $p>0$, the parabola opens to the right.

For a parabola in the form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h+p,k).
$$

Substitute $h=8$, $k=-1$, and $p=1$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h+p,k)
&=(8+1,-1)\\
&=(9,-1).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(9,-1).
$$

The directrix is

$$
x=h-p.
$$

Substitute $h=8$ and $p=1$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=8-1\\
&=7.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
x=7.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h+p,k\pm 2p).
$$

Substitute $h=8$, $k=-1$, and $p=1$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h+p,k\pm 2p)
&=(8+1,-1\pm 2(1))\\
&=(9,-1\pm 2)\\
&=(9,-1-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(9,-1+2).
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(9,-3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(9,1).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening to the right, symmetric about the line $y=-1$.



> [!figure]
> 
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240036-yp1sq-eq-4xminus8.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (y + 1)^2 == 4 (x - 8),
  {x, 5, 16}, {y, -5, 3},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{7, -5}, {7, 3}}]},
    {LightGray, Dotted, Line[{{5, -1}, {16, -1}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{8, -1}}],
    Red, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{9, -1}}],
    Blue, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{9, 1}, {9, -3}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (8,-1)", {8, -1}, {1.3, 0}],
    Text["Focus (9,-1)", {9, -1}, {1.3, -1.1}],
    Text["(9,1)", {9, 1}, {1.1, 1.0}],
    Text["(9,-3)", {9, -3}, {1.1, -1.0}],
    Text["x = 7", {7, 2.3}, {1.2, 0}]
  }
]
```




**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-5, 3, 600)
x = ((y + 1) ** 2) / 4 + 8

vertex = (8, -1)
focus = (9, -1)
latus_rectum = [(9, 1), (9, -3)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$(y+1)^2=4(x-8)$")
ax.axvline(7, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$x=7$")
ax.axhline(-1, color="lightgray", linestyle=":")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(8, -1, r"Vertex $(8,-1)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(9, -1, r"Focus $(9,-1)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(9, 1, r"$(9,1)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(9, -3, r"$(9,-3)$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(5, 16)
ax.set_ylim(-5, 3)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++


> [!exercise]
> Example 12.3.5: Graphing a Parabola from an Equation Given in General Form
> Graph $x^2-8 x-28 y-208=0$. Identify and label the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.



##### solution

We begin by rewriting the equation in standard form. Since the equation contains an $x^2$-term but no $y^2$-term, we expect the parabola to have axis of symmetry parallel to the $y$-axis. So we aim for the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

Start with the given equation:

$$
x^2-8x-28y-208=0.
$$

Move the $y$-terms and constant to the right-hand side:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-8x-28y-208&=0\\
x^2-8x&=28y+208.
\end{aligned}
$$

Complete the square in $x$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-8x+16&=28y+208+16\\
(x-4)^2&=28y+224\\
(x-4)^2&=28(y+8)\\
(x-4)^2&=4\cdot 7\cdot (y+8).
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the standard form is

$$
(x-4)^2=28(y+8),
$$

so

$$
h=4,
\qquad
k=-8,
\qquad
4p=28,
\qquad
p=7.
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(h,k)=(4,-8).
$$

Since this parabola is in the form $(x-h)^2=4p(y-k)$, its axis of symmetry is the vertical line

$$
x=h.
$$

Substituting $h=4$, we get

$$
x=4.
$$

Because $p>0$, the parabola opens upward.

For a parabola in the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h,k+p).
$$

Substitute $h=4$, $k=-8$, and $p=7$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k+p)
&=(4,-8+7)\\
&=(4,-1).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(4,-1).
$$

The directrix is

$$
y=k-p.
$$

Substitute $k=-8$ and $p=7$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=-8-7\\
&=-15.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
y=-15.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h\pm 2p,k+p).
$$

Substitute $h=4$, $k=-8$, and $p=7$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm 2p,k+p)
&=(4\pm 2(7),-8+7)\\
&=(4\pm 14,-1)\\
&=(4-14,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4+14,-1).
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(-10,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(18,-1).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening upward, symmetric about the line $x=4$.



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240043-xsq-8x-28y-208.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 - 8 x - 28 y - 208 == 0,
  {x, -12, 20}, {y, -17, 12},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-12, -15}, {20, -15}}]},
    {LightGray, Dotted, Line[{{4, -17}, {4, 12}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{4, -8}}],
    Red, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{4, -1}}],
    Blue, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-10, -1}, {18, -1}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (4,-8)", {4, -8}, {1.4, -1.0}],
    Text["Focus (4,-1)", {4, -1}, {1.4, 0}],
    Text["(-10,-1)", {-10, -1}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(18,-1)", {18, -1}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["y = -15", {14, -15}, {0, 1.0}]
  }
]
```




**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-12, 20, 800)
y = ((x - 4) ** 2) / 28 - 8

vertex = (4, -8)
focus = (4, -1)
latus_rectum = [(-10, -1), (18, -1)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$x^2-8x-28y-208=0$")
ax.axhline(-15, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$y=-15$")
ax.axvline(4, color="lightgray", linestyle=":")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(4, -8, r"Vertex $(4,-8)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(4, -1, r"Focus $(4,-1)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-10, -1, r"$(-10,-1)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(18, -1, r"$(18,-1)$", ha="left", va="bottom")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-12, 20)
ax.set_ylim(-17, 12)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++


Start by writing the equation of the parabola in standard form. The standard form that applies to the given equation is $(x-h)^2=4 p(y-k)$. Thus, the axis of symmetry is parallel to the $y$-axis. To express the equation of the parabola in this form, we begin by isolating the terms that contain the variable $x$ in order to complete the square.

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-8 x-28 y-208 & =0 \\
x^2-8 x & =28 y+208 \\
x^2-8 x+16 & =28 y+208+16 \\
(x-4)^2 & =28 y+224 \\
(x-4)^2 & =28(y+8) \\
(x-4)^2 & =4 \cdot 7 \cdot(y+8)
\end{aligned}
$$


It follows that:
- the vertex is $(h, k)=(4,-8)$
- the axis of symmetry is $x=h=4$
- since $p=7, p>0$ and so the parabola opens up
- the coordinates of the focus are $(h, k+p)=(4,-8+7)=(4,-1)$
- the equation of the directrix is $y=k-p=-8-7=-15$
- the endpoints of the latus rectum are $(h \pm 2 p, k+p)=(4 \pm 2(7),-8+7)$, or $(-10,-1)$ and $(18,-1)$

Next we plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and latus rectum, and draw a smooth curve to form the parabola (Figure 12.3.14).



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412011744.png)
> 
> 



++++



> [!exercise]
> Graph $(x+2)^2=-20(y-3)$. Identify and label the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum.



##### solution

We are given the parabola

$$
(x+2)^2=-20(y-3).
$$

This is in the standard form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

Comparing the two equations, we obtain

$$
h=-2,
\qquad
k=3,
\qquad
4p=-20.
$$

Now solve for $p$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4p&=-20\\
p&=\frac{-20}{4}\\
&=-5.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(h,k)=(-2,3).
$$

Since this parabola is in the form $(x-h)^2=4p(y-k)$, its axis of symmetry is the vertical line

$$
x=h.
$$

Substituting $h=-2$, we get

$$
x=-2.
$$

Because $p<0$, the parabola opens downward.

For a parabola in the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h,k+p).
$$

Substitute $h=-2$, $k=3$, and $p=-5$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h,k+p)
&=(-2,3+(-5))\\
&=(-2,-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

So the focus is

$$
(-2,-2).
$$

The directrix is

$$
y=k-p.
$$

Substitute $k=3$ and $p=-5$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=3-(-5)\\
&=8.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the directrix is

$$
y=8.
$$

The endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(h\pm 2p,k+p).
$$

Substitute $h=-2$, $k=3$, and $p=-5$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(h\pm 2p,k+p)
&=(-2\pm 2(-5),3+(-5))\\
&=(-2\pm (-10),-2)\\
&=(-2-10,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-2+10,-2).
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the endpoints of the latus rectum are

$$
(-12,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(8,-2).
$$

To graph the parabola, plot the vertex, axis of symmetry, focus, directrix, and endpoints of the latus rectum. Then draw a smooth curve opening downward, symmetric about the line $x=-2$.



> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240047-xp2sq-neg20yminus3.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica

  (x + 2)^2 == -20 (y - 3),
  {x, -14, 10}, {y, -10, 10},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-14, 8}, {10, 8}}]},
    {LightGray, Dotted, Line[{{-2, -10}, {-2, 10}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-2, 3}}],
    Red, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-2, -2}}],
    Blue, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-12, -2}, {8, -2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (-2,3)", {-2, 3}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (-2,-2)", {-2, -2}, {1.3, -1.0}],
    Text["(-12,-2)", {-12, -2}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(8,-2)", {8, -2}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["y = 8", {6, 8}, {0, 1.0}]
  }
]
```

**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-14, 10, 800)
y = 3 - ((x + 2) ** 2) / 20

vertex = (-2, 3)
focus = (-2, -2)
latus_rectum = [(-12, -2), (8, -2)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, label=r"$(x+2)^2=-20(y-3)$")
ax.axhline(8, color="gray", linestyle="--", label=r"$y=8$")
ax.axvline(-2, color="lightgray", linestyle=":")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*latus_rectum), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-2, 3, r"Vertex $(-2,3)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, -2, r"Focus $(-2,-2)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(-12, -2, r"$(-12,-2)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(8, -2, r"$(8,-2)$", ha="left", va="bottom")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.set_xlim(-14, 10)
ax.set_ylim(-10, 10)
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++




# 1.5 Rotation of Axes



## 1.5.1 Identifying Nondegenerate Conics in General Form



> [!review]
> 1. What is the general form of the equation of a conic section, and what condition must its coefficients satisfy?
> 2. Given a conic in the form $A x^2+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ (with no $x y$ term), how do the values of $A$ and $C$ determine whether the equation represents a circle, an ellipse, a parabola, or a hyperbola?
> 3. What does a nonzero coefficient $B$ on the $x y$ term in $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+ E y+F=0$ indicate about the orientation of the conic relative to the coordinate axes?
> 4. What are the possible degenerate cases of a conic section, and how can they arise from an equation in general form?


##### problem 1

The general form of the equation of a conic section is

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

where the coefficients $A$, $B$, and $C$ are not all zero. This condition is necessary so that the equation actually contains at least one quadratic term.

##### problem 2

For an equation of the form

$$
Ax^2+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

with no $xy$ term, the values of $A$ and $C$ determine the type of conic as follows. If $A=C\neq 0$, the equation represents a circle. If $A$ and $C$ are nonzero, have the same sign, and are not equal, the equation represents an ellipse. If $A$ and $C$ are nonzero and have opposite signs, the equation represents a hyperbola. If exactly one of $A$ or $C$ is zero, the equation represents a parabola.

##### problem 3

A nonzero coefficient $B$ on the $xy$ term indicates that the conic is rotated relative to the coordinate axes. In other words, the axes of symmetry of the conic are no longer parallel to the $x$- and $y$-axes.

##### problem 4

The degenerate cases of conic sections include a point, two intersecting lines, and in some cases no real graph at all. These arise when the general-form equation factors or simplifies in such a way that the conic collapses. For example, an ellipse or circle can degenerate to a single point, a hyperbola can degenerate to two intersecting lines, and an equation may also have no real solutions, so that no graph appears in the plane.


+++++


In previous sections of this chapter, we have focused on the standard form equations for nondegenerate conic sections. In this section, we will shift our focus to the general form equation, which can be used for any conic. The general form is set equal to zero, and the terms and coefficients are given in a particular order, as shown below.

$$
A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0
$$

where $A, B$, and $C$ are not all zero. We can use the values of the coefficients to identify which type conic is represented by a given equation.
You may notice that the general form equation has an $x y$ term that we have not seen in any of the standard form equations. As we will discuss later, the $x y$ term rotates the conic whenever $B$ is not equal to zero.



> [!NOTE] GENERAL FORM OF CONIC SECTIONS
> 
> A conic section has the general form
> 
> $$
> A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0
> $$
> 
> where $A, B$, and $C$ are not all zero. Table 12.4.2 summarizes the different conic sections where $B=0$, and $A$ and $C$ are nonzero real numbers. This indicates that the conic has not been rotated.
> 
>
>
> | ellipse | $A x^2+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0, A \neq C$ and $A C>0$ |
> | :--- | :--- |
> | circle | $A x^2+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0, A=C$ |
> | hyperbola | $\begin{gathered} A x^2-C y^2+D x+E y+F=0 \text { or }-A x^2+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0, \text { where } A \text { and } C \\ \text { are positive } \end{gathered}$ |
> | parabola | $A x^2+D x+E y+F=0$ or $C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ |
> 
> 




> [!NOTE] How to: Given the equation of a conic, identify the type of conic
> 
> 1. Rewrite the equation in the general form (Equation 12.4.1), $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$
> 2. Identify the values of $A$ and $C$ from the general form.
> - If $A$ and $C$ are nonzero, have the same sign, and are not equal to each other, then the graph may be an ellipse.
> - If $A$ and $C$ are equal and nonzero and have the same sign, then the graph may be a circle.
> - If $A$ and $C$ are nonzero and have opposite signs, then the graph may be a hyperbola.
> - If either $A$ or $C$ is zero, then the graph may be a parabola.
> 
> If $B=0$, the conic section will have a vertical and/or horizontal axes. If $B$ does not equal 0 , as shown below, the conic section is rotated. Notice the phrase "may be" in the definitions. That is because the equation may not represent a conic section at all, depending on the values of $A, B, C, D, E$, and $F$. For example, the degenerate case of a circle or an ellipse is a point:
> 
> $$
> A x^2+B y^2=0
> $$
> 
> when $A$ and $B$ have the same sign.
> The degenerate case of a hyperbola is two intersecting straight lines: $A x^2+B y^2=0$, when $A$ and $B$ have opposite signs.
> On the other hand, the equation, $A x^2+B y^2+1=0$, when $A$ and $B$ are positive does not represent a graph at all, since there are no real ordered pairs which satisfy it.



> [!exercise]
> Example: Identifying a Conic from Its General Form
> 
> Identify the graph of each of the following nondegenerate conic sections.
> a. $4 x^2-9 y^2+36 x+36 y-125=0$
> b. $9 y^2+16 x+36 y-10=0$
> c. $3 x^2+3 y^2-2 x-6 y-4=0$
> d. $-25 x^2-4 y^2+100 x+16 y+20=0$
> e. $16 y^2-x^2+x-4 y-9=0$
> f. $16 x^2+4 y^2+16 x+49 y-81=0$
> 



##### solution

To identify each conic, we compare the coefficients of the squared terms in the general form

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0.
$$

Because none of these equations contains an $xy$-term, the conics are not rotated. We classify them from the values of $A$ and $C$.

For part (a),

$$
4x^2-9y^2+36x+36y-125=0,
$$

we have $A=4$ and $C=-9$. Since $A$ and $C$ have opposite signs, the graph is a hyperbola.

For part (b),

$$
9y^2+16x+36y-10=0,
$$

there is a $y^2$-term but no $x^2$-term. Thus exactly one of $A$ or $C$ is zero, so the graph is a parabola.

For part (c),

$$
3x^2+3y^2-2x-6y-4=0,
$$

we have $A=3$ and $C=3$. Since $A=C\neq 0$, the graph is a circle.

For part (d),

$$
-25x^2-4y^2+100x+16y+20=0,
$$

we have $A=-25$ and $C=-4$. These coefficients have the same sign and are not equal, so the graph is an ellipse.

For part (e),

$$
16y^2-x^2+x-4y-9=0,
$$

we have $A=-1$ and $C=16$. Since $A$ and $C$ have opposite signs, the graph is a hyperbola.

For part (f),

$$
16x^2+4y^2+16x+49y-81=0,
$$

we have $A=16$ and $C=4$. These coefficients have the same sign and are not equal, so the graph is an ellipse.

Therefore:

- (a) hyperbola
- (b) parabola
- (c) circle
- (d) ellipse
- (e) hyperbola
- (f) ellipse



++++



## 1.5.2 Finding a New Representation of the Given Equation after Rotating through a Given Angle



> [!review]
> 1. Geometrically, what is the effect of including a nonzero $x y$ term in the general-form equation of a conic section?
> 2. Suppose the $x$ - and $y$-axes are rotated through an angle $\theta$ about the origin to produce new axes, the $x^{\prime}$-axis and $y^{\prime}$-axis. How are the unit vectors $\hat{i}^{\prime}$ and $\hat{j}^{\prime}$ along the new axes expressed in terms of the original unit vectors $\hat{i}$ and $\hat{j}$ ?
> 3. Let the $x$ - and $y$-axes be rotated through an angle $\theta$ about the origin to produce new axes $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$. Derive the equations of rotation expressing the original coordinates ( $x, y$ ) of a point in terms of its coordinates $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$ with respect to the rotated axes.
> 4. Given the equation of a conic section in the coordinates $(x, y)$, what procedure produces its equation in the rotated coordinates $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$ obtained by rotating the axes through an angle $\theta$ ?



##### problem 1

Geometrically, a nonzero $xy$ term indicates that the conic is rotated relative to the original $x$- and $y$-axes. Its axes of symmetry are no longer parallel to the coordinate axes.

##### problem 2

If the axes are rotated through an angle $\theta$, then the unit vectors along the new axes are

$$
\hat{i}^{\prime}=\cos \theta\,\hat{i}+\sin \theta\,\hat{j}
$$

and

$$
\hat{j}^{\prime}=-\sin \theta\,\hat{i}+\cos \theta\,\hat{j}.
$$

##### problem 3

If a point has coordinates $\left(x^{\prime},y^{\prime}\right)$ in the rotated system, then its coordinates $(x,y)$ in the original system are given by the equations of rotation

$$
x=x^{\prime}\cos\theta-y^{\prime}\sin\theta
$$

and

$$
y=x^{\prime}\sin\theta+y^{\prime}\cos\theta.
$$

These follow by expressing the position vector in terms of the rotated basis vectors and then collecting the coefficients of $\hat{i}$ and $\hat{j}$.

##### problem 4

To obtain the equation in the rotated coordinates, first express $x$ and $y$ in terms of $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ using the equations of rotation. Then substitute those expressions into the original equation and simplify. Finally, rewrite the resulting equation in standard form in the variables $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$.


+++++


Until now, we have looked at equations of conic sections without an $x y$ term, which aligns the graphs with the $x$ - and $y$-axes. When we add an $x y$ term, we are rotating the conic about the origin. If the $x$ - and $y$-axes are rotated through an angle, say $\theta$, then every point on the plane may be thought of as having two representations: $(x, y)$ on the Cartesian plane with the original $x$-axis and $y$-axis, and $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$ on the new plane defined by the new, rotated axes, called the $x^{\prime}$-axis and $y^{\prime}$-axis (Figure 12.4.3).



> [!figure]
> 
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412013233.png)
> 
> The graph of the rotated ellipse x^2+y^2-x y-15=0



We will find the relationships between $x$ and $y$ on the Cartesian plane with $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ on the new rotated plane (Figure 12.4.4).


> [!figure]
> 
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412013350.png)
> The Cartesian plane with $x$ - and $y$-axes and the resulting $x^{\prime}$ - and $y^{\prime}$-axes formed by a rotation by an angle $\theta$.



The original coordinate $x$ - and $y$-axes have unit vectors $\hat{i}$ and $\hat{j}$. The rotated coordinate axes have unit vectors $\hat{i}^{\prime}$ and $\hat{j}^{\prime}$. The angle $\theta$ is known as the angle of rotation (Figure 12.4.5). We may write the new unit vectors in terms of the original ones.

$$
\begin{gathered}
\hat{i}^{\prime}=\cos \theta \hat{i}+\sin \theta \hat{j} \\
\hat{j}^{\prime}=-\sin \theta \hat{i}+\cos \theta \hat{j}
\end{gathered}
$$


> [!figure]
> 
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412013518.png)
> Relationship between the old and new coordinate planes.
> 




Consider a vector $\vec{u}$ in the new coordinate plane. It may be represented in terms of its coordinate axes.

$$
\begin{array}{rlr}
\vec{u} & =x^{\prime} i^{\prime}+y^{\prime} j^{\prime} & \\
& =x^{\prime}(i \cos \theta+j \sin \theta)+y^{\prime}(-i \sin \theta+j \cos \theta) & \text { Substitute. } \\
& =i x^{\prime} \cos \theta+j x^{\prime} \sin \theta-i y^{\prime} \sin \theta+j y^{\prime} \cos \theta & \text { Distribute. } \\
& =i x^{\prime} \cos \theta-i y^{\prime} \sin \theta+j x^{\prime} \sin \theta+j y^{\prime} \cos \theta & \text { Apply commutative property. } \\
