& =\left(x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta\right) i+\left(x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta\right) j & \text { Factor by grouping. }
\end{array}
$$


Because $\vec{u}=x^{\prime} i^{\prime}+y^{\prime} j^{\prime}$, we have representations of $x$ and $y$ in terms of the new coordinate system.

$$
x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta
$$

and

$$
y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta
$$



> [!NOTE] EQUATIONS OF ROTATION
> 
> If a point ( $x, y$ ) on the Cartesian plane is represented on a new coordinate plane where the axes of rotation are formed by rotating an angle $\theta$ from the positive $x$-axis, then the coordinates of the point with respect to the new axes are $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$. We can use the following equations of rotation to define the relationship between $(x, y)$ and $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$ :
> 
> $$
> x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta
> $$
> 
> and
> 
> $$
> y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta
> $$



> [!NOTE] How to: Given the equation of a conic, find a new representation after rotating through an angle
> 
> 1. Find $x$ and $y$ where $x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta$ and $y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta$.
> 2. Substitute the expression for $x$ and $y$ into in the given equation, then simplify.
> 3. Write the equations with $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ in standard form.



> [!exercise] Example: Finding a New Representation of an Equation after Rotating through a Given Angle
> 
> Find a new representation of the equation $2 x^2-x y+2 y^2-30=0$ after rotating through an angle of $\theta=45^{\circ}$.




##### solution

We are asked to rewrite

$$
2x^2-xy+2y^2-30=0
$$

in the rotated coordinate system obtained by rotating the axes through

$$
\theta=45^\circ.
$$

For a rotation through an angle $\theta$, the equations of rotation are

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta.
$$

Since

$$
\cos 45^\circ=\sin 45^\circ=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}},
$$

we get

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=x'\cos 45^\circ-y'\sin 45^\circ\\
&=x'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)-y'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\\
&=\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt{2}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=x'\sin 45^\circ+y'\cos 45^\circ\\
&=x'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)+y'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\\
&=\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt{2}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now substitute these into the original equation:

$$
2\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)+2\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-30=0.
$$

Simplify step by step:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)+2\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-30&=0\\
2\left(\frac{(x'-y')^2}{2}\right)-\frac{(x'-y')(x'+y')}{2}+2\left(\frac{(x'+y')^2}{2}\right)-30&=0\\
(x'-y')^2-\frac{(x'-y')(x'+y')}{2}+(x'+y')^2-30&=0\\
\left(x'^2-2x'y'+y'^2\right)-\frac{x'^2-y'^2}{2}+\left(x'^2+2x'y'+y'^2\right)-30&=0\\
2x'^2+2y'^2-\frac{x'^2-y'^2}{2}-30&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Multiply both sides by $2$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2\left(2x'^2+2y'^2-\frac{x'^2-y'^2}{2}-30\right)&=2(0)\\
4x'^2+4y'^2-(x'^2-y'^2)-60&=0\\
4x'^2+4y'^2-x'^2+y'^2-60&=0\\
3x'^2+5y'^2-60&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Move the constant term to the right:

$$
3x'^2+5y'^2=60.
$$

Divide by $60$:

$$
\frac{3x'^2}{60}+\frac{5y'^2}{60}=1.
$$

Simplify the fractions:

$$
\frac{x'^2}{20}+\frac{y'^2}{12}=1.
$$

Therefore, the new representation of the equation in the rotated coordinates is

$$
\frac{x'^2}{20}+\frac{y'^2}{12}=1,
$$

which is the equation of an ellipse in standard form.



++++


Find $x$ and $y$, where $x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta$ and $y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta$.
Because $\theta=45^{\circ}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& x=x^{\prime} \cos \left(45^{\circ}\right)-y^{\prime} \sin \left(45^{\circ}\right) \\
& x=x^{\prime}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)-y^{\prime}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right) \\
& x=\frac{x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
& y=x^{\prime} \sin \left(45^{\circ}\right)+y^{\prime} \cos \left(45^{\circ}\right) \\
& y=x^{\prime}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)+y^{\prime}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right) \\
& y=\frac{x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$


Substitute $x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta$ and $y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta$ into $2 x^2-x y+2 y^2-30=0$.

$$
2\left(\frac{x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-\left(\frac{x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}\right)\left(\frac{x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}\right)+2\left(\frac{x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2-30=0
$$


Simplify.

$$
\begin{aligned}
2 \frac{\left(x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)\left(x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)}{2}-\frac{\left(x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)\left(x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}\right)}{2}+2 \frac{\left(x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}\right)\left(x^{\prime}+y^{\prime}\right)}{2}-30=0 & \text { FOIL method } \\
x^{\prime 2}-2 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+y^{\prime 2}-\frac{\left(x^{\prime 2}-y^{\prime 2}\right)}{2}+x^{\prime 2}+2 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+y^{\prime 2}-30=0 & \text { Combine like terms. } \\
2 x^{\prime 2}+2 y^{\prime 2}-\frac{\left(x^{\prime 2}-y^{\prime 2}\right)}{2}=30 & \text { Combine like terms. } \\
2\left(2 x^{\prime 2}+2 y^{\prime 2}-\frac{\left(x^{\prime 2}-y^{\prime 2}\right)}{2}\right)=2(30) & \text { Multiply both sides by } 2 . \\
4 x^{\prime 2}+4 y^{\prime 2}-\left(x^{\prime 2}-y^{\prime 2}\right)=60 & \text { Simplify. } \\
4 x^{\prime 2}+4 y^{\prime 2}-x^{\prime 2}+y^{\prime 2} 2=60 & \text { Distribute. }
\end{aligned}
$$


$$
\frac{3 x^{\prime 2}}{60}+\frac{5 y^{\prime 2}}{60}=\frac{60}{60} \quad \text { Set equal to } 1
$$


Write the equations with $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ in the standard form.

$$
\frac{x^{\prime 2}}{20}+\frac{y^{\prime 2}}{12}=1
$$


This equation is an ellipse. Figure 12.4.6 shows the graph.


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412013838.png)
> 
> 




## 1.5.3 Writing Equations of Rotated Conics in Standard Form



> [!review]
> 1. Consider a conic section given by $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ with $B \neq$ 0 . Through what angle $\theta$ should the axes be rotated so that, in the new coordinates ( $x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}$ ), the resulting equation contains no $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term?
> 2. When eliminating the $x y$ term from a conic in general form by rotating the axes, how does the sign of $\cot (2 \theta)$ determine the interval in which the acute rotation angle $\theta$ lies, and what angle $\theta$ is used in the special case $A=C$ ?
> 3. Given the equation of a conic in the form $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ with $B \neq 0$, what procedure rewrites the equation in rotated coordinates ( $x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}$ ) so that no $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term appears?



##### problem 1

The axes should be rotated through an acute angle $\theta$ satisfying

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

This choice of $\theta$ eliminates the $x'y'$ term in the rotated coordinate system.

##### problem 2

The sign of $\cot(2\theta)$ determines where the acute angle $\theta$ lies. If

$$
\cot(2\theta)>0,
$$

then $2\theta$ lies in the first quadrant, so

$$
0^\circ<\theta<45^\circ.
$$

If

$$
\cot(2\theta)<0,
$$

then $2\theta$ lies in the second quadrant, so

$$
45^\circ<\theta<90^\circ.
$$

In the special case

$$
A=C,
$$

we have

$$
\cot(2\theta)=0,
$$

and the angle used is

$$
\theta=45^\circ.
$$

##### problem 3

To rewrite the equation without an $x'y'$ term, first compute $\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}$ and determine the rotation angle $\theta$. Next find $\sin\theta$ and $\cos\theta$. Then substitute

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta
$$

into the original equation. Simplify the result and write the new equation in standard form in the variables $x'$ and $y'$, where the $x'y'$ term has disappeared.


+++++


Now that we can find the standard form of a conic when we are given an angle of rotation, we will learn how to transform the equation of a conic given in the form $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ into standard form by rotating the axes. To do so, we will rewrite the general form as an equation in the $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ coordinate system without the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term, by rotating the axes by a measure of $\theta$ that satisfies

$$
\cot (2 \theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}
$$


We have learned already that any conic may be represented by the second degree equation

$$
A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0
$$

where $A$, $B$, and $C$ are not all zero. However, if $B \neq 0$, then we have an $x y$ term that prevents us from rewriting the equation in standard form. To eliminate it, we can rotate the axes by an acute angle $\theta$ where $\cot (2 \theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}$.
- If $\cot (2 \theta)>0$, then $2 \theta$ is in the first quadrant, and $\theta$ is between $\left(0^{\circ}, 45^{\circ}\right)$.
- If $\cot (2 \theta)<0$, then $2 \theta$ is in the second quadrant, and $\theta$ is between $\left(45^{\circ}, 90^{\circ}\right)$.
- If $A=C$, then $\theta=45^{\circ}$.



> [!NOTE] How to: Given an equation for a conic in the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ system, rewrite the equation without the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term in terms of $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$,where the $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ axes are rotations of the standard axes by $\theta$ degrees
> 
> 1. Find $\cot (2 \theta)$.
> 2. Find $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$.
> 3. Substitute $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$ into $x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta$ and $y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta$.
> 4. Substitute the expression for $x$ and $y$ into in the given equation, and then simplify.
> 5. Write the equations with $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ in the standard form with respect to the rotated axes.



> [!exercise] Example: Rewriting an Equation with respect to the $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ axes without the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ Term
> 
> Rewrite the equation $8 x^2-12 x y+17 y^2=20$ in the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ system without an $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term.



##### solution

We want to rewrite

$$
8x^2-12xy+17y^2=20
$$

in rotated coordinates so that the $x'y'$ term disappears.

For a conic

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

the rotation angle satisfies

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=8,
\qquad
B=-12,
\qquad
C=17.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{8-17}{-12}\\
&=\frac{-9}{-12}\\
&=\frac{3}{4}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus we may take a reference triangle for $2\theta$ with adjacent side $3$, opposite side $4$, and hypotenuse

$$
\begin{aligned}
h^2&=3^2+4^2\\
&=9+16\\
&=25,
\end{aligned}
$$

so

$$
h=5.
$$

Hence

$$
\cos(2\theta)=\frac{3}{5}.
$$

Since $\theta$ is acute, we use the positive square roots in the half-angle formulas:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sin\theta
&=\sqrt{\frac{1-\cos(2\theta)}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{1-\frac{3}{5}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{5}{5}-\frac{3}{5}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{2}{5}\cdot\frac{1}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{1}{5}}\\
&=\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cos\theta
&=\sqrt{\frac{1+\cos(2\theta)}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{1+\frac{3}{5}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{5}{5}+\frac{3}{5}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{8}{5}\cdot\frac{1}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{4}{5}}\\
&=\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now use the rotation formulas

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta.
$$

Substitute the values of $\sin\theta$ and $\cos\theta$:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=x'\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}\right)-y'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}}\right)\\
&=\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt{5}},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=x'\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}}\right)+y'\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}\right)\\
&=\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt{5}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Substitute these into the equation:

$$
8\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2-12\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)+17\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2=20.
$$

Simplify:

$$
\begin{aligned}
8\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2-12\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)+17\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2&=20\\
8\left(\frac{(2x'-y')^2}{5}\right)-12\left(\frac{(2x'-y')(x'+2y')}{5}\right)+17\left(\frac{(x'+2y')^2}{5}\right)&=20.
\end{aligned}
$$

Multiply both sides by $5$:

$$
8(2x'-y')^2-12(2x'-y')(x'+2y')+17(x'+2y')^2=100.
$$

Expand:

$$
\begin{aligned}
8(2x'-y')^2-12(2x'-y')(x'+2y')+17(x'+2y')^2
&=8(4x'^2-4x'y'+y'^2)\\
&\quad -12(2x'^2+3x'y'-2y'^2)\\
&\quad +17(x'^2+4x'y'+4y'^2)\\
&=32x'^2-32x'y'+8y'^2\\
&\quad -24x'^2-36x'y'+24y'^2\\
&\quad +17x'^2+68x'y'+68y'^2\\
&=25x'^2+100y'^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore

$$
25x'^2+100y'^2=100.
$$

Divide by $100$:

$$
\frac{25x'^2}{100}+\frac{100y'^2}{100}=1.
$$

So the equation in standard form is

$$
\frac{x'^2}{4}+y'^2=1,
$$

or equivalently

$$
\frac{x'^2}{4}+\frac{y'^2}{1}=1.
$$

Thus the rotated conic is an ellipse with no $x'y'$ term.


++++

First, we find $\cot (2 \theta)$.

$$
8 x^2-12 x y+17 y^2=20 \rightarrow A=8, B=-12 \text { and } C=17
$$


> [!figure]
> ![300](Pasted%20image%2020260412014124.png)
> 



From Figure 12.4.7:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot (2 \theta) & =\frac{A-C}{B}=\frac{8-17}{-12} \\
& =\frac{-9}{-12}=\frac{3}{4} \\
\cot (2 \theta) & =\frac{3}{4}=\frac{\text { adjacent }}{\text { opposite }}
\end{aligned}
$$


So the hypotenuse is

$$
\begin{aligned}
3^2+4^2 & =h^2 \\
9+16 & =h^2 \\
25 & =h^2 \\
h & =5
\end{aligned}
$$


Next, we find $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta=\sqrt{\frac{1-\cos (2 \theta)}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{1-\frac{3}{5}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{5}{5}-\frac{3}{5}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{5-3}{5} \cdot \frac{1}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{2}{10}}=\sqrt{\frac{1}{5}} \\
& \sin \theta=\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}} \\
& \cos \theta=\sqrt{\frac{1+\cos (2 \theta)}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{1+\frac{3}{5}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{5}{5}+\frac{3}{5}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{5+3}{5} \cdot \frac{1}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{8}{10}}=\sqrt{\frac{4}{5}} \\
& \cos \theta=\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}
\end{aligned}
$$


Substitute the values of $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$ into $x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta$ and $y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
x & =x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta \\
& =x^{\prime}\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}\right)-y^{\prime}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}}\right) \\
& =\frac{2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{5}}
\end{aligned}
$$



Substitute the expressions for $x$ and $y$ into in the given equation, and then simplify.

$$
\begin{aligned}
8\left(\frac{2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2-12\left(\frac{2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{5}}\right)\left(\frac{x^{\prime}+2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{5}}\right)+17\left(\frac{x^{\prime}+2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{5}}\right)^2 & =20 \\
8\left(\frac{\left(2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)\left(2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)}{5}\right)-12\left(\frac{\left(2 x^{\prime}-y^{\prime}\right)\left(x^{\prime}+2 y^{\prime}\right)}{5}\right)+17\left(\frac{\left(x^{\prime}+2 y^{\prime}\right)\left(x^{\prime}+2 y^{\prime}\right)}{5}\right) & =20 \\
8\left(4 x^{\prime 2}-4 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+y^{\prime 2}\right)-12\left(2 x^{\prime 2}+3 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime 2}\right)+17\left(x^{\prime 2}+4 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+4 y^{\prime 2}\right) & =100 \\
32 x^{\prime 2}-32 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+8 y^{\prime 2}-24 x^{\prime 2}-36 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+24 y^{\prime 2}+17 x^{\prime 2}+68 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+68 y^{\prime 2} & =100 \\
25 x^{\prime 2}+100 y^{\prime 2} & =100 \\
\frac{25}{100} x^{\prime 2}+\frac{100}{100} y^{\prime 2} & =\frac{100}{100}
\end{aligned}
$$


Write the equations with $x^{\prime}$ and $y^{\prime}$ in the standard form with respect to the new coordinate system.

$$
\frac{x^{\prime 2}}{4}+\frac{y^{\prime 2}}{1}=1
$$


Figure 12.4.8 shows the graph of the ellipse.




> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%2020260412014226.png)
> 



> [!exercise]
> Rewrite the $13 x^2-6 \sqrt{3} x y+7 y^2=16$ in the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ system without the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term.



##### solution

We want to rewrite

$$
13x^2-6\sqrt{3}\,xy+7y^2=16
$$

in rotated coordinates so that the $x'y'$ term disappears.

For a conic of the form

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

the rotation angle satisfies

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=13,
\qquad
B=-6\sqrt{3},
\qquad
C=7.
$$

Therefore

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{13-7}{-6\sqrt{3}}\\
&=\frac{6}{-6\sqrt{3}}\\
&=-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the acute rotation angle satisfies $45^\circ<\theta<90^\circ$ when $\cot(2\theta)<0$, and since

$$
\cot(120^\circ)=-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}},
$$

we take

$$
2\theta=120^\circ,
\qquad
\theta=60^\circ.
$$

Thus

$$
\cos\theta=\cos 60^\circ=\frac{1}{2}
$$

and

$$
\sin\theta=\sin 60^\circ=\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}.
$$

Now use the rotation formulas

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta.
$$

Substituting $\cos\theta=\frac12$ and $\sin\theta=\frac{\sqrt3}{2}$ gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=x'\left(\frac12\right)-y'\left(\frac{\sqrt3}{2}\right)\\
&=\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=x'\left(\frac{\sqrt3}{2}\right)+y'\left(\frac12\right)\\
&=\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Substitute these expressions into the equation:

$$
13\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)^2-6\sqrt3\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)+7\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)^2=16.
$$

Expand each term:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)^2
&=\frac{x'^2-2\sqrt3\,x'y'+3y'^2}{4},\\
\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)
&=\frac{\sqrt3\,x'^2-2x'y'-\sqrt3\,y'^2}{4},\\
\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)^2
&=\frac{3x'^2+2\sqrt3\,x'y'+y'^2}{4}.
\end{aligned}
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
13\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)^2-6\sqrt3\left(\frac{x'-\sqrt3\,y'}{2}\right)\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)+7\left(\frac{\sqrt3\,x'+y'}{2}\right)^2
&=\frac{13x'^2-26\sqrt3\,x'y'+39y'^2}{4}\\
&\quad +\frac{-18x'^2+12\sqrt3\,x'y'+18y'^2}{4}\\
&\quad +\frac{21x'^2+14\sqrt3\,x'y'+7y'^2}{4}\\
&=\frac{16x'^2+64y'^2}{4}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the rotated equation becomes

$$
\frac{16x'^2+64y'^2}{4}=16,
$$

which simplifies to

$$
16x'^2+64y'^2=64.
$$

Divide by $64$:

$$
\frac{16x'^2}{64}+\frac{64y'^2}{64}=1.
$$

Therefore the equation in the $x'y'$ system is

$$
\frac{x'^2}{4}+y'^2=1,
$$

or equivalently

$$
\frac{x'^2}{4}+\frac{y'^2}{1}=1.
$$

Thus the conic is an ellipse with no $x'y'$ term in the rotated coordinate system.


++++



> [!exercise] Example: Graphing an Equation That Has No $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ Terms
> 
> Graph the following equation relative to the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ system:
> 
> $$
> x^2+12 x y-4 y^2=30
> $$
> 
> 



##### solution

We want to graph

$$
x^2+12xy-4y^2=30.
$$

To remove the $x'y'$ term, we rotate the axes through an angle $\theta$ satisfying

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=1,
\qquad
B=12,
\qquad
C=-4.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{1-(-4)}{12}\\
&=\frac{5}{12}.
\end{aligned}
$$

We use a reference triangle with adjacent side $5$, opposite side $12$, and hypotenuse

$$
\begin{aligned}
h^2&=5^2+12^2\\
&=25+144\\
&=169,
\end{aligned}
$$

so

$$
h=13.
$$

Hence

$$
\cos(2\theta)=\frac{5}{13}.
$$

Using the half-angle formulas,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sin\theta
&=\sqrt{\frac{1-\cos(2\theta)}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{1-\frac{5}{13}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{13}{13}-\frac{5}{13}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{8}{13}\cdot\frac{1}{2}}\\
&=\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cos\theta
&=\sqrt{\frac{1+\cos(2\theta)}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{1+\frac{5}{13}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{13}{13}+\frac{5}{13}}{2}}\\
&=\sqrt{\frac{18}{13}\cdot\frac{1}{2}}\\
&=\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore

$$
\begin{aligned}
x
&=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta\\
&=x'\left(\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}\right)-y'\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}}\right)\\
&=\frac{3x'-2y'}{\sqrt{13}},
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
y
&=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta\\
&=x'\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}}\right)+y'\left(\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}\right)\\
&=\frac{2x'+3y'}{\sqrt{13}}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Now substitute these into the given equation:

$$
\left(\frac{3x'-2y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2+12\left(\frac{3x'-2y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)\left(\frac{2x'+3y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)-4\left(\frac{2x'+3y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2=30.
$$

Simplify:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{3x'-2y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2+12\left(\frac{3x'-2y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)\left(\frac{2x'+3y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)-4\left(\frac{2x'+3y'}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2
&=30\\
\frac{1}{13}\left[(3x'-2y')^2+12(3x'-2y')(2x'+3y')-4(2x'+3y')^2\right]
&=30\\
\frac{1}{13}\left[9x'^2-12x'y'+4y'^2+12(6x'^2+5x'y'-6y'^2)-4(4x'^2+12x'y'+9y'^2)\right]
&=30\\
\frac{1}{13}\left[9x'^2-12x'y'+4y'^2+72x'^2+60x'y'-72y'^2-16x'^2-48x'y'-36y'^2\right]
&=30\\
\frac{1}{13}\left[65x'^2-104y'^2\right]
&=30.
\end{aligned}
$$

Multiply both sides by $13$:

$$
65x'^2-104y'^2=390.
$$

Divide by $390$:

$$
\frac{65x'^2}{390}-\frac{104y'^2}{390}=1.
$$

Simplify:

$$
\frac{x'^2}{6}-\frac{4y'^2}{15}=1.
$$

Thus the equation in the rotated coordinate system is

$$
\frac{x'^2}{6}-\frac{4y'^2}{15}=1,
$$

which is a hyperbola centered at the origin with transverse axis along the $x'$-axis. Its vertices are

$$
(\pm\sqrt{6},0),
$$

and its asymptotes are

$$
y'=\pm\sqrt{\frac{15}{24}}\,x'=\pm\frac{\sqrt{10}}{4}x'.
$$

[corrected] The downstream sketch begins with `x^2+12xy-4y^2=20`, but the exercise statement and the correct derivation use `x^2+12xy-4y^2=30`.


++++

First, we find $\cot (2 \theta)$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+12 x y-4 y^2=20 & \rightarrow A=1, B=12, \text { and } C=-4 \\
\cot (2 \theta) & =\frac{A-C}{B} \\
\cot (2 \theta) & =\frac{1-(-4)}{12} \\
\cot (2 \theta) & =\frac{5}{12}
\end{aligned}
$$


Because $\cot (2 \theta)=\frac{5}{12}$, we can draw a reference triangle as in Figure 12.4.9.




> [!figure]
> ![300](Pasted%20image%2020260412014424.png)
> 
> 



$$
\cot (2 \theta)=\frac{5}{12}=\frac{\text { adjacent }}{\text { opposite }}
$$


Thus, the hypotenuse is

$$
\begin{aligned}
5^2+12^2 & =h^2 \\
25+144 & =h^2 \\
169 & =h^2 \\
h & =13
\end{aligned}
$$


Next, we find $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$. We will use half-angle identities.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta=\sqrt{\frac{1-\cos (2 \theta)}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{1-\frac{5}{13}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{13}{13}-\frac{5}{13}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{8}{13} \cdot \frac{1}{2}}=\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}} \\
& \cos \theta=\sqrt{\frac{1+\cos (2 \theta)}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{1+\frac{5}{13}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{\frac{13}{13}+\frac{5}{13}}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{18}{13} \cdot \frac{1}{2}}=\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}
\end{aligned}
$$


Now we find $x$ and $y$.

$$
\begin{gathered}
x=x^{\prime} \cos \theta-y^{\prime} \sin \theta \\
x=x^{\prime}\left(\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}\right)-y^{\prime}\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}}\right) \\
x=\frac{3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}
\end{gathered}
$$

and

$$
\begin{gathered}
y=x^{\prime} \sin \theta+y^{\prime} \cos \theta \\
y=x^{\prime}\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{13}}\right)+y^{\prime}\left(\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}\right) \\
y=\frac{2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}
\end{gathered}
$$



Now we substitute $x=\frac{3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}$ and $y=\frac{2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}$ into $x^2+12 x y-4 y^2=30$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2+12\left(\frac{3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}\right)\left(\frac{2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}\right)-4\left(\frac{2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}}{\sqrt{13}}\right)^2=30 & \\
\left(\frac{1}{13}\right)\left[\left(3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}\right)^2+12\left(3 x^{\prime}-2 y^{\prime}\right)\left(2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}\right)-4\left(2 x^{\prime}+3 y^{\prime}\right)^2\right]=30 & \text { Factor. } \\
\left(\frac{1}{13}\right)\left[9 x^{\prime 2}-12 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+4 y^{\prime 2}+12\left(6 x^{\prime 2}+5 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}-6 y^{\prime 2}\right)-4\left(4 x^{\prime 2}+12 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+9 y^{\prime 2}\right)\right]=30 & \text { Multiply. } \\
\left(\frac{1}{13}\right)\left[9 x^{\prime 2}-12 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+4 y^{\prime 2}+72 x^{\prime 2}+60 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}-72 y^{\prime 2}-16 x^{\prime 2}-48 x^{\prime} y^{\prime}-36 y^{\prime 2}\right]=30 & \text { Distribute. } \\
\left(\frac{1}{13}\right)\left[65 x^{\prime 2}-104 y^{\prime 2}\right]=30 & \text { Combine like terms. } \\
65 x^{\prime 2}-104 y^{\prime 2}=390 & \text { Multiply. } \\
\frac{x^{\prime 2}}{6}-\frac{4 y^{\prime 2}}{15}=1 & \text { Divide by } 390
\end{aligned}
$$


Figure 12.4.10 shows the graph of the hyperbola $\frac{x^{\prime 2}}{6}-\frac{4 y^{\prime 2}}{15}=1$


> [!figure]
> ![300](Pasted%20image%2020260412014531.png)
> 
> 



## 1.5.4 Identifying Conics without Rotating Axes


> [!review]
> 1. Suppose the equation $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ is transformed by rotating the axes into an equation $A^{\prime} x^{\prime 2}+B^{\prime} x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+C^{\prime} y^{\prime 2}+D^{\prime} x^{\prime}+E^{\prime} y^{\prime}+F^{\prime}=0$. Which expression in the coefficients is invariant under this rotation, and what is this expression called?
> 2. Given a conic in the general form $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$, how does the sign of the discriminant $B^2-4 A C$ determine whether the conic is an ellipse, a parabola, or a hyperbola?


##### problem 1

The expression

$$
B^2-4AC
$$

is invariant under rotation of axes. This expression is called the discriminant of the conic.

##### problem 2

For a conic in the general form

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

the sign of the discriminant determines the type of conic:

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC<0 & \quad \text{ellipse},\\
B^2-4AC=0 & \quad \text{parabola},\\
B^2-4AC>0 & \quad \text{hyperbola}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Because the discriminant is unchanged by rotation, this test works even when an $xy$ term is present.


+++++




Now we have come full circle. How do we identify the type of conic described by an equation? What happens when the axes are rotated? Recall, the general form of a conic is

$$
A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0
$$


If we apply the rotation formulas to this equation we get the form

$$
A^{\prime} x^{\prime 2}+B^{\prime} x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+C^{\prime} y^{\prime 2}+D^{\prime} x^{\prime}+E^{\prime} y^{\prime}+F^{\prime}=0
$$


It may be shown that

$$
B^2-4 A C=B^{\prime 2}-4 A^{\prime} C^{\prime}
$$


The expression does not vary after rotation, so we call the expression invariant. The discriminant, $B^2-4 A C$, is invariant and remains unchanged after rotation. Because the discriminant remains unchanged, observing the discriminant enables us to identify the conic section.



> [!NOTE] HOW TO: USING THE DISCRIMINANT TO IDENTIFY A CONIC
> 
> If the equation
> 
> $$
> A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0
> $$
> 
> is transformed by rotating axes into the equation
> 
> $$
> A^{\prime} x^{\prime 2}+B^{\prime} x^{\prime} y^{\prime}+C^{\prime} y^{\prime 2}+D^{\prime} x^{\prime}+E^{\prime} y^{\prime}+F^{\prime}=0
> $$
> 
> then
> 
> $$
> B^2-4 A C=B^{\prime 2}-4 A^{\prime} C^{\prime}
> $$
> 
> 
> The equation $A x^2+B x y+C y^2+D x+E y+F=0$ is an ellipse, a parabola, or a hyperbola, or a degenerate case of one of these. If the discriminant, $B^2 -4 A C$, is
> - $<0$, the conic section is an ellipse
> - $=0$, the conic section is a parabola
> - $>0$, the conic section is a hyperbola



> [!exercise]
> Example: Identifying the Conic without Rotating Axes
> 
> Identify the conic for each of the following without rotating axes.
> a. $5 x^2+2 \sqrt{3} x y+2 y^2-5=0$
> b. $5 x^2+2 \sqrt{3} x y+12 y^2-5=0$
> 



##### solution

To identify each conic without rotating axes, we use the discriminant

$$
B^2-4AC.
$$

For a general equation

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

the sign of the discriminant determines the conic:

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC<0 & \quad \text{ellipse},\\
B^2-4AC=0 & \quad \text{parabola},\\
B^2-4AC>0 & \quad \text{hyperbola}.
\end{aligned}
$$

For part (a),

$$
5x^2+2\sqrt{3}\,xy+2y^2-5=0,
$$

we have

$$
A=5,
\qquad
B=2\sqrt{3},
\qquad
C=2.
$$

Now compute the discriminant:

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=(2\sqrt{3})^2-4(5)(2)\\
&=4\cdot 3-40\\
&=12-40\\
&=-28.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
-28<0,
$$

the conic is an ellipse.

For part (b),

$$
5x^2+2\sqrt{3}\,xy+12y^2-5=0,
$$

we have

$$
A=5,
\qquad
B=2\sqrt{3},
\qquad
C=12.
$$

Now compute the discriminant:

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=(2\sqrt{3})^2-4(5)(12)\\
&=4\cdot 3-240\\
&=12-240\\
&=-228.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
-228<0,
$$

the conic is also an ellipse.

Therefore:

- (a) ellipse
- (b) ellipse


++++

a. Let's begin by determining $A, B$, and $C$.

$$
{\underset{A}{A}}_5^{x^2}+\underbrace{2 \sqrt{3}}_B x y+{\underset{C}{2}}^2 y^2-5=0
$$


Now, we find the discriminant.

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4 A C & =(2 \sqrt{3})^2-4(5)(2) \\
& =4(3)-40 \\
& =12-40 \\
& =-28<0
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore, $5 x^2+2 \sqrt{3} x y+2 y^2-5=0$ represents an ellipse.
b. Again, let's begin by determining $A, B$, and $C$.

$$
{\underset{A}{5}}^5 x^2+\underbrace{2 \sqrt{3}}_B x y+\underbrace{12}_C y^2-5=0
$$


Now, we find the discriminant.

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4 A C & =(2 \sqrt{3})^2-4(5)(12) \\
& =4(3)-240 \\
& =12-240 \\
& =-228<0
\end{aligned}
$$


Therefore, $5 x^2+2 \sqrt{3} x y+12 y^2-5=0$ represents an ellipse.





> [!exercise]
> Exercise
> 
> Identify the conic for each of the following without rotating axes.
> a. $x^2-9 x y+3 y^2-12=0$
> b. $10 x^2-9 x y+4 y^2-4=0$


##### solution

To identify each conic without rotating axes, we compute the discriminant

$$
B^2-4AC.
$$

For part (a),

$$
x^2-9xy+3y^2-12=0,
$$

so

$$
A=1,
\qquad
B=-9,
\qquad
C=3.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=(-9)^2-4(1)(3)\\
&=81-12\\
&=69.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
69>0,
$$

the conic is a hyperbola.

For part (b),

$$
10x^2-9xy+4y^2-4=0,
$$

so

$$
A=10,
\qquad
B=-9,
\qquad
C=4.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=(-9)^2-4(10)(4)\\
&=81-160\\
&=-79.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since

$$
-79<0,
$$

the conic is an ellipse.

Therefore:

- (a) hyperbola
- (b) ellipse


++++



# Exercises


## 1.2.1 The Ellipse

> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, write the equation of the ellipse in standard form. Then identify the center, vertices, and foci.
> 1. $\frac{x^2}{25}+\frac{y^2}{64}=1$
> 2. $\frac{(x-2)^2}{100}+\frac{(y+3)^2}{36}=1$
> 3. $9 x^2+y^2+54 x-4 y+76=0$
> 4. $9 x^2+36 y^2-36 x+72 y+36=0$



##### problem 1

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{x^2}{25}+\frac{y^2}{64}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $64$, the major axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=64,
\qquad
b^2=25,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=64-25=39.
$$

So the center is

$$
(0,0),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(0,\pm 8),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(0,\pm \sqrt{39}).
$$

##### problem 2

This equation is also already in standard form:

$$
\frac{(x-2)^2}{100}+\frac{(y+3)^2}{36}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $100$, the major axis is horizontal. Thus

$$
a^2=100,
\qquad
b^2=36,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=100-36=64.
$$

So the center is

$$
(2,-3),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(2\pm 10,-3)=(-8,-3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(12,-3),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(2\pm 8,-3)=(-6,-3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(10,-3).
$$

##### problem 3

We begin by writing the equation in standard form:

$$
9x^2+y^2+54x-4y+76=0.
$$

Group the $x$-terms and $y$-terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
9x^2+54x+y^2-4y+76&=0\\
9(x^2+6x)+(y^2-4y)+76&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
9\bigl((x+3)^2-9\bigr)+\bigl((y-2)^2-4\bigr)+76&=0\\
9(x+3)^2-81+(y-2)^2-4+76&=0\\
9(x+3)^2+(y-2)^2-9&=0\\
9(x+3)^2+(y-2)^2&=9.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $9$:

$$
\frac{(x+3)^2}{1}+\frac{(y-2)^2}{9}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $9$, the major axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=9,
\qquad
b^2=1,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=9-1=8.
$$

So the center is

$$
(-3,2),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(-3,2\pm 3)=(-3,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-3,5),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(-3,2\pm \sqrt{8})=(-3,2\pm 2\sqrt{2}).
$$

##### problem 4

We begin by writing the equation in standard form:

$$
9x^2+36y^2-36x+72y+36=0.
$$

First divide by $9$:

$$
x^2+4y^2-4x+8y+4=0.
$$

Group the terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
(x^2-4x)+4(y^2+2y)+4&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bigl((x-2)^2-4\bigr)+4\bigl((y+1)^2-1\bigr)+4&=0\\
(x-2)^2-4+4(y+1)^2-4+4&=0\\
(x-2)^2+4(y+1)^2-4&=0\\
(x-2)^2+4(y+1)^2&=4.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $4$:

$$
\frac{(x-2)^2}{4}+\frac{(y+1)^2}{1}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $4$, the major axis is horizontal. Thus

$$
a^2=4,
\qquad
b^2=1,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=4-1=3.
$$

So the center is

$$
(2,-1),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(2\pm 2,-1)=(0,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4,-1),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(2\pm \sqrt{3},-1).
$$



> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, graph the ellipse, noting center, vertices, and foci.
> 5. $\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{9}=1$
> 6. $\frac{(x-4)^2}{25}+\frac{(y+3)^2}{49}=1$
> 7. $4 x^2+y^2+16 x+4 y-44=0$
> 8. $2 x^2+3 y^2-20 x+12 y+38=0$



##### problem 5

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{9}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $36$, the major axis is horizontal. Thus

$$
a^2=36,
\qquad
b^2=9,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=36-9=27.
$$

So the center is

$$
(0,0),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(\pm 6,0),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(\pm 3\sqrt{3},0).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240121-ellipse-5.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2/36 + y^2/9 == 1,
  {x, -8, 8}, {y, -5, 5},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-6, 0}, {6, 0}}],
    Red, Point[{{-3 Sqrt[3], 0}, {3 Sqrt[3], 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (0,0)", {0, 0}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-6,0)", {-6, 0}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(6,0)", {6, 0}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-3 Sqrt[3],0)", {-3 Sqrt[3], 0}, {-0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(3 Sqrt[3],0)", {3 Sqrt[3], 0}, {0.8, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-6, 6, 600)
y = np.sqrt(9 * (1 - x**2 / 36))

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(-6, 0), (6, 0)]
foci = [(-3 * np.sqrt(3), 0), (3 * np.sqrt(3), 0)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(x, -y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$\frac{x^2}{36}+\frac{y^2}{9}=1$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.2, 0.2, r"Center $(0,0)$")
ax.text(-6, 0, r"$(-6,0)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(6, 0, r"$(6,0)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-3 * np.sqrt(3), 0, r"$(-3\sqrt{3},0)$", ha="right", va="top")
ax.text(3 * np.sqrt(3), 0, r"$(3\sqrt{3},0)$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-8, 8)
ax.set_ylim(-5, 5)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 6

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{(x-4)^2}{25}+\frac{(y+3)^2}{49}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $49$, the major axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=49,
\qquad
b^2=25,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=49-25=24.
$$

So the center is

$$
(4,-3),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(4,-3\pm 7)=(4,4)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4,-10),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(4,-3\pm \sqrt{24})=(4,-3\pm 2\sqrt{6}).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240121-ellipse-6.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (x - 4)^2/25 + (y + 3)^2/49 == 1,
  {x, -3, 11}, {y, -12, 6},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{4, -3}}],
    Blue, Point[{{4, 4}, {4, -10}}],
    Red, Point[{{4, -3 + 2 Sqrt[6]}, {4, -3 - 2 Sqrt[6]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (4,-3)", {4, -3}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(4,4)", {4, 4}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(4,-10)", {4, -10}, {0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(4,-3+2 Sqrt[6])", {4, -3 + 2 Sqrt[6]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(4,-3-2 Sqrt[6])", {4, -3 - 2 Sqrt[6]}, {1.2, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-10, 4, 700)
x_offset = np.sqrt(25 * (1 - (y + 3) ** 2 / 49))

center = (4, -3)
vertices = [(4, 4), (4, -10)]
foci = [(4, -3 + 2 * np.sqrt(6)), (4, -3 - 2 * np.sqrt(6))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(4 + x_offset, y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(4 - x_offset, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$\frac{(x-4)^2}{25}+\frac{(y+3)^2}{49}=1$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(4.2, -2.8, r"Center $(4,-3)$")
ax.text(4, 4, r"$(4,4)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(4, -10, r"$(4,-10)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(4, -3 + 2 * np.sqrt(6), r"$(4,-3+2\sqrt{6})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(4, -3 - 2 * np.sqrt(6), r"$(4,-3-2\sqrt{6})$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-3, 11)
ax.set_ylim(-12, 6)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 7

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
4x^2+y^2+16x+4y-44=0.
$$

Group the $x$-terms and $y$-terms:

$$
4(x^2+4x)+(y^2+4y)-44=0.
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
4\bigl((x+2)^2-4\bigr)+\bigl((y+2)^2-4\bigr)-44&=0\\
4(x+2)^2-16+(y+2)^2-4-44&=0\\
4(x+2)^2+(y+2)^2-64&=0\\
4(x+2)^2+(y+2)^2&=64.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $64$:

$$
\frac{(x+2)^2}{16}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{64}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $64$, the major axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=64,
\qquad
b^2=16,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=64-16=48.
$$

So the center is

$$
(-2,-2),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(-2,-2\pm 8)=(-2,6)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-2,-10),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(-2,-2\pm \sqrt{48})=(-2,-2\pm 4\sqrt{3}).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240121-ellipse-7.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  4 x^2 + y^2 + 16 x + 4 y - 44 == 0,
  {x, -7, 3}, {y, -12, 8},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-2, -2}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-2, 6}, {-2, -10}}],
    Red, Point[{{-2, -2 + 4 Sqrt[3]}, {-2, -2 - 4 Sqrt[3]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (-2,-2)", {-2, -2}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-2,6)", {-2, 6}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-2,-10)", {-2, -10}, {0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(-2,-2+4 Sqrt[3])", {-2, -2 + 4 Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-2,-2-4 Sqrt[3])", {-2, -2 - 4 Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-10, 6, 700)
x_offset = np.sqrt(16 * (1 - (y + 2) ** 2 / 64))

center = (-2, -2)
vertices = [(-2, 6), (-2, -10)]
foci = [(-2, -2 + 4 * np.sqrt(3)), (-2, -2 - 4 * np.sqrt(3))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(-2 + x_offset, y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(-2 - x_offset, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$4x^2+y^2+16x+4y-44=0$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-1.8, -1.8, r"Center $(-2,-2)$")
ax.text(-2, 6, r"$(-2,6)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, -10, r"$(-2,-10)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(-2, -2 + 4 * np.sqrt(3), r"$(-2,-2+4\sqrt{3})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2, -2 - 4 * np.sqrt(3), r"$(-2,-2-4\sqrt{3})$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-7, 3)
ax.set_ylim(-12, 8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 8

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
2x^2+3y^2-20x+12y+38=0.
$$

Group the terms:

$$
2(x^2-10x)+3(y^2+4y)+38=0.
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2\bigl((x-5)^2-25\bigr)+3\bigl((y+2)^2-4\bigr)+38&=0\\
2(x-5)^2-50+3(y+2)^2-12+38&=0\\
2(x-5)^2+3(y+2)^2-24&=0\\
2(x-5)^2+3(y+2)^2&=24.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $24$:

$$
\frac{(x-5)^2}{12}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{8}=1.
$$

Since the larger denominator is $12$, the major axis is horizontal. Thus

$$
a^2=12,
\qquad
b^2=8,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=12-8=4.
$$

So the center is

$$
(5,-2),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(5\pm \sqrt{12},-2)=(5\pm 2\sqrt{3},-2),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(5\pm 2,-2)=(3,-2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(7,-2).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240121-ellipse-8.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  2 x^2 + 3 y^2 - 20 x + 12 y + 38 == 0,
  {x, 0, 10}, {y, -6, 2},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{5, -2}}],
    Blue, Point[{{5 - 2 Sqrt[3], -2}, {5 + 2 Sqrt[3], -2}}],
    Red, Point[{{3, -2}, {7, -2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (5,-2)", {5, -2}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(5-2 Sqrt[3],-2)", {5 - 2 Sqrt[3], -2}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(5+2 Sqrt[3],-2)", {5 + 2 Sqrt[3], -2}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(3,-2)", {3, -2}, {-0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(7,-2)", {7, -2}, {0.8, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(5 - 2 * np.sqrt(3), 5 + 2 * np.sqrt(3), 700)
y_offset = np.sqrt(8 * (1 - (x - 5) ** 2 / 12))

center = (5, -2)
vertices = [(5 - 2 * np.sqrt(3), -2), (5 + 2 * np.sqrt(3), -2)]
foci = [(3, -2), (7, -2)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, -2 + y_offset, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(x, -2 - y_offset, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$2x^2+3y^2-20x+12y+38=0$")

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(5.2, -1.8, r"Center $(5,-2)$")
ax.text(5 - 2 * np.sqrt(3), -2, r"$(5-2\sqrt{3},-2)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(5 + 2 * np.sqrt(3), -2, r"$(5+2\sqrt{3},-2)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(3, -2, r"$(3,-2)$", ha="right", va="top")
ax.text(7, -2, r"$(7,-2)$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(0, 10)
ax.set_ylim(-6, 2)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```



++++


> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, use the given information to find the equation for the ellipse.
> 9. Center at $(0,0)$, focus at $(3,0)$, vertex at $(-5,0)$
> 10. Center at $(2,-2)$, vertex at $(7,-2)$, focus at $(4,-2)$





##### problem 9

The center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

The given vertex is

$$
(-5,0),
$$

so the major axis is horizontal and

$$
a=5.
$$

The given focus is

$$
(3,0),
$$

so

$$
c=3.
$$

For an ellipse, the relationship among $a$, $b$, and $c$ is

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=a^2-c^2\\
&=25-9\\
&=16.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the ellipse is centered at the origin and has horizontal major axis, its equation is

$$
\frac{x^2}{a^2}+\frac{y^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{x^2}{25}+\frac{y^2}{16}=1.
$$

##### problem 10

The center is

$$
(2,-2).
$$

The given vertex is

$$
(7,-2),
$$

so the major axis is horizontal and

$$
a=7-2=5.
$$

The given focus is

$$
(4,-2),
$$

so

$$
c=4-2=2.
$$

Again, for an ellipse,

$$
c^2=a^2-b^2.
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=a^2-c^2\\
&=25-4\\
&=21.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the ellipse has center $(h,k)=(2,-2)$ and horizontal major axis, its equation is

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}+\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{(x-2)^2}{25}+\frac{(y+2)^2}{21}=1.
$$



++++



## 1.2.2 The Hyperbola


> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, write the equation of the hyperbola in standard form. Then give the center, vertices, and foci.
> 11. $\frac{x^2}{81}-\frac{y^2}{9}=1$
> 12. $\frac{(y+1)^2}{16}-\frac{(x-4)^2}{36}=1$
> 13. $9 y^2-4 x^2+54 y-16 x+29=0$
> 14. $3 x^2-y^2-12 x-6 y-9=0$




##### problem 11

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{x^2}{81}-\frac{y^2}{9}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola centered at the origin with horizontal transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=81,
\qquad
b^2=9,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=81+9=90.
$$

So the center is

$$
(0,0),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(\pm 9,0),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(\pm \sqrt{90},0)=(\pm 3\sqrt{10},0).
$$

##### problem 12

This equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{(y+1)^2}{16}-\frac{(x-4)^2}{36}=1.
$$

Since the positive term involves $y$, the transverse axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=16,
\qquad
b^2=36,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=16+36=52.
$$

So the center is

$$
(4,-1),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(4,-1\pm 4)=(4,3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4,-5),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(4,-1\pm \sqrt{52})=(4,-1\pm 2\sqrt{13}).
$$

##### problem 13

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
9y^2-4x^2+54y-16x+29=0.
$$

Group the $y$-terms and $x$-terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
9(y^2+6y)-4(x^2+4x)+29&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
9\bigl((y+3)^2-9\bigr)-4\bigl((x+2)^2-4\bigr)+29&=0\\
9(y+3)^2-81-4(x+2)^2+16+29&=0\\
9(y+3)^2-4(x+2)^2-36&=0\\
9(y+3)^2-4(x+2)^2&=36.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $36$:

$$
\frac{(y+3)^2}{4}-\frac{(x+2)^2}{9}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola with vertical transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=4,
\qquad
b^2=9,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=4+9=13.
$$

So the center is

$$
(-2,-3),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(-2,-3\pm 2)=(-2,-1)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-2,-5),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(-2,-3\pm \sqrt{13}).
$$

##### problem 14

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
3x^2-y^2-12x-6y-9=0.
$$

Group the terms:

$$
\begin{aligned}
3(x^2-4x)-(y^2+6y)-9&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
3\bigl((x-2)^2-4\bigr)-\bigl((y+3)^2-9\bigr)-9&=0\\
3(x-2)^2-12-(y+3)^2+9-9&=0\\
3(x-2)^2-(y+3)^2-12&=0\\
3(x-2)^2-(y+3)^2&=12.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $12$:

$$
\frac{(x-2)^2}{4}-\frac{(y+3)^2}{12}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola with horizontal transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=4,
\qquad
b^2=12,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=4+12=16.
$$

So the center is

$$
(2,-3),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(2\pm 2,-3)=(0,-3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(4,-3),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(2\pm 4,-3)=(-2,-3)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(6,-3).
$$



++++


> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, graph the hyperbola, labeling vertices and foci.
> 15. $\frac{x^2}{9}-\frac{y^2}{16}=1$
> 16. $\frac{(y-1)^2}{49}-\frac{(x+1)^2}{4}=1$
> 17. $x^2-4 y^2+6 x+32 y-91=0$
> 18. $2 y^2-x^2-12 y-6=0$



##### problem 15

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{x^2}{9}-\frac{y^2}{16}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola centered at the origin with horizontal transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=9,
\qquad
b^2=16,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=9+16=25.
$$

So the center is

$$
(0,0),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(\pm 3,0),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(\pm 5,0).
$$

The asymptotes are

$$
y=\pm \frac{4}{3}x.
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240132-hyperbola-15.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2/9 - y^2/16 == 1,
  {x, -7, 7}, {y, -7, 7},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-6, -8}, {6, 8}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-6, 8}, {6, -8}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-3, 0}, {3, 0}}],
    Red, Point[{{-5, 0}, {5, 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (0,0)", {0, 0}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-3,0)", {-3, 0}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(3,0)", {3, 0}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-5,0)", {-5, 0}, {-0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(5,0)", {5, 0}, {0.8, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-7, 7, 800)
x = np.sqrt(9 * (1 + y**2 / 16))

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(-3, 0), (3, 0)]
foci = [(-5, 0), (5, 0)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(-x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$\frac{x^2}{9}-\frac{y^2}{16}=1$")

xx = np.linspace(-6, 6, 200)
ax.plot(xx, (4 / 3) * xx, "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)
ax.plot(xx, -(4 / 3) * xx, "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.2, 0.2, r"Center $(0,0)$")
ax.text(-3, 0, r"$(-3,0)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(3, 0, r"$(3,0)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-5, 0, r"$(-5,0)$", ha="right", va="top")
ax.text(5, 0, r"$(5,0)$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-7, 7)
ax.set_ylim(-7, 7)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 16

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
\frac{(y-1)^2}{49}-\frac{(x+1)^2}{4}=1.
$$

Since the positive term involves $y$, the transverse axis is vertical. Thus

$$
a^2=49,
\qquad
b^2=4,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=49+4=53.
$$

So the center is

$$
(-1,1),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(-1,1\pm 7)=(-1,8)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-1,-6),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(-1,1\pm \sqrt{53}).
$$

The asymptotes are

$$
y-1=\pm \frac{7}{2}(x+1).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240132-hyperbola-16.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (y - 1)^2/49 - (x + 1)^2/4 == 1,
  {x, -5, 3}, {y, -9, 11},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-3, -6}, {1, 8}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-3, 8}, {1, -6}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-1, 1}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-1, 8}, {-1, -6}}],
    Red, Point[{{-1, 1 + Sqrt[53]}, {-1, 1 - Sqrt[53]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (-1,1)", {-1, 1}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1,8)", {-1, 8}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1,-6)", {-1, -6}, {0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(-1,1+Sqrt[53])", {-1, 1 + Sqrt[53]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1,1-Sqrt[53])", {-1, 1 - Sqrt[53]}, {1.2, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-5, 3, 800)
y = np.sqrt(49 * (1 + (x + 1) ** 2 / 4))

center = (-1, 1)
vertices = [(-1, 8), (-1, -6)]
foci = [(-1, 1 + np.sqrt(53)), (-1, 1 - np.sqrt(53))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, 1 + y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(x, 1 - y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$\frac{(y-1)^2}{49}-\frac{(x+1)^2}{4}=1$")

xx = np.linspace(-3, 1, 200)
ax.plot(xx, 1 + (7 / 2) * (xx + 1), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)
ax.plot(xx, 1 - (7 / 2) * (xx + 1), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-0.8, 1.2, r"Center $(-1,1)$")
ax.text(-1, 8, r"$(-1,8)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-1, -6, r"$(-1,-6)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(-1, 1 + np.sqrt(53), r"$(-1,1+\sqrt{53})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-1, 1 - np.sqrt(53), r"$(-1,1-\sqrt{53})$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-5, 3)
ax.set_ylim(-9, 11)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 17

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
x^2-4y^2+6x+32y-91=0.
$$

Group the terms:

$$
(x^2+6x)-4(y^2-8y)-91=0.
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bigl((x+3)^2-9\bigr)-4\bigl((y-4)^2-16\bigr)-91&=0\\
(x+3)^2-9-4(y-4)^2+64-91&=0\\
(x+3)^2-4(y-4)^2-36&=0\\
(x+3)^2-4(y-4)^2&=36.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $36$:

$$
\frac{(x+3)^2}{36}-\frac{(y-4)^2}{9}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola with horizontal transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=36,
\qquad
b^2=9,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=36+9=45.
$$

So the center is

$$
(-3,4),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(-3\pm 6,4)=(3,4)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(-9,4),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(-3\pm \sqrt{45},4)=(-3\pm 3\sqrt{5},4).
$$

The asymptotes are

$$
y-4=\pm \frac{1}{2}(x+3).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240132-hyperbola-17.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 - 4 y^2 + 6 x + 32 y - 91 == 0,
  {x, -12, 6}, {y, -4, 12},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-12, -1/2}, {6, 17/2}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-12, 17/2}, {6, -1/2}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-3, 4}}],
    Blue, Point[{{3, 4}, {-9, 4}}],
    Red, Point[{{-3 + 3 Sqrt[5], 4}, {-3 - 3 Sqrt[5], 4}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (-3,4)", {-3, 4}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(3,4)", {3, 4}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-9,4)", {-9, 4}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-3+3 Sqrt[5],4)", {-3 + 3 Sqrt[5], 4}, {0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(-3-3 Sqrt[5],4)", {-3 - 3 Sqrt[5], 4}, {-0.8, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-4, 12, 800)
x = np.sqrt(36 * (1 + (y - 4) ** 2 / 9))

center = (-3, 4)
vertices = [(3, 4), (-9, 4)]
foci = [(-3 + 3 * np.sqrt(5), 4), (-3 - 3 * np.sqrt(5), 4)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(-3 + x, y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(-3 - x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$x^2-4y^2+6x+32y-91=0$")

xx = np.linspace(-12, 6, 200)
ax.plot(xx, 4 + 0.5 * (xx + 3), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)
ax.plot(xx, 4 - 0.5 * (xx + 3), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-2.8, 4.2, r"Center $(-3,4)$")
ax.text(3, 4, r"$(3,4)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-9, 4, r"$(-9,4)$", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(-3 + 3 * np.sqrt(5), 4, r"$(-3+3\sqrt{5},4)$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(-3 - 3 * np.sqrt(5), 4, r"$(-3-3\sqrt{5},4)$", ha="right", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-12, 6)
ax.set_ylim(-4, 12)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 18

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
2y^2-x^2-12y-6=0.
$$

Group the terms:

$$
2(y^2-6y)-x^2-6=0.
$$

Complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2\bigl((y-3)^2-9\bigr)-x^2-6&=0\\
2(y-3)^2-18-x^2-6&=0\\
2(y-3)^2-x^2-24&=0\\
2(y-3)^2-x^2&=24.
\end{aligned}
$$

Divide by $24$:

$$
\frac{(y-3)^2}{12}-\frac{x^2}{24}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola with vertical transverse axis. Thus

$$
a^2=12,
\qquad
b^2=24,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=12+24=36.
$$

So the center is

$$
(0,3),
$$

the vertices are

$$
(0,3\pm \sqrt{12})=(0,3\pm 2\sqrt{3}),
$$

and the foci are

$$
(0,3\pm 6)=(0,9)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(0,-3).
$$

The asymptotes are

$$
y-3=\pm \frac{\sqrt{12}}{\sqrt{24}}x=\pm \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}x.
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240132-hyperbola-18.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  2 y^2 - x^2 - 12 y - 6 == 0,
  {x, -10, 10}, {y, -6, 12},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-10, 3 - 5 Sqrt[2]}, {10, 3 + 5 Sqrt[2]}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-10, 3 + 5 Sqrt[2]}, {10, 3 - 5 Sqrt[2]}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{0, 3}}],
    Blue, Point[{{0, 3 + 2 Sqrt[3]}, {0, 3 - 2 Sqrt[3]}}],
    Red, Point[{{0, 9}, {0, -3}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (0,3)", {0, 3}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(0,3+2 Sqrt[3])", {0, 3 + 2 Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(0,3-2 Sqrt[3])", {0, 3 - 2 Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["(0,9)", {0, 9}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(0,-3)", {0, -3}, {1.2, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-10, 10, 800)
y = np.sqrt(12 * (1 + x**2 / 24))

center = (0, 3)
vertices = [(0, 3 + 2 * np.sqrt(3)), (0, 3 - 2 * np.sqrt(3))]
foci = [(0, 9), (0, -3)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, 3 + y, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(x, 3 - y, color="black", linewidth=2, label=r"$2y^2-x^2-12y-6=0$")

xx = np.linspace(-10, 10, 200)
ax.plot(xx, 3 + (np.sqrt(2) / 2) * xx, "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)
ax.plot(xx, 3 - (np.sqrt(2) / 2) * xx, "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.2, 3.2, r"Center $(0,3)$")
ax.text(0, 3 + 2 * np.sqrt(3), r"$(0,3+2\sqrt{3})$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(0, 3 - 2 * np.sqrt(3), r"$(0,3-2\sqrt{3})$", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(0, 9, r"$(0,9)$", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(0, -3, r"$(0,-3)$", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-10, 10)
ax.set_ylim(-6, 12)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```


++++



> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, find the equation of the hyperbola.
> 19. Center at $(0,0)$, vertex at $(0,4)$, focus at $(0,-6)$
> 20. Foci at $(3,7)$ and $(7,7)$, vertex at $(6,7)$



##### problem 19

The center is

$$
(0,0).
$$

The given vertex is

$$
(0,4),
$$

so the transverse axis is vertical and

$$
a=4.
$$

The given focus is

$$
(0,-6),
$$

so

$$
c=6.
$$

For a hyperbola, the relationship among $a$, $b$, and $c$ is

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=36-16\\
&=20.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the hyperbola is centered at the origin with vertical transverse axis, its equation is

$$
\frac{y^2}{a^2}-\frac{x^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{y^2}{16}-\frac{x^2}{20}=1.
$$

##### problem 20

The foci are

$$
(3,7)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(7,7),
$$

so the center is their midpoint:

$$
\left(\frac{3+7}{2},\frac{7+7}{2}\right)=(5,7).
$$

Since the foci have the same $y$-coordinate, the transverse axis is horizontal. The given vertex is

$$
(6,7),
$$

so

$$
a=6-5=1.
$$

The distance from the center $(5,7)$ to either focus is

$$
c=7-5=2.
$$

For a hyperbola,

$$
c^2=a^2+b^2.
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
b^2
&=c^2-a^2\\
&=4-1\\
&=3.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the hyperbola has center $(h,k)=(5,7)$ and horizontal transverse axis, its equation is

$$
\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2}-\frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2}=1.
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{(x-5)^2}{1}-\frac{(y-7)^2}{3}=1.
$$


++++



## 1.2.3 The Parablola


> [!exercise]
> 21. $y^2=12 x$
> 22. $(x+2)^2=\frac{1}{2}(y-1)$
> 23. $y^2-6 y-6 x-3=0$
> 24. $x^2+10 x-y+23=0$


##### problem 21

The equation

$$
y^2=12x
$$

is already in standard form

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

Thus

$$
4p=12,
\qquad
p=3.
$$

So the vertex is

$$
(0,0),
$$

the focus is

$$
(3,0),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=-3.
$$

##### problem 22

The equation

$$
(x+2)^2=\frac{1}{2}(y-1)
$$

is in the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

Thus

$$
h=-2,
\qquad
k=1,
\qquad
4p=\frac{1}{2}.
$$

So

$$
p=\frac{1}{8}.
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(-2,1),
$$

the focus is

$$
\left(-2,1+\frac{1}{8}\right)=\left(-2,\frac{9}{8}\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
y=1-\frac{1}{8}=\frac{7}{8}.
$$

##### problem 23

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
y^2-6y-6x-3=0.
$$

Group the $y$-terms and complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
y^2-6y&=6x+3\\
y^2-6y+9&=6x+3+9\\
(y-3)^2&=6x+12\\
(y-3)^2&=6(x+2).
\end{aligned}
$$

This is in the form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h),
$$

so

$$
h=-2,
\qquad
k=3,
\qquad
4p=6,
\qquad
p=\frac{3}{2}.
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(-2,3),
$$

the focus is

$$
\left(-2+\frac{3}{2},3\right)=\left(-\frac{1}{2},3\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=-2-\frac{3}{2}=-\frac{7}{2}.
$$

##### problem 24

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
x^2+10x-y+23=0.
$$

Group the $x$-terms and complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+10x&=y-23\\
x^2+10x+25&=y-23+25\\
(x+5)^2&=y+2.
\end{aligned}
$$

This is in the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k),
$$

so

$$
h=-5,
\qquad
k=-2,
\qquad
4p=1,
\qquad
p=\frac{1}{4}.
$$

Therefore the vertex is

$$
(-5,-2),
$$

the focus is

$$
\left(-5,-2+\frac{1}{4}\right)=\left(-5,-\frac{7}{4}\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
y=-2-\frac{1}{4}=-\frac{9}{4}.
$$


++++


> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, graph the parabola, labeling vertex, focus, and directrix.
> 25. $x^2+4 y=0$
> 26. $(y-1)^2=\frac{1}{2}(x+3)$
> 27. $x^2-8 x-10 y+46=0$
> 28. $2 y^2+12 y+6 x+15=0$


##### problem 25

We first write the equation in standard form:

$$
x^2+4y=0
\qquad\Longrightarrow\qquad
x^2=-4y.
$$

This matches the standard form

$$
x^2=4py.
$$

Therefore,

$$
4p=-4,
\qquad
p=-1.
$$

So the parabola has vertex

$$
(0,0),
$$

focus

$$
(0,p)=(0,-1),
$$

and directrix

$$
y=-p=1.
$$

Since $p<0$, the parabola opens downward.


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240145-parabola-25.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 + 4 y == 0,
  {x, -5, 5}, {y, -6, 3},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-5, 1}, {5, 1}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Red, Point[{{0, -1}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (0,0)", {0, 0}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (0,-1)", {0, -1}, {1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["Directrix y = 1", {2.5, 1}, {0, 1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-4.5, 4.5, 800)
y = -(x**2) / 4

vertex = (0, 0)
focus = (0, -1)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label="x^2 + 4y = 0")
ax.axhline(1, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="y = 1")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.15, 0.2, "Vertex (0,0)")
ax.text(0.15, -1.15, "Focus (0,-1)")
ax.text(2.2, 1.15, "Directrix y = 1")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-5, 5)
ax.set_ylim(-6, 3)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 26

The equation is already in standard form:

$$
(y-1)^2=\frac{1}{2}(x+3).
$$

Compare this with

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h).
$$

Thus

$$
h=-3,
\qquad
k=1,
\qquad
4p=\frac{1}{2},
\qquad
p=\frac{1}{8}.
$$

So the vertex is

$$
(-3,1),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h+p,k)=\left(-3+\frac{1}{8},1\right)=\left(-\frac{23}{8},1\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=h-p=-3-\frac{1}{8}=-\frac{25}{8}.
$$

Since $p>0$, the parabola opens to the right.


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240145-parabola-26.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (y - 1)^2 == (1/2) (x + 3),
  {x, -23/5, 11/5}, {y, -9/5, 19/5},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-25/8, -9/5}, {-25/8, 19/5}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-3, 1}}],
    Red, Point[{{-23/8, 1}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (-3,1)", {-3, 1}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (-23/8,1)", {-23/8, 1}, {1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["Directrix x = -25/8", {-25/8, 3.1}, {-1.0, 0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-1.5, 3.5, 800)
x = 2 * (y - 1) ** 2 - 3

vertex = (-3, 1)
focus = (-23 / 8, 1)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label="(y-1)^2 = 0.5(x+3)")
ax.axvline(-25 / 8, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="x = -25/8")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-2.95, 1.15, "Vertex (-3,1)")
ax.text(-2.75, 0.75, "Focus (-23/8,1)")
ax.text(-4.5, 3.1, "Directrix x = -25/8")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-4.6, 2.2)
ax.set_ylim(-1.8, 3.8)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend(loc="upper right")
plt.show()
```

##### problem 27

We first complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-8x-10y+46&=0\\
x^2-8x&=10y-46\\
x^2-8x+16&=10y-46+16\\
(x-4)^2&=10y-30\\
(x-4)^2&=10(y-3).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compare with the standard form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

Thus

$$
h=4,
\qquad
k=3,
\qquad
4p=10,
\qquad
p=\frac{5}{2}.
$$

So the vertex is

$$
(4,3),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h,k+p)=\left(4,3+\frac{5}{2}\right)=\left(4,\frac{11}{2}\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
y=k-p=3-\frac{5}{2}=\frac{1}{2}.
$$

Since $p>0$, the parabola opens upward.


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240145-parabola-27.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  x^2 - 8 x - 10 y + 46 == 0,
  {x, -3, 11}, {y, -1, 9},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-3, 1/2}, {11, 1/2}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{4, 3}}],
    Red, Point[{{4, 11/2}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (4,3)", {4, 3}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (4,11/2)", {4, 11/2}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Directrix y = 1/2", {8, 1/2}, {0, 1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

x = np.linspace(-3, 11, 800)
y = ((x - 4) ** 2) / 10 + 3

vertex = (4, 3)
focus = (4, 11 / 2)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label="x^2 - 8x - 10y + 46 = 0")
ax.axhline(1 / 2, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="y = 1/2")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(4.15, 3.1, "Vertex (4,3)")
ax.text(4.15, 5.6, "Focus (4,11/2)")
ax.text(7.2, 0.65, "Directrix y = 1/2")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-3, 11)
ax.set_ylim(-1, 9)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

##### problem 28

We first complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
2y^2+12y+6x+15&=0\\
2y^2+12y&=-6x-15\\
y^2+6y&=-3x-\frac{15}{2}\\
y^2+6y+9&=-3x-\frac{15}{2}+9\\
(y+3)^2&=-3x+\frac{3}{2}\\
(y+3)^2&=-3\left(x-\frac{1}{2}\right).
\end{aligned}
$$

Now compare with the standard form

$$
(y-k)^2=4p(x-h).
$$

Thus

$$
h=\frac{1}{2},
\qquad
k=-3,
\qquad
4p=-3,
\qquad
p=-\frac{3}{4}.
$$

So the vertex is

$$
\left(\frac{1}{2},-3\right),
$$

the focus is

$$
(h+p,k)=\left(\frac{1}{2}-\frac{3}{4},-3\right)=\left(-\frac{1}{4},-3\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=h-p=\frac{1}{2}+\frac{3}{4}=\frac{5}{4}.
$$

Since $p<0$, the parabola opens to the left.


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240145-parabola-28.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  2 y^2 + 12 y + 6 x + 15 == 0,
  {x, -13, 3}, {y, -7, 1},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{5/4, -7}, {5/4, 1}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{1/2, -3}}],
    Red, Point[{{-1/4, -3}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (1/2,-3)", {1/2, -3}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (-1/4,-3)", {-1/4, -3}, {-1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["Directrix x = 5/4", {5/4, -1/4}, {1.0, 0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

y = np.linspace(-6.5, 0.5, 800)
x = 0.5 - ((y + 3) ** 2) / 3

vertex = (0.5, -3)
focus = (-0.25, -3)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(x, y, color="black", linewidth=2, label="2y^2 + 12y + 6x + 15 = 0")
ax.axvline(5 / 4, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="x = 5/4")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.62, -2.75, "Vertex (1/2,-3)")
ax.text(-2.25, -3.25, "Focus (-1/4,-3)")
ax.text(1.35, -0.35, "Directrix x = 5/4")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-13, 3)
ax.set_ylim(-7, 1)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend(loc="lower left")
plt.show()
```


++++


> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, write the equation of the parabola using the given information.
> 29. Focus at $(-4,0)$; directrix is $x=4$
> 30. Focus at $\left(2, \frac{9}{8}\right)$; directrix is $y=\frac{7}{8}$


##### problem 29

The focus is

$$
(-4,0),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=4.
$$

Since the focus and directrix are symmetric about the origin, the vertex is the midpoint between them:

$$
(0,0).
$$

The axis of symmetry is horizontal, so the parabola has the form

$$
y^2=4px.
$$

For this form, the focus is

$$
(p,0),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
x=-p.
$$

Because the focus is $(-4,0)$, we have

$$
p=-4.
$$

Therefore

$$
y^2=4(-4)x=-16x.
$$

##### problem 30

The focus is

$$
\left(2,\frac{9}{8}\right),
$$

and the directrix is

$$
y=\frac{7}{8}.
$$

The vertex lies halfway between the focus and the directrix along the axis of symmetry, so its $x$-coordinate is

$$
2,
$$

and its $y$-coordinate is

$$
\frac{\frac{9}{8}+\frac{7}{8}}{2}
=\frac{\frac{16}{8}}{2}
=\frac{2}{2}
=1.
$$

Thus the vertex is

$$
(2,1).
$$

Since the axis of symmetry is vertical, the parabola has the form

$$
(x-h)^2=4p(y-k).
$$

Here

$$
(h,k)=(2,1).
$$

The distance from the vertex to the focus is

$$
p=\frac{9}{8}-1=\frac{9}{8}-\frac{8}{8}=\frac{1}{8}.
$$

So

$$
4p=4\left(\frac{1}{8}\right)=\frac{1}{2}.
$$

Therefore

$$
(x-2)^2=\frac{1}{2}(y-1).
$$


++++


## 1.2.4 Rotation of Axes

> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, determine which of the conic sections is represented.
> 31. $16 x^2+24 x y+9 y^2+24 x-60 y-60=0$
> 32. $4 x^2+14 x y+5 y^2+18 x-6 y+30=0$
> 33. $4 x^2+x y+2 y^2+8 x-26 y+9=0$



##### solution

To identify each conic, we use the discriminant

$$
B^2-4AC
$$

from the general form

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0.
$$

Recall:

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC&<0 &&\Rightarrow \text{ellipse},\\
B^2-4AC&=0 &&\Rightarrow \text{parabola},\\
B^2-4AC&>0 &&\Rightarrow \text{hyperbola}.
\end{aligned}
$$

For 31,

$$
16x^2+24xy+9y^2+24x-60y-60=0,
$$

so

$$
A=16,
\qquad
B=24,
\qquad
C=9.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=24^2-4(16)(9)\\
&=576-576\\
&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore 31 is a parabola.

For 32,

$$
4x^2+14xy+5y^2+18x-6y+30=0,
$$

so

$$
A=4,
\qquad
B=14,
\qquad
C=5.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=14^2-4(4)(5)\\
&=196-80\\
&=116.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since this is positive, 32 is a hyperbola.

For 33,

$$
4x^2+xy+2y^2+8x-26y+9=0,
$$

so

$$
A=4,
\qquad
B=1,
\qquad
C=2.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B^2-4AC
&=1^2-4(4)(2)\\
&=1-32\\
&=-31.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since this is negative, 33 is an ellipse.


++++



> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, determine the angle $\theta$ that will eliminate the $x y$ term, and write the corresponding equation without the $x y$ term.
> 34. $x^2+4 x y-2 y^2-6=0$
> 35. $x^2-x y+y^2-6=0$


##### problem 34

We want to remove the $xy$ term from

$$
x^2+4xy-2y^2-6=0.
$$

For a conic of the form

$$
Ax^2+Bxy+Cy^2+Dx+Ey+F=0,
$$

the rotation angle satisfies

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=1,
\qquad
B=4,
\qquad
C=-2.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{1-(-2)}{4}\\
&=\frac{3}{4}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $\cot(2\theta)=\frac34$, we may take

$$
\cos(2\theta)=\frac35,
\qquad
\sin(2\theta)=\frac45.
$$

Because $0^\circ<\theta<45^\circ$, we use the positive half-angle values:

$$
\cos\theta=\frac{2}{\sqrt5},
\qquad
\sin\theta=\frac{1}{\sqrt5}.
$$

Now substitute

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
=\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt5}
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta
=\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt5}.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2+4xy-2y^2
&=\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt5}\right)^2
+4\left(\frac{2x'-y'}{\sqrt5}\right)\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt5}\right)
-2\left(\frac{x'+2y'}{\sqrt5}\right)^2\\
&=\frac{4x'^2-4x'y'+y'^2}{5}
+\frac{8x'^2+12x'y'-8y'^2}{5}
-\frac{2x'^2+8x'y'+8y'^2}{5}\\
&=\frac{10x'^2-15y'^2}{5}\\
&=2x'^2-3y'^2.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the rotated equation is

$$
2x'^2-3y'^2-6=0,
$$

or

$$
2x'^2-3y'^2=6.
$$

In standard form,

$$
\frac{x'^2}{3}-\frac{y'^2}{2}=1.
$$

##### problem 35

We want to remove the $xy$ term from

$$
x^2-xy+y^2-6=0.
$$

Again,

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=1,
\qquad
B=-1,
\qquad
C=1.
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{1-1}{-1}\\
&=0.
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $A=C$, we take

$$
\theta=45^\circ.
$$

Therefore

$$
\cos\theta=\sin\theta=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}.
$$

Now substitute

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta
=\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt2}
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta
=\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt2}.
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
x^2-xy+y^2
&=\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt2}\right)^2
-\left(\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt2}\right)\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt2}\right)
+\left(\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt2}\right)^2\\
&=\frac{x'^2-2x'y'+y'^2}{2}
-\frac{x'^2-y'^2}{2}
+\frac{x'^2+2x'y'+y'^2}{2}\\
&=\frac{x'^2+3y'^2}{2}.
\end{aligned}
$$

So the rotated equation becomes

$$
\frac{x'^2+3y'^2}{2}-6=0.
$$

Multiply by $2$:

$$
x'^2+3y'^2-12=0,
$$

so

$$
x'^2+3y'^2=12.
$$

In standard form,

$$
\frac{x'^2}{12}+\frac{y'^2}{4}=1.
$$


++++



> [!exercise]
> For the following exercises, graph the equation relative to the $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ system in which the equation has no $x^{\prime} y^{\prime}$ term.
> 36. $9 x^2-24 x y+16 y^2-80 x-60 y+100=0$
> 37. $x^2-x y+y^2-2=0$
> 38. $6 x^2+24 x y-y^2-12 x+26 y+11=0$



##### problem 36

We want to graph

$$
9x^2-24xy+16y^2-80x-60y+100=0
$$

relative to the $x'y'$ system.

To remove the $x'y'$ term, rotate the axes through an angle $\theta$ satisfying

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}.
$$

Here

$$
A=9,
\qquad
B=-24,
\qquad
C=16.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{9-16}{-24}\\
&=\frac{-7}{-24}\\
&=\frac{7}{24}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Using the $7$-$24$-$25$ triangle,

$$
\cos(2\theta)=\frac{7}{25}.
$$

Since $0^\circ<\theta<45^\circ$, the half-angle formulas give

$$
\cos\theta=\frac45,
\qquad
\sin\theta=\frac35.
$$

Thus

$$
x=x'\cos\theta-y'\sin\theta=\frac{4x'-3y'}{5}
$$

and

$$
y=x'\sin\theta+y'\cos\theta=\frac{3x'+4y'}{5}.
$$

Substituting into the equation and simplifying gives

$$
25y'^2-100x'+100=0.
$$

Therefore

$$
y'^2-4x'+4=0,
$$

so

$$
y'^2=4(x'-1).
$$

This is a parabola in the $x'y'$ system with vertex

$$
(1,0),
$$

focus

$$
(2,0),
$$

and directrix

$$
x'=0.
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240206-rotated-36.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  yp^2 == 4 (xp - 1),
  {xp, -1, 8}, {yp, -5, 5},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  AxesLabel -> {"x'", "y'"},
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{0, -5}, {0, 5}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{1, 0}}],
    Red, Point[{{2, 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Vertex (1,0)", {1, 0}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["Focus (2,0)", {2, 0}, {1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["Directrix x' = 0", {0.2, 4.4}, {-1.0, 0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

yp = np.linspace(-5, 5, 800)
xp = 1 + yp**2 / 4

vertex = (1, 0)
focus = (2, 0)

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(xp, yp, color="black", linewidth=2, label="y'^2 = 4(x' - 1)")
ax.axvline(0, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="x' = 0")

ax.scatter(*vertex, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*focus, color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(1.1, 0.25, "Vertex (1,0)")
ax.text(2.1, -0.35, "Focus (2,0)")
ax.text(0.1, 4.4, "Directrix x' = 0")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-1, 8)
ax.set_ylim(-5, 5)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend(loc="upper left")
plt.show()
```

##### problem 37

We want to graph

$$
x^2-xy+y^2-2=0
$$

relative to the $x'y'$ system.

Here

$$
A=1,
\qquad
B=-1,
\qquad
C=1.
$$

So

$$
\cot(2\theta)=\frac{A-C}{B}=\frac{1-1}{-1}=0.
$$

Since $A=C$, we take

$$
\theta=45^\circ.
$$

Thus

$$
\cos\theta=\sin\theta=\frac{1}{\sqrt2},
$$

and the rotation formulas become

$$
x=\frac{x'-y'}{\sqrt2},
\qquad
y=\frac{x'+y'}{\sqrt2}.
$$

Substituting into the equation gives

$$
\frac{x'^2+3y'^2}{2}-2=0.
$$

Hence

$$
x'^2+3y'^2=4,
$$

or

$$
\frac{x'^2}{4}+\frac{y'^2}{4/3}=1.
$$

This is an ellipse centered at

$$
(0,0)
$$

with vertices

$$
(\pm 2,0).
$$

Also,

$$
a^2=4,
\qquad
b^2=\frac43,
\qquad
c^2=a^2-b^2=4-\frac43=\frac83.
$$

So the foci are

$$
\left(\pm\sqrt{\frac83},0\right)=\left(\pm\frac{2\sqrt6}{3},0\right).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240206-rotated-37.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  xp^2/4 + yp^2/(4/3) == 1,
  {xp, -3, 3}, {yp, -11/5, 11/5},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  AxesLabel -> {"x'", "y'"},
  Epilog -> {
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{0, 0}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-2, 0}, {2, 0}}],
    Red, Point[{{-2 Sqrt[6]/3, 0}, {2 Sqrt[6]/3, 0}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (0,0)", {0, 0}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-2,0)", {-2, 0}, {-0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(2,0)", {2, 0}, {0.8, 1.0}],
    Text["(-2 Sqrt[6]/3,0)", {-2 Sqrt[6]/3, 0}, {-0.8, -1.0}],
    Text["(2 Sqrt[6]/3,0)", {2 Sqrt[6]/3, 0}, {0.8, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

xp = np.linspace(-2, 2, 800)
yp = np.sqrt((4 / 3) * (1 - xp**2 / 4))

center = (0, 0)
vertices = [(-2, 0), (2, 0)]
foci = [(-2 * np.sqrt(6) / 3, 0), (2 * np.sqrt(6) / 3, 0)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(xp, yp, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(xp, -yp, color="black", linewidth=2, label="x'^2/4 + y'^2/(4/3) = 1")

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(0.1, 0.15, "Center (0,0)")
ax.text(-2, 0, "(-2,0)", ha="right", va="bottom")
ax.text(2, 0, "(2,0)", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-2 * np.sqrt(6) / 3, 0, "(-2sqrt(6)/3,0)", ha="right", va="top")
ax.text(2 * np.sqrt(6) / 3, 0, "(2sqrt(6)/3,0)", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-3, 3)
ax.set_ylim(-2.2, 2.2)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend(loc="upper right")
plt.show()
```

##### problem 38

We want to graph

$$
6x^2+24xy-y^2-12x+26y+11=0
$$

relative to the $x'y'$ system.

Here

$$
A=6,
\qquad
B=24,
\qquad
C=-1.
$$

So

$$
\begin{aligned}
\cot(2\theta)
&=\frac{A-C}{B}\\
&=\frac{6-(-1)}{24}\\
&=\frac{7}{24}.
\end{aligned}
$$

Again, using the $7$-$24$-$25$ triangle and the half-angle formulas,

$$
\cos\theta=\frac45,
\qquad
\sin\theta=\frac35.
$$

Thus

$$
x=\frac{4x'-3y'}{5},
\qquad
y=\frac{3x'+4y'}{5}.
$$

Substituting into the equation and simplifying gives

$$
15x'^2+6x'-10y'^2+28y'+11=0.
$$

Now complete the square:

$$
\begin{aligned}
15x'^2+6x'-10y'^2+28y'+11&=0\\
15\left(x'^2+\frac{2}{5}x'\right)-10\left(y'^2-\frac{14}{5}y'\right)+11&=0\\
15\left[\left(x'+\frac{1}{5}\right)^2-\frac{1}{25}\right]-10\left[\left(y'-\frac{7}{5}\right)^2-\frac{49}{25}\right]+11&=0\\
15\left(x'+\frac{1}{5}\right)^2-\frac{3}{5}-10\left(y'-\frac{7}{5}\right)^2+\frac{98}{5}+11&=0\\
15\left(x'+\frac{1}{5}\right)^2-10\left(y'-\frac{7}{5}\right)^2+30&=0\\
10\left(y'-\frac{7}{5}\right)^2-15\left(x'+\frac{1}{5}\right)^2&=30.
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{(y'-7/5)^2}{3}-\frac{(x'+1/5)^2}{2}=1.
$$

This is a hyperbola with center

$$
\left(-\frac{1}{5},\frac{7}{5}\right).
$$

Its vertices are

$$
\left(-\frac{1}{5},\frac{7}{5}\pm\sqrt3\right),
$$

and since

$$
a^2=3,
\qquad
b^2=2,
\qquad
c^2=a^2+b^2=5,
$$

the foci are

$$
\left(-\frac{1}{5},\frac{7}{5}\pm\sqrt5\right).
$$

The asymptotes are

$$
y'-\frac{7}{5}=\pm\sqrt{\frac32}\left(x'+\frac{1}{5}\right).
$$


> [!figure]
> ![400](Pasted%20image%20202604240206-rotated-38.png)
> 
> 


**Mathematica code:**

```mathematica
ContourPlot[
  (yp - 7/5)^2/3 - (xp + 1/5)^2/2 == 1,
  {xp, -4, 4}, {yp, -3, 6},
  AspectRatio -> 1,
  Axes -> True,
  AxesLabel -> {"x'", "y'"},
  Epilog -> {
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-4, 7/5 - Sqrt[3/2] (19/5)}, {4, 7/5 + Sqrt[3/2] (21/5)}}]},
    {Gray, Dashed, Line[{{-4, 7/5 + Sqrt[3/2] (19/5)}, {4, 7/5 - Sqrt[3/2] (21/5)}}]},
    Green, PointSize[0.02], Point[{{-1/5, 7/5}}],
    Blue, Point[{{-1/5, 7/5 + Sqrt[3]}, {-1/5, 7/5 - Sqrt[3]}}],
    Red, Point[{{-1/5, 7/5 + Sqrt[5]}, {-1/5, 7/5 - Sqrt[5]}}],
    Black,
    Text["Center (-1/5,7/5)", {-1/5, 7/5}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1/5,7/5+Sqrt[3])", {-1/5, 7/5 + Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1/5,7/5-Sqrt[3])", {-1/5, 7/5 - Sqrt[3]}, {1.2, -1.0}],
    Text["(-1/5,7/5+Sqrt[5])", {-1/5, 7/5 + Sqrt[5]}, {1.2, 1.0}],
    Text["(-1/5,7/5-Sqrt[5])", {-1/5, 7/5 - Sqrt[5]}, {1.2, -1.0}]
  }
]
```


**Python code:**

```python
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

xp = np.linspace(-4, 4, 800)
yp_offset = np.sqrt(3 * (1 + (xp + 1 / 5) ** 2 / 2))

center = (-1 / 5, 7 / 5)
vertices = [(-1 / 5, 7 / 5 + np.sqrt(3)), (-1 / 5, 7 / 5 - np.sqrt(3))]
foci = [(-1 / 5, 7 / 5 + np.sqrt(5)), (-1 / 5, 7 / 5 - np.sqrt(5))]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7))
ax.plot(xp, 7 / 5 + yp_offset, color="black", linewidth=2)
ax.plot(xp, 7 / 5 - yp_offset, color="black", linewidth=2, label="(y' - 7/5)^2/3 - (x' + 1/5)^2/2 = 1")

xx = np.linspace(-4, 4, 400)
ax.plot(xx, 7 / 5 + np.sqrt(3 / 2) * (xx + 1 / 5), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)
ax.plot(xx, 7 / 5 - np.sqrt(3 / 2) * (xx + 1 / 5), "--", color="gray", linewidth=1)

ax.scatter(*center, color="green", s=55, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*vertices), color="blue", s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(*zip(*foci), color="red", s=60, zorder=3)

ax.text(-0.05, 1.55, "Center (-1/5,7/5)")
ax.text(-0.2, 7 / 5 + np.sqrt(3), "(-1/5,7/5+sqrt(3))", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-0.2, 7 / 5 - np.sqrt(3), "(-1/5,7/5-sqrt(3))", ha="left", va="top")
ax.text(-0.2, 7 / 5 + np.sqrt(5), "(-1/5,7/5+sqrt(5))", ha="left", va="bottom")
ax.text(-0.2, 7 / 5 - np.sqrt(5), "(-1/5,7/5-sqrt(5))", ha="left", va="top")

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-4, 4)
ax.set_ylim(-3, 6)
ax.set_aspect("equal", adjustable="box")
ax.legend(loc="lower right")
plt.show()
```
